Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
Clarifying the Meaning of Cause in the Infinite Regress Argument
Commentator clarifies definition of cause as independent agent to resolve self-causation objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator redefines 'cause' as the independent agent with all prerequisites, thereby resolving the objection that the series cannot be self-caused.

Core Idea
Cause means the independent agent with all dependencies, so the whole series is self-caused without metaphor.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a subtle objection to the infinite regress argument. The objection claims that saying the series is caused by itself is a mere metaphor because the first effect cannot be a cause of the individuals. The commentator clarifies that by 'cause' we mean the independent agent along with everything the effect depends on. With this definition, the series as a whole is genuinely self-caused. Let's follow his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the translation. Let the commentator's own rhythm come through.
Notice the dense style typical of commentaries.
2
Understand the objection
Identify why the objector thinks 'caused by itself' is metaphorical. The beginning of the series is not a cause of any individual, so the cause of the whole seems external.
3
Learn the commentator's definition of cause
The commentator states that cause here means 'al-mustaqill bi-l-ta'thir' – the independent agent together with all that the effect depends on. This includes everything necessary for the effect to exist.
This definition is key to resolving the objection.
4
See how self-causation becomes literal
Since the whole series includes all dependencies, the agent (first cause) plus all prerequisites is the same as the series itself. Thus, the series is self-caused without metaphor.
5
Note the refutation of the objector's confusion
The objector confuses individuals with wholes. The commentator shows that the objector's earlier statements refer to individuals, but later ones shift to wholes, causing inconsistency.
This helps resolve the doubt entirely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فتكون تلك الأشياء إلخ ) أي مجموعها معللة بنفسها قيل : لا خة في أن المعلول الذي هو مبدأ السلسلة ليس علة لشيء من الآحاد فعلة المجموع ما قبله والتعبير عنه بنفسها مسامحة بمعنى أنها ليست خارجة عنها كما صرح به والمراد بالأشياء الجمل فهذا الاعتراض بعينه الاعتراض المشار إليه بقوله وبهذا تبين فساد ما قيل إلخ، وحينئذ لا يتجه الجواب، فإنه جواب عن كون الاعتراض باختيار كون علة الشيء نفسها حقيقة كما لا يخفى ويكون الترديد الآتي بقوله وحينئذ نقول تلك العلل الموجدة إلخ قبيحاً لعدم احتمال العينية أقول قد عرفت أن جميع المراد بالعلة هاهنا المستقلة بالتأثير أي الفاعل مع جميع ما يتوقف عليه التأثير فالمعلول المذكور وإن لم يكن فاعلاً معتبر فيما يتوقف عليه وجود المجموع لكونه جزءاً منه فعلى هذا يصح كونه علة السلسلة نفسها من غير تجوز لكون الفاعل مع جميع ما يتوقف عليه نفسها فاندفع الشبهة بالكلية، ولعمري مفاسد قلة التأمل أكثر من أن تحصى . قوله : ( على معنى أنها كافية إلخ لا بمعنى أن هذا المجموع الواحد المعين علة لنفسه، حتى يلزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه. قوله : ( لا يدخل فيها غيرها إلخ) أي في تلك السلسلة غير الآحاد . قوله : ( وحينئذ نقول جميع تلك العلل إلخ فيه بحث؛ لأن المعترض صرح مراراً أن مراده بالنفس ما هو غير خارج فيظهر من تكريره التفسير أن مراده بالنفس ليس حقيقتها، بل ما هو الداخل فيها ومراده بكل واحد من الأشياء في قوله أعني أن يعلل كل واحد من الأشياء المجموعات الواقعة في السلسلة من تمامها ومما نقص منه بواحد أو باثنين أو بثلاثة إلى غير ذلك يدل على هذا أنه جعل المعلل الجملة المعتبرة بدون الهيئة وعلتها علل الأفراد وكذا المراد بما قبله فإنه أيضاً المجموعات بخلاف قوله أولاً والثاني علة للأول والثالث للثاني فإن مراده بالأول والثاني والثالث وغيرها الآحاد لا المجموعات فهذا الاعتراض في التحقيق هو الاعتراض الذي نقله الشارح في آخر البحث بقوله وبهذا تبين بطلان ما قد قيل إلخ. وحينئذ يندفع عنه
His statement: (Thus those things etc.) i.e., their whole is caused by itself. It was said: There is no doubt that the effect which is the beginning of the series is not a cause for any of the individuals, so the cause of the whole is what precedes it, and the expression 'by itself' is a metaphor meaning that it is not external to it, as he explicitly stated. The intended meaning of 'things' is the whole. So this objection is precisely the objection referred to by his statement: 'And thereby the corruption of what was said etc.' Then the answer is not directed [properly], for it is an answer concerning the objection by choosing that the cause of a thing is itself in reality, as is clear. And the following repetition in his statement 'then we say these effective causes etc.' is ugly due to the impossibility of identity. I say: You have known that all that is meant by cause here is the independent agent (al-mustaqill bi-l-ta'thir), i.e., the agent with everything on which the effect depends. So the mentioned effect, even if it is not an agent, is considered among what the existence of the whole depends on because it is a part of it. On this basis, it is correct for it to be the cause of the series itself without metaphor, because the agent [together] with everything on which it depends is [the same as] itself. Thus the doubt is entirely removed. By my life, the corruptions of lack of reflection are more than can be counted. His statement: (In the sense that it is sufficient etc.) not meaning that this particular single whole is a cause of itself, such that a thing would precede itself. His statement: (Nothing else enters into it etc.) i.e., into that series other than the individuals. His statement: (Then we say all those causes etc.) contains a discussion; because the objector repeatedly stated that his intention by 'self' is what is not external. So it appears from his repeated explanation that his intention by 'self' is not its reality, but rather what is internal to it. And his intention by 'each one of the things' in his statement 'I mean that each one of the things is caused' [refers to] the wholes occurring in the series – whether the complete [series] or what is deficient by one, two, three, etc. – this indicates that he made the thing caused [to be] the whole considered without the relational aspect, and its cause is the causes of the individuals. Similarly, the intended meaning of 'what precedes it' is also the wholes, unlike his earlier statement 'the second is cause of the first, the third of the second' where his intention by 'first', 'second', 'third', etc. is the individuals, not the wholes. So this objection in reality is the objection that the commentator transmitted at the end of the research with his statement 'and thereby the corruption of what was said etc.' And then it is repelled.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary clarifying the meaning of 'cause' ('illa) in the context of the infinite regress argument. The commentator addresses a potential misunderstanding: the effect at the beginning of the series is not a cause of any individual, so the expression 'caused by itself' might seem metaphorical. He explains that by 'cause' is meant the independent effective agent (al-mustaqill bi-l-ta'thir) together with all necessary prerequisites for the effect. Since the whole series includes everything that the existence of the series depends upon, it is correct to say it is self-caused without metaphor. He then refutes an objection about a single particular whole being self-caused, clarifying that the intended meaning is different. The objector's repeated use of 'self' (nafs) refers to what is internal, not the literal essence. The commentator shows that the objector actually conflates individuals with wholes, leading to confusion. The real objection is the one already refuted at the end of the previous discussion, and it is therefore invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The series cannot be self-caused because the first effect is not a cause of the individuals, so 'self-causation' is only a metaphor.
Correction: The cause here means the independent agent with all dependencies. Since the series includes all that it depends on, it is genuinely self-caused without metaphor.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the correct meaning of 'cause' (علة) in this context?
A) Any factor that precedes an effect in time.
B) The independent effective agent together with everything the effect depends upon.
C) The first item in the chain.
D) A metaphorical relation without real efficacy.

Key Takeaway
By defining cause as the independent agent with all its dependencies, the commentator shows that the infinite series is genuinely self-caused, resolving the objection without metaphor.

What is one specific area of aqeedah you will re-examine this week with greater care?
?
The objector misunderstood 'self-causation' because he assumed 'cause' meant something distinct from the effect. Is there a belief you hold about divine decree or human free will that might be built on a misdefinition of a key term? ?

Think of a theological difficulty you have struggled with—could it be rooted in a misunderstanding of a term? Commit today to looking up the precise technical meaning of that term from reliable sources and see if the difficulty dissolves.

Causality and Created Things
beginner

Objection and Answer on Self-Causing Chain
The author presents an objection about self-causation and answers it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that an infinite chain of causes would be self-causing is invalid, and how the answer clarifies that the chain's cause is the aggregate of its parts.

Core Idea
The chain's cause is the sum of its parts, not a single self-causing member.

This passage tackles a common objection to the impossibility of infinite regress. If every possible depends on a cause, and the chain has no external cause, doesn't the chain cause itself? Sanusi clarifies that what is impossible is a single thing causing itself; the chain as a whole being caused by its own parts is not problematic. Let's examine the objection and its precise answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is stated: if every possible depends on an external cause, then the chain would be self-causing. Notice the phrase 'self-causing' and how it is linked to the aggregate.
The objection seems plausible at first glance.
2
Identify the two possible meanings
The answer distinguishes between two interpretations: a single specific thing causing itself (impossible) vs. the aggregate of the chain being caused by its own parts (possible). Focus on where the passage says 'What is intended is the second meaning'.
This distinction is crucial.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
Follow the reasoning: each individual in the chain has a cause within the chain, so the cause of all individuals together is simply the collection of those causes. This shows that the chain is not self-causing in the problematic sense.
The passage ends by stating that all efficient causes of the individuals are the efficient cause of all individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثامن : التسلسل محال أن يستند الممكن إلى علة الأشياء إلى علة أخرى خارجة عنها، فتكون تلك الأشياء معللة بنفسها، على معنى أنها كافية لوجودها بما فيها، إنما الممتنع تعليل شيء واحد معين بنفسه، والجواب : إن المراد هو المعنى الثاني كما أشار إليه بقوله : أي بحيث لا يدخل فيها غيرها، فيكون المجموع حينئذ عين الآحاد ، ولا شك أن هذه الآحاد ممكنات موجودة، كما أن كل واحد منها موجود ممكن. وكما أن الموجود الممكن محتاج إلى علة موجدة كافية في إيجاده، كذلك الممكنات المتعددة الموجودة محتاجة إلى علة موجدة كافية في إيجادها بالضرورة. وحيث كان لكل واحد من تلك السلسلة علة موجدة داخلة في السلسلة كانت العلة الموجدة لجميع الآحاد تلك العلل الموجدة للآحاد . وحينئذ نقول : جميع تلك العلل الموجدة للآحاد التي هي علة موجدة لجميع الآحاد،
The Fifth Observation - The Eighth Object: Infinite regress is impossible. That the possible be attributed to a cause of things to another cause external to them, then those things would be caused by themselves, meaning that they are sufficient for their existence by what is in them. What is impossible is causing a single specific thing by itself. The answer: What is intended is the second meaning, as he indicated by his saying: such that nothing other than them enters into it, so the aggregate is then identical to the individuals. And there is no doubt that these individuals are possible existents, just as each one of them is an existing possible. And just as the existing possible needs a sufficient efficient cause for its existence, likewise multiple existing possibles necessarily need a sufficient efficient cause for their existence. And since each one of that chain has an efficient cause inside the chain, the efficient cause of all the individuals is those efficient causes of the individuals. Then we say: All those efficient causes of the individuals are the efficient cause of all the individuals.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the claim that infinite regress is impossible. The objection states that if every possible thing depends on an external cause, then the entire chain of possibles would be self-causing, since each part causes another. The answer clarifies that what is meant is not that a single specific thing causes itself, but that the aggregate of the chain is identical to its parts. Each individual is a possible needing a cause, and because each has a cause within the chain, the aggregate's cause is precisely the collection of those individual causes. The argument concludes that the entire set of causes is the cause of the whole aggregate, without circularity.

Key Terms
تسلسل
ممكن
علة
معلول
مجموع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the chain's cause is internal, then a single thing causes itself.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the cause is the aggregate of parts, not any single part.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the objection about self-causation fail?
A) Because a single thing can cause itself.
B) Because the intended meaning is that the aggregate is caused by its parts, not that a single thing is self-caused.
C) Because the chain is not really a series of possibles.
D) Because the cause must be external to the chain.

Key Takeaway
The answer distinguishes between a single self-causing thing and an aggregate caused by its own parts; the latter is not impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the ultimate cause of your very existence – your heartbeat, your breath, your consciousness – do you ever catch yourself feeling self-sufficient, as if you are the cause of your own being? The passage teaches that even a chain of causes cannot be self-causing in the forbidden sense. Today, identify one area in your life where you silently claim independence from Allah, and consciously repeat in your heart: 'I am utterly dependent on You for this.' What is that area, and when will you make this reminder?
?
The objection in the passage assumes that if causes are internal, the whole chain is self-causing. The answer clarifies that only a single self-causing thing is impossible. Think of a recurring sin or struggle you face. Do you treat it as a 'single thing' you can overcome by your own willpower? Or do you see it as part of a chain that requires Allah's grace and the support of others? Commit to one specific action today that invites Allah's help into that struggle – whether it is a du'a, seeking advice, or a small act of obedience.
?
The passage emphasizes that the aggregate's cause is the collection of its parts, not any single part. Reflect on your role in your family, community, or workplace. Do you try to be the sole cause of success or happiness, or do you see yourself as part of an aggregate where each person contributes? In what way can you humbly acknowledge that you are not the sufficient cause, but that Allah works through many means? Write down one practical step to shift from self-reliance to collective reliance on Allah's provision.

Classification of the Cause and the Confusion Between Individual and Aggregate
The author classifies the possible cause of the chain and distinguishes explaining individuals from explaining the whole.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of possible causes for an infinite regress and the critical distinction between explaining each individual in the chain and explaining the aggregate as a whole.

Core Idea
Confusing individual causation with aggregate causation leads to fallacies in arguments about infinite regress.

This passage tackles a subtle but crucial point in discussions of infinite regress: what could possibly be the cause of an endless chain? The author classifies three possibilities and then warns against conflating two different kinds of explanation—one concerning each link individually and another concerning the entire chain as a single entity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the three-fold classification
The passage begins by stating that the cause of the chain must be either its very essence, a part of it, or external to it. Find these three options.
3
Understand why the first is impossible
Note the reasoning: the cause must precede its effect, but the aggregate cannot precede itself. This eliminates the first possibility.
4
Distinguish the two types of explanation
The passage then clarifies that the confusion arises from mixing up explaining each individual by another in the chain (the disputed case) and explaining the whole aggregate by its parts (clearly invalid). Recognize this distinction.
5
Examine examples and further refinement
Read the examples about the loaf and the stone, and the analysis of the aggregate as a mental construct. This solidifies the difference between 'each one' and 'all'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إما أن تكون عين السلسلة أو داخلة فيها أو خارجة عنها والأول محال ؛ لأن العلة الموجدة لشيء سواء كان ذلك الشيء واحداً معيناً أو مركباً من آحاد متناهية، أو غير متناهية، يجب أن يتقدم بالوجود على ذلك الشيء، ومن المستحيل تقدم المجموع على نفسه بالوجود والاشتباه إنما وقع بين تعليل كل واحد من السلسلة بآخر منها، وبين تعليل مجموعها بمجموعها وهما أمران متغايران والأول هو المتنازع فيه الذي نحن بصدد إبطاله بطريق الاستدلال والثاني مما ينبه على بطلانه، فإنه باطل
قوله : ( والاشتباه ) أي للسائل حيث قال : فلكل واحد من آحاد السلسلة علة، ولما لم يكن المجموع على هذا الوجه غير الأفراد لم يحتج إلى علة خارجة . قوله : (وهما أمران متغايران) أي التعليلان متغايران؛ لكون كل واحد والكل متغايرين في المفهوم والأحكام الخارجية، أما الأول فلأن معنى كل واحد واحد أي واحد كان غير أن من يكون معه آخر، ومعنى الثاني واحد مع آخر وأما الثاني فلصدق قولنا كل واحد يشبعه هذا الرغيف دون كلهم وكلهم يحمل هذا الحجر دون كل واحد وقيل في إثبات التغاير أنه إذا تحقق (۱) و (ب) تحقق ثالث هو مجموع (ا) و (ب) لا المجموع المركب منهما ومن الهيئة الاجتماعية العارضة لهما ، إذ لا تتحقق تلك الهيئة في الخارج، بل المراد معروض تلك الهيئة الاعتبارية، وذلك لأنا نعلم ضرورة أنه تحقق هاهنا ما كان موصوفاً بالكثرة والاثنينية، ومعروض الهيئة، وهو غير كل واحد ؛ لأن كل واحد معروض بوصف ..الوحدة وأيضاً كل واحد جزء وذلك في جواب الشارح قطعاً؛ إذ قد علم أن مختاره في الحقيقة هو الشق الثاني أعني كون علة السلسلة جزءاً منه والشارح يتكلم على اختيار الشق الأول فهو إيراد على ظاهر عبارته على أن في تقريره ترديداً قبيحاً لأنه لما حكم أولاً بأن علة مجموع السلسلة علل الآحاد التي كل واحد منها داخل السلسلة تعين عدم الخروج، فالترديد الذي ذكره مثل أن يقال : هذه الجملة من أجزاء الشيء إما غير خارجة عنه، أو خارجة عنه ولا خفاء في قبحه. وقد يناقش أيضاً بأن هذا الذي ذكره مبني على توهم أن السلسلة موجود آخر ممكن محتاج إلى علة أخرى هي جميع تلك العلل وليس كذلك . بل ليس هناك إلا ممكنات قد احتاج كل منها إلى علة، وما يقال: إن وجودات الآحاد غير وجود كل واحد منها كلام خال عن التحصيل . وفيه بحث ظاهر
Either it is the eye of the chain, or inside it, or external to it. The first is impossible because the efficient cause of a thing, whether that thing is a single specific thing or composed of finite or infinite individuals, must precede that thing in existence, and it is impossible for the aggregate to precede itself in existence. The confusion only occurred between explaining each one of the chain by another from it, and explaining its aggregate by its aggregate. They are two different matters. The first is the disputed one which we are in the process of invalidating by proof, while the second is that which warns of its invalidity, for it is invalid. His saying: (And the confusion) meaning from the questioner where he said: For each one of the individuals of the chain there is a cause, and since the aggregate in this manner is not other than the individuals, it does not need an external cause. His saying: (They are two different matters) meaning the two explanations are different; because the individual and the whole are different in concept and external judgments. As for the first, the meaning of 'each one' is one, i.e., any one, but with another present, and the meaning of the second is one with another. As for the second, it is by the truth of our statement: 'Each one fills this loaf' is not true of all of them, and 'All of them carry this stone' is not true of each one. And it was said in establishing the difference that if (a) and (b) exist, a third exists which is the sum of (a) and (b), not the composite of them and the social form (al-hay'a al-ijtimā'iyya) accidental to them, since that form does not exist externally, but what is intended is the bearer of that mental form. And that is because we know necessarily that there exists here something described by multiplicity and twoness, and the bearer of the form, and it is other than each one; because each one is a bearer described by unity. Also, each one is a part, and that is in the answer of the commentator definitively; for it is known that his chosen view in reality is the second side, namely that the cause of the chain is a part of it, while the commentator speaks on the choice of the first side, so it is an objection to the apparent meaning of his statement, and indeed in his exposition he has an ugly hesitation. Because when he first ruled that the cause of the whole chain is the causes of the individuals, each of which is inside the chain, then externality is precluded. So the hesitation he mentioned is like saying: this sentence from the parts of a thing is either not external to it, or external to it. There is no hiddenness in its ugliness. And it may also be objected that what he mentioned is based on the illusion that the chain is another possible existent needing another cause which is all those causes, and it is not so. Rather, there is nothing but possibles each of which needs a cause. And what is said: that the existences of the individuals are other than the existence of each one of them is a statement devoid of verification. And in it there is apparent investigation.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a classification and clarification of the cause of an infinite regress. The author states that the cause of the chain must be either identical to the chain, a part of it, or external. The first is impossible because any cause must precede its effect, and the aggregate cannot precede itself. He then distinguishes two types of causation: explaining each individual by another in the chain, and explaining the whole aggregate by itself. The confusion arises from conflating these. The first type is the one under dispute, while the second is clearly invalid. The passage then provides a detailed commentary on the distinction, including examples of how 'each one' differs from 'all' in terms of satisfaction and carrying capacity. It also discusses the nature of the aggregate as a mental construct and the difference between a mere sum and a composite with a form. The commentator defends the view that the cause of the chain is a part of it, and criticizes the hesitation in the opposing view. He also refutes the notion that the chain is a separate possible needing its own cause beyond the individual causes.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the aggregate's cause is internal, then the aggregate causes itself, or that explaining each individual is equivalent to explaining the whole.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the aggregate cannot be its own cause because the cause must precede the effect. Moreover, explaining each individual separately differs from explaining the whole, as shown by examples and the analysis of the aggregate as a mental construct.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it impossible for the chain to be its own cause?
A) Because the chain is composed of individuals.
B) Because the cause must precede its effect, and the aggregate cannot precede itself.
C) Because the cause must be external to the chain.
D) Because the chain is not a real entity.

Key Takeaway
The cause of an infinite chain cannot be identical to it, and explaining each individual is different from explaining the whole aggregate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own sins, do you justify each one by its own excuse while ignoring the overall pattern of your life? How can you apply the lesson's distinction between individual and aggregate explanations to take responsibility for your entire spiritual state?
?
This passage shows that explaining every part does not explain the whole. In your relationship with Allah, have you been satisfied with performing isolated good deeds while neglecting the overall orientation of your heart? What would it mean for you to seek a holistic transformation rather than piecemeal improvements?
?
The author warns against confusing two different types of causation. In your daily choices, where might you be conflating short-term individual decisions with the long-term sum of your character? Commit to one area where you will stop treating parts as sufficient and instead address the whole.

Contradiction in the Chain of Ten Intellects
Objection against philosophers' chain of ten intellects, showing contradiction with tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical contradiction in the philosophers' theory of a finite chain of intellects regarding the cause of the aggregate and the principle that only one effect proceeds from a single cause.

Core Idea
The philosophers' chain of ten intellects leads to either multiple necessary beings or an infinite regress, contradicting their own principles.

Sanūsī now presents a powerful objection against the philosophers' causal chain. He shows that if the chain is finite and each individual requires a cause, the cause of the entire aggregate cannot be itself or internal, nor can it be external without leading to contradictions. This passage exposes a fundamental inconsistency in their system.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and the English translation. Pay attention to the structure of the objection: the condition, the two possibilities, and the concluding contradiction.
The passage is dense; reading it twice may help.
2
Identify the objection's premise
Find the statement: if the individuals of the chain are distinct from the aggregate and each needs a cause, then the cause of the aggregate cannot be itself or inside it.
This sets up the dilemma.
3
Trace the two possibilities
The passage says the cause must be external, and then it must be either necessary (wājib) or possible (mumkin). Follow each branch and its consequence.
One branch leads to multiple necessary beings, the other to a succession of causes.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author states that the two positions (chain ends with Necessary Being and denial of two effects from one agent) are contradictory. Grasp why this is a problem.
This is the heart of the objection.
5
Reflect on the author's hint
The author mentions that an answer was given earlier. Consider how that might resolve the objection.
Contemplation is encouraged.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وها هنا مناقشة على أن الحكماء لا بد أن ينبه عليها وهي آحاد السلسلة إذا كان مغايراً لكل واحد من مجموع ومحتاجاً إلى علة غير علة كل واحد من الآحاد ، ورد عليهم الاعتراض في السلسلة المتناهية كسلسلة العقول العشرة مثلاً، فإن علة مجموع هذه السلسلة لا يجوز أن تكون نفسها، ولا داخلة فيها، وهو ظاهر، ولا يجوز أن تكون خارجة عنها وإلا لكانت واجبة أو ممكنة، فإن كانت واجبة لزم تعدد الواجب لأنهم لا يجوزون صدور أثرين عنه تعالى وقد أسندوا إليه العقل الأول، فعلة المجموع لا بد أن يكون واجباً آخر، وإن كانت ممكنة لزم توارد العلل والحاصل أن القول بانتهاء سلسلة العلل إلى الواجب ومنع جواز صدور أثرين عن مؤثر واحد متناقضان . وكأنا أشرنا في أوائل المقصد الثالث إلى ما يمكن أن يدفع به هذا الاعتراض فليتأمل فيه
And here is a discussion that the philosophers must be alerted to: the individuals of the chain, if it is different from each one of the aggregate and need a cause other than the cause of each individual, then the objection is raised against them in the finite chain, such as the chain of the ten intellects. For the cause of the aggregate of this chain cannot be itself, nor inside it, and that is obvious, nor can it be external to it, otherwise it would be either necessary or possible. If it is necessary, then there would be multiple Necessary Beings, because they do not allow two effects to proceed from Him, the Exalted, and they have attributed the First Intellect to Him. So the cause of the aggregate would have to be another necessary being. And if it is possible, then there would be a succession of causes. The conclusion is that the statement that the chain of causes ends with the Necessary Being and the denial of the permissibility of two effects from one agent are contradictory. And we have pointed out in the beginning of the Third Object what may repel this objection, so let it be contemplated.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage presents another objection against the philosophers (al-hukamā') concerning their theory of a finite chain of ten intellects. If the aggregate of the chain is distinct from each individual and needs a separate cause, then for a finite chain like the ten intellects, the cause of the whole cannot be itself or inside it, nor can it be external—because if external, it must be either necessary or possible. If necessary, this implies multiple necessary beings, contradicting their principle that only one effect proceeds from God (since they attribute the First Intellect to Him). If possible, it leads to an infinite regress of causes. Thus, their claims that the chain ends with the Necessary Being and that only one effect proceeds from a single agent are contradictory. The author hints that this objection is addressed earlier in the text.

Key Terms
مناقشة
الحكماء
سلسلة العقول العشرة
واجب
ممكن
تعدد الواجب
صدور أثرين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection against the philosophers is only about infinite regress, not about tawhid.
Correction: The passage shows that if the external cause is necessary, it leads to multiple necessary beings, directly contradicting tawhid (the oneness of the Necessary Being). The contradiction is between their claim of one Necessary Being and their denial of multiple effects from one cause.

Checkpoint
What contradiction does the author claim in the philosophers' position?
A) They say the chain is infinite but also finite.
B) They claim the chain ends with the Necessary Being but deny that one cause can produce two effects.
C) They believe in multiple necessary beings.
D) They deny that the chain needs a cause.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' finite chain of ten intellects leads to a contradiction: if the cause of the aggregate is external and necessary, it implies multiple necessary beings; if possible, it implies infinite regress, both opposing tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about Allah's oneness that, if examined logically, implies multiple necessary beings? How can you use this objection to safeguard your own theology against contradictions?
?
The philosophers' chain seems neat, but the objection exposes a hidden inconsistency. What hidden assumptions in your own understanding of causality and divine action might be inconsistent with true tawhid? Are you ready to search for them?
?
If the finite chain of ten intellects leads to a contradiction, what does that imply for any human attempt to explain creation through intermediaries? Will you trust the Quran's simple teaching that Allah creates directly?

The Distinction Between the Whole and Its Parts in Causation
The commentator clarifies the confusion and proves the real distinction between the whole and its parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the whole is not reducible to its parts in causation and how the distinction is proven.

Core Idea
The whole and its parts are distinct in concept and effect, so the whole needs its own cause.

When we say that each part has a cause, does the whole automatically have a cause? The questioner thinks so, but the commentator shows that the whole is different from the sum of its parts. This lesson explains the confusion and proves the real distinction using conceptual and empirical examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the confusion
Read the first line where the questioner argues that since each individual has a cause, the series as a whole needs no external cause. Identify the mistake.
The questioner assumes the whole is identical to the parts.
2
Understand the distinction
The commentator says the two types of causation are distinct. Notice how 'each single' differs from 'all together' in meaning and in real-world judgments.
Example: a loaf suffices one but not all.
3
Follow the proof of distinction
Read the proof that if A and B exist, a third entity (the sum) exists. Understand that this sum is not the composite with its relational form, but the substrate of multiplicity.
This shows the whole is other than each part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (والاشتباه) أي للسائل حيث قال: فلكل واحد من آحاد السلسلة علة، ولما لم يكن المجموع على هذا الوجه غير الأفراد لم يحتج إلى علة خارجة. قوله: (وهما أمران متغايران) أي التعليلان متغايران; لكون كل واحد والكل متغايرين في المفهوم والأحكام الخارجية، أما الأول فلأن معنى كل واحد واحد أي واحد كان غير أن من يكون معه آخر، ومعنى الثاني واحد مع آخر وأما الثاني فلصدق قولنا كل واحد يشبعه هذا الرغيف دون كلهم وكلهم يحمل هذا الحجر دون كل واحد وقيل في إثبات التغاير أنه إذا تحقق (ا) و (ب) تحقق ثالث هو مجموع (ا) و (ب) لا المجموع المركب منهما ومن الهيئة الاجتماعية العارضة لهما، إذ لا تتحقق تلك الهيئة في الخارج، بل المراد معروض تلك الهيئة الاعتبارية، وذلك لأنا نعلم ضرورة أنه تحقق هاهنا ما كان موصوفاً بالكثرة والاثنينية، ومعروض الهيئة، وهو غير كل واحد; لأن كل واحد معروض بوصف الوحدة وأيضاً كل واحد جزء وذلك في جواب الشارح قطعاً; إذ قد علم أن مختاره في الحقيقة هو الشق الثاني أعني كون علة السلسلة جزءاً منه والشارح يتكلم على اختيار الشق الأول فهو إيراد على ظاهر عبارته على أن في تقريره ترديداً قبيحاً لأنه لما حكم أولاً بأن علة مجموع السلسلة علل الآحاد التي كل واحد منها داخل السلسلة تعين عدم الخروج، فالترديد الذي ذكره مثل أن يقال: هذه الجملة من أجزاء الشيء إما غير خارجة عنه، أو خارجة عنه ولا خفاء في قبحه. وقد يناقش أيضاً بأن هذا الذي ذكره مبني على توهم أن السلسلة موجود آخر ممكن محتاج إلى علة أخرى هي جميع تلك العلل وليس كذلك. بل ليس هناك إلا ممكنات قد احتاج كل منها إلى علة، وما يقال: إن وجودات الآحاد غير وجود كل واحد منها كلام خال عن التحصيل. وفيه بحث ظاهر، وها هنا مناقشة على أن الحكماء لا بد أن ينبه عليها وهي آحاد السلسلة إذا كان مغايراً لكل واحد من مجموع ومحتاجاً إلى علة غير علة كل واحد من الآحاد، ورد عليهم الاعتراض في السلسلة المتناهية كسلسلة العقول العشرة مثلاً، فإن علة مجموع هذه السلسلة لا يجوز أن تكون نفسها، ولا داخلة فيها، وهو ظاهر، ولا يجوز أن تكون خارجة عنها وإلا لكانت واجبة أو ممكنة، فإن كانت واجبة لزم تعدد الواجب لأنهم لا يجوزون صدور أثرين عنه تعالى وقد أسندوا إليه العقل الأول، فعلة المجموع لا بد أن يكون واجباً آخر، وإن كانت ممكنة لزم توارد العلل والحاصل أن القول بانتهاء سلسلة العلل إلى الواجب ومنع جواز صدور أثرين عن مؤثر واحد متناقضان. وكأنا أشرنا في أوائل المقصد الثالث إلى ما يمكن أن يدفع به هذا الاعتراض فليتأمل فيه
His statement: 'and the confusion' means according to the questioner when he said: 'For every single one of the individuals of the series there is a cause, and since the aggregate in this respect is not other than the individuals, it does not need an external cause.' His statement: 'and they are two distinct matters' means that the two causations are distinct, because each single and the whole are distinct in concept and external judgments. As for the first, because the meaning of 'each single' is one, i.e., any one, except that one with whom there is another; and the meaning of the second is 'one with another'. As for the second, because our statement 'each single is filled by this loaf' is true without 'all of them', and 'all of them carry this stone' is true without 'each single'. And it was said in proving the distinction that if (A) and (B) exist, a third exists which is the sum of (A) and (B), not the composite sum consisting of them and the relational form accidental to them, since that form does not exist externally, but rather what is intended is the substrate of that conceptual form. This is because we know necessarily that what was qualified by multiplicity and duality has come into existence here, and the substrate of the form, and it is other than each single, because each single is qualified by the attribute of unity. Also, each single is a part. And this [argument] is definitively in the answer of the commentator, since it is known that his chosen position in reality is the second side, namely that the cause of the series is a part of it, while the commentator speaks on the adoption of the first side. So it is an objection to the apparent meaning of his statement, and in his formulation there is an ugly hesitation because when he first judged that the cause of the whole series is the causes of the individuals each of which is inside the series, non-externality is determined. So the hesitation he mentioned is like saying: 'This totality of parts of the thing is either not external to it, or external to it'—and its ugliness is not hidden. And it may also be objected that what he mentioned is based on the assumption that the series is another contingent existent needing another cause which is all those causes, but it is not so. Rather, there are only contingents, each of which needs a cause, and what is said: 'the existences of the individuals are other than the existence of each one of them' is talk devoid of achievement. And there is an apparent research in it. And here there is an objection which the philosophers must be alerted to: that the individuals of the series, if the aggregate is other than each single and needs a cause other than the cause of each single, then an objection is brought against them in the finite series, like the series of ten intellects for example. For the cause of the aggregate of this series cannot be itself, nor inside it—this is obvious—nor can it be outside it, for then it would be necessary or contingent. If necessary, then multiplicity of the Necessary would follow, because they do not allow the emanation of two effects from Him—exalted is He—and they have attributed the First Intellect to Him. So the cause of the aggregate must be another necessary being. And if contingent, then a succession of causes would follow. The conclusion is that the statement that the chain of causes ends at the Necessary and the denial of the permissibility of two effects from a single agent are contradictory. And we have indicated in the beginning of the third section what may repel this objection, so let it be considered.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on al-Iji's text, explaining the distinction between the two types of causation. The commentator first clarifies that the confusion ('ishtibah') arises from the questioner's claim that since each individual has a cause within the series, the whole series, being nothing but the individuals, needs no external cause. The commentator then explains the statement 'they are two distinct matters' by arguing that 'each single' and 'the whole' are different in concept and in external judgments. He gives examples: a loaf suffices for each single but not for all together, and a stone is carried by all together but not by each single. He then presents a proof of distinction based on the existence of a third entity that is the sum of A and B, distinct from the composite with its relational form. This leads to a discussion of the commentator's own position and an objection against the philosophers regarding finite series, showing that their view leads to a contradiction between the ending of the chain at the Necessary and the denial of multiple effects from one agent. The passage ends by referring to an earlier section for a possible resolution.

Key Terms
الاشتباه
التغاير
المجموع
الهيئة الاجتماعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The whole is nothing but the sum of its parts, so if each part has a cause, the whole automatically has a cause.
Correction: The whole is conceptually and really distinct from its parts; it has its own properties and requires its own cause.

Checkpoint
Which example does the commentator use to show the distinction between the whole and each single?
A) A loaf suffices each single but not all together.
B) A stone is carried by each single but not by all.
C) Both A and B.
D) Neither.

Key Takeaway
The whole and its parts are distinct in meaning and effect, so the whole needs its own cause, separate from the causes of the parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own life, do you treat each action (prayer, charity, work) as isolated units with their own causes, forgetting that your entire self and your relationship with Allah is a whole that requires its own distinct cause and intention? How can you consciously seek a holistic purpose for your whole life, not just its parts?
?
The passage shows that the whole is not reducible to its parts. In your spiritual journey, do you ever reduce your faith to a checklist of deeds, missing the emergent reality of your connection with Allah? What one change can you make today to stop treating your deen as just a sum of parts and instead seek its unifying cause?

The Objection from Finite Series to the Philosophers' View
The commentator raises an objection against the philosophers regarding finite series and shows a contradiction in their doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the philosophers' position on the chain of causes leads to a contradiction when applied to a finite series like the ten intellects.

Core Idea
If the whole needs a cause outside the series, then in a finite series that cause leads to either multiple necessary beings or an infinite regress.

The commentator now addresses the philosophers' view that the cause of the series must be part of it. He shows that if the cause were external, then for a finite series (e.g., the ten intellects), the external cause would have to be either necessary or contingent, both of which create problems. This lesson explores that contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The commentator discusses the position that the cause of the whole series is internal rather than external. Read the critique of that position.
The commentator points out an 'ugly hesitation' in the formulation.
2
Consider the finite series objection
Focus on the objection regarding a finite series like the ten intellects. The cause of the whole cannot be inside the series, nor can it be external without leading to problems.
If external, it would be either necessary (leading to multiple necessary beings) or contingent (leading to a succession of causes).
3
Identify the contradiction
The philosophers' claim that the chain ends at the Necessary Being conflicts with their denial that a single agent can produce multiple effects, causing inconsistency.
The commentator hints at a resolution from an earlier section.

And it was said in proving the distinction that if (A) and (B) exist, a third exists which is the sum of (A) and (B), not the composite sum consisting of them and the relational form accidental to them, since that form does not exist externally, but rather what is intended is the substrate of that conceptual form. This is because we know necessarily that what was qualified by multiplicity and duality has come into existence here, and the substrate of the form, and it is other than each single, because each single is qualified by the attribute of unity. Also, each single is a part. And this [argument] is definitively in the answer of the commentator, since it is known that his chosen position in reality is the second side, namely that the cause of the series is a part of it, while the commentator speaks on the adoption of the first side. So it is an objection to the apparent meaning of his statement, and in his formulation there is an ugly hesitation because when he first judged that the cause of the whole series is the causes of the individuals each of which is inside the series, non-externality is determined. So the hesitation he mentioned is like saying: 'This totality of parts of the thing is either not external to it, or external to it'—and its ugliness is not hidden. And it may also be objected that what he mentioned is based on the assumption that the series is another contingent existent needing another cause which is all those causes, but it is not so. Rather, there are only contingents, each of which needs a cause, and what is said: 'the existences of the individuals are other than the existence of each one of them' is talk devoid of achievement. And there is an apparent research in it. And here there is an objection which the philosophers must be alerted to: that the individuals of the series, if the aggregate is other than each single and needs a cause other than the cause of each single, then an objection is brought against them in the finite series, like the series of ten intellects for example. For the cause of the aggregate of this series cannot be itself, nor inside it—this is obvious—nor can it be outside it, for then it would be necessary or contingent. If necessary, then multiplicity of the Necessary would follow, because they do not allow the emanation of two effects from Him—exalted is He—and they have attributed the First Intellect to Him. So the cause of the aggregate must be another necessary being. And if contingent, then a succession of causes would follow. The conclusion is that the statement that the chain of causes ends at the Necessary and the denial of the permissibility of two effects from a single agent are contradictory. And we have indicated in the beginning of the third section what may repel this objection, so let it be considered.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on al-Iji's text, explaining the distinction between the two types of causation. The commentator first clarifies that the confusion ('ishtibah') arises from the questioner's claim that since each individual has a cause within the series, the whole series, being nothing but the individuals, needs no external cause. The commentator then explains the statement 'they are two distinct matters' by arguing that 'each single' and 'the whole' are different in concept and in external judgments. He gives examples: a loaf suffices for each single but not for all together, and a stone is carried by all together but not by each single. He then presents a proof of distinction based on the existence of a third entity that is the sum of A and B, distinct from the composite with its relational form. This leads to a discussion of the commentator's own position and an objection against the philosophers regarding finite series, showing that their view leads to a contradiction between the ending of the chain at the Necessary and the denial of multiple effects from one agent. The passage ends by referring to an earlier section for a possible resolution.

العقول العشرة

توارد العلل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' view that the chain of causes ends at the Necessary Being is consistent with their denial of multiple effects from one agent.
Correction: The commentator shows that in a finite series, the cause of the whole must be external and leads to either multiple necessary beings or an infinite regress, contradicting their own principles.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what consequence follows if the cause of the aggregate in a finite series is necessary?
A) It would lead to an infinite regress.
B) It would imply multiple necessary beings.
C) It would be impossible for the aggregate to exist.
D) It would mean the cause is contingent.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' position on the chain of causes is inconsistent when applied to finite series, as it forces either multiple necessary beings or an infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the chain of events that brought you to this moment, do you honestly believe that every cause is ultimately dependent on Allah, or do you sometimes act as if you are self-sufficient? This lesson shows that even a finite series cannot stand without an external cause. Where in your life do you need to acknowledge your utter dependence on Him right now?
?
The philosophers admitted that the chain ends at the Necessary Being but denied that one agent can produce multiple direct effects. Are there areas in your faith where you hold one truth but reject another that logically follows from it? Will you commit to seeking consistency in your beliefs, even if it means changing a comfortable position?
?
The commentator hints that a solution exists in the earlier discussion. If you were faced with a theological contradiction, would you search the scriptures for resolution, or rely solely on your own reasoning? What does your answer reveal about the state of your heart towards divine revelation?

The Fourth Argument: Cause of the Whole and Each Part
The fourth argument with its example, response, and denial of a third entity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument that the cause of the whole does not have to cause each part individually, and the immediate response that distinguishes independent causation from other forms.

Core Idea
The cause of the whole does not need to individually cause each part.

This lesson presents the fourth argument in the series: if the cause of the whole were required to cause each part, then a part that is the cause of the whole would cause itself. The author illustrates with the example of the Necessary acting on a contingent, and then responds by specifying that the discussion concerns independent causation. A denial of a 'third entity' follows, emphasizing that multiplicity is conceptual.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and its premise
Start by reading the passage that states the fourth argument: the cause of the whole does not have to cause each part. Identify the consequence (self-causation) that the argument aims to avoid.
2
Understand the example
The author gives an example: if the Necessary acts on a contingent, the resulting sum is contingent and needs a cause. That cause cannot cause everything. Ponder why this example supports the argument.
3
Examine the response
The response restricts the discussion to an independent efficient cause. Note that a part of the series cannot be such a cause.
4
Reflect on the denial of a third entity
The passage denies a 'third entity' beyond the two parts; duality and multiplicity are merely conceptual. This counters a possible objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع أن العلة الموجدة للكل لا يجب أن تكون موجدة لكل واحد من أجزائه حتى يلزم من كون العلة الموجدة للسلسلة جزءاً منها كون ذلك الجزء موجداً لنفسه، فإن الواجب إذا أثر في ممكن حصل مجموعها، وذلك المجموع ممكن لتوقفه على الممكن الذي هو جزؤه، فلا بد له من موجد ويمتنع أن يكون ذلك الموجد موجداً لكل . لامتناع كون الواجب أثراً لشيء. والجواب : أن الكلام في العلة الموجدة المستقلة بالتأثير والإيجاد ولا يمكن أن يكون بعض السلسلة المفروضة علة موجدة لها جزء منه، الثالث كل فكان كل واحد داخلاً فيه انتهى وفيه : أنا لا نسلم تحقق ثالث إنما المعلوم ضرورة عروض الاثنينية والكثرة، والجزئية والكلية ويجوز أن يكون معروضها المتحقق كل واحد من (۱) و (ب) والتغاير بينهما بالاعتبار، وهو لا يكفي في تعليله بعلة موجدة. واعلم أن الشارح قدس سره قد قرر هذا البرهان في حواشي شرح حكمة العين بوجه لا يحتاج إلى إثبات التغاير، ولخصه بما لا مزيد عليه وإن شئت فارجع إليه . قوله : ( على أي وجه فرض إلخ ) أشار بذلك إلى أن تعليل المجموع بالمجموع ليس عين تعليل كل واحد من آحاد السلسلة بآخر لتحققه في صورة يكون مجموع الآحاد متناهية معللاً كل واحد بالآخر وإلى أن الاستدلال المذكور يبطل الدور أيضاً . قوله : ( سواء فرض إلخ) بل نقول تعليل المجموع بالمجموع وإن لم يفرض تعليل الآحاد . قوله : ( الرابع إلخ) منع مع السند وهو في الحقيقة صورة نقض، ولذا تعرض في الجواب يعد إثبات المقدمة لدفع السند . كلامه قوله : ( على سبيل الدور أولا على سبيل الدور) إذا فرض في تعليل المجموع بالمجموع تعليل الآحاد بالآحاد على سبيل الدور كان مغايراً لما نحن فيه ولا ضير لأن مقصوده بيان أن مطلق تعليل مجموع بالمجموع محال بديهة سواء كان فيه تعليل الآحاد بالآحاد لا على سبيل الدور، كما فيما نحن بصدده، أو على سبيل الدور كما في صورة أخرى. وقد يقال : معنى إنما قلنا أولاً أن في تعليل الآحاد بالآحاد تعليل المجموع بالمجموع، وهو باطل بديهة، سواء قلنا : إن في تعليل المجموع بالمجموع تعليل الآحاد بالآحاد ، فإنه لا يضر ذلك القول بالجزم بأن تعليل المجموع بالمجموع باطل وهو الفرض على سبيل الدور أو لم نقل بأن فيه ذلك، فإنه أيضاً لا يضر وهو الفرض لا على سبيل الدور. قوله : ( والجواب أن الكلام في العلة الموجدة (المستقلة يرد على هذا الجواب أنه لا يلزم أن يكون موجد الكل بنفسه موجداً لكل جزء منه بنفسه بل يجوز أن يكون موجداً له بما هو داخل فيها للقطع بأن (ا) إذا أوجد (ج) و (ب) إذا أوجد ( د ) كان مجموع (اب) علة مستقلة
The fourth: that the efficient cause of the whole does not have to be the efficient cause of each one of its parts, such that from the fact that the efficient cause of the series is a part of it, it follows that that part is the cause of itself. For if the Necessary acts upon a contingent, its sum comes into existence, and that sum is contingent because it depends upon the contingent which is its part, so it must have a cause, and it is impossible that that cause be the cause of everything, due to the impossibility of the Necessary being an effect of something. And the answer: that the discussion is about the efficient cause that is independent in effect and origination, and it is impossible that some part of the supposed series be an efficient cause of it, a part of it, the third: everything so that each single was inside it—end. And in it: we do not accept the existence of a third; rather, what is known necessarily is the occurrence of duality and multiplicity, and partness and wholeness, and it is possible that their actual substrate is each one of (A) and (B), and the distinction between them is conceptual, and that is not sufficient for its being caused by an efficient cause. And know that the commentator—may his secret be sanctified—has formulated this proof in his glosses on the commentary of Hikmat al-Ayn in a way that does not need to prove the distinction, and summarized it in a manner beyond which there is no further. And if you wish, refer to it. His statement: 'in whatever way it is supposed etc.' indicated by that that the causation of the aggregate by the aggregate is not identical to the causation of each one of the individuals of the series by another, since it occurs in the case where the aggregate of individuals is finite, each one being caused by the other, and that the aforementioned argument also refutes the cycle. His statement: 'whether it is supposed etc.' rather we say that the causation of the aggregate by the aggregate, even if the causation of the individuals is not supposed. His statement: 'the fourth etc.' is a denial with a premise, and it is in reality the form of a refutation, so he addresses in the answer after establishing the premise to repel the premise. His statement: 'on the way of cycle or not on the way of cycle' if it is supposed in the causation of the aggregate by the aggregate a causation of the individuals by the individuals on the way of cycle, it would be different from what we are dealing with, and there is no harm because his purpose is to show that absolute causation of an aggregate by an aggregate is impossible by intuition, whether it contains the causation of the individuals by the individuals not on the way of cycle, as in our case, or on the way of cycle as in another case. And it may be said: the meaning of 'only we said first' is that in the causation of the individuals by the individuals there is a causation of the aggregate by the aggregate, and it is false by intuition, whether we say that in the causation of the aggregate by the aggregate there is a causation of the individuals by the individuals—for that does not harm the statement of certainty that the causation of the aggregate by the aggregate is false, and it is the presupposition on the way of cycle—or we do not say that there is that in it—for that also does not harm, and it is the presupposition not on the way of cycle. His statement: 'and the answer is that the discussion is about the efficient cause (independent)' an objection is raised against this answer: that it does not follow that the cause of the whole by itself is the cause of each part by itself; rather, it is permissible that it be the cause of it insofar as it is inside it, because it is known necessarily that if (A) causes (C) and (B) causes (D), then the sum (AB) is an independent cause.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument and its subsequent discussion. The argument states that the efficient cause of the whole does not have to cause each part individually; otherwise, if the cause of the whole series is a part of it, that part would be causing itself. The example of the Necessary acting on a contingent is given: the resulting sum is contingent and needs a cause, but that cause cannot be the cause of everything. In response, it is said that the discussion concerns an independent efficient cause, and a part of the series cannot be such a cause. The passage then includes a denial of the third entity argument, saying that duality and multiplicity are merely conceptual. It then references the commentator's alternative proof in his glosses on Hikmat al-Ayn. The remainder of the passage explains the commentator's glosses on various phrases: 'in whatever way it is supposed' indicates that the causation of the aggregate is not the same as causing each individual, and it refutes the cycle as well. 'Whether it is supposed' means that even without assuming causation of individuals, the point stands. 'The fourth' is a denial with a premise, and the answer addresses it. The discussion of 'on the way of cycle or not' clarifies that the argument applies to both cases. Finally, an objection is raised against the response: that causing the whole does not require causing each part individually; rather, causing the whole compositely is possible, as illustrated by the example of (A) causing (C) and (B) causing (D), making (AB) an independent cause.

Key Terms

العلة الموجدة
الواجب
الممكن
التعليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is the cause of a whole, it must be the cause of each part individually.
Correction: The cause of the whole can be the cause of the whole as a composite without directly causing each part; otherwise, self-causation would occur.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, why is it not necessary for the cause of the whole to cause each part individually?
A) Because the parts are infinite and cannot all be caused.
B) Because if it did, the part that is the cause of the whole would cause itself.
C) Because the cause of the whole is the sum of the causes of the parts.
D) Because the whole is not composed of parts.

Key Takeaway
The cause of the whole does not need to cause each part individually; doing so would lead to self-causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever rely on means or secondary causes as if they were independent, forgetting that Allah is the only independent efficient cause? How does this argument humble you to attribute every effect directly to Him?
?
Have you ever thought that Allah's care for the 'big picture' of your life means He neglects the details? How does the denial of a 'third entity' remind you that the whole is not separate from the parts, so His care extends to every detail?
?
The argument shows that the cause of the whole does not need to cause each part individually. In your daily choices, do you act as if you are the efficient cause of your own success? How can you practice attributing every small outcome to Allah's will?

Commentary on the Fourth Argument: Clarifications and Objection-Answer
The commentator's glosses on the fourth argument and the objection-answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's elaboration on the fourth argument, including the refutation of the cycle and the objection that causing the whole compositely is possible.

Core Idea
The commentator shows that causing the whole by the aggregate is impossible in all cases, but an objection offers a way out.

This lesson covers the commentator's extensive analysis of the fourth argument. He explains that the argument also refutes cyclical causation, and clarifies that whether the causation of individuals is assumed or not, the causation of the aggregate is impossible. An objection then challenges the response, suggesting that causing the whole by composite causation is possible, using an example.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentator's glosses
Start by reading the commentator's summary of the argument, noting that he provides a version that does not require the proof of distinction.
2
Understand the point about 'aggregate causation'
The commentator explains that 'causing the aggregate by the aggregate' is not the same as causing each individual. He also says the argument refutes the cycle.
3
Analyze the objection
The objection states that the cause of the whole does not have to cause each part individually; it can cause the whole compositely, as in the example of (A) causing (C) and (B) causing (D) making (AB) independent cause.

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument and its subsequent discussion. The argument states that the efficient cause of the whole does not have to cause each part individually; otherwise, if the cause of the whole series is a part of it, that part would be causing itself. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The commentator's argument requires a distinction between whole and parts.
Correction: The commentator offers a version that does not need that distinction.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what does the phrase 'on the way of cycle or not' indicate?
A) That the argument only works if there is a cycle.
B) That the argument fails if there is no cycle.
C) That the argument applies whether or not the causation of individuals is cyclical.
D) That the cycle is the only way to avoid self-causation.

Key Takeaway
The commentator's analysis shows that the argument refutes both cyclical and non-cyclical causation of the whole, but an objection suggests composite causation as a counterexample.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you ever rely on 'composite causes'—multiple factors that together make something happen—while ignoring the ultimate, independent cause behind them? What would it take for you to shift your reliance entirely to Allah as the true cause?
?
The argument shows that cyclical causation is impossible. Are there areas of your thinking (e.g., justifying sins, rationalizing procrastination) where you fall into circular reasoning? Where specifically do you need to break that cycle?
?
When faced with an objection that seems to refute a core belief, like the composite causation objection here, your immediate reaction might be to defend your position. How willing are you to let the argument test your certainty, and what step will you take today to examine a deep belief you hold?

Causality and Created Things
advanced
6 min
Thesis and the Fourth Argument Against Infinite Regress
Al-Iji presents the thesis against infinite regress and the fourth argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the thesis that infinite regress is impossible and the fourth argument that the cause of the whole does not need to cause each part.

Core Idea
The cause of the whole chain does not need to cause every part, otherwise a part would cause itself.

Al-Iji begins by asserting that an infinite chain of contingent beings depending on a cause is impossible, a foundational principle in Islamic theology. He then clarifies that this holds in all modes of causality, and presents the fourth argument that the cause of the entire series does not have to be the cause of each individual member. This argument avoids a regress because otherwise a part would be causing itself, which is absurd.

Guided Path
1
Read the thesis statement
Read the opening sentence carefully: 'التسلسل محال أن يستند الممكن إلى علة بديهة'. This is the claim that infinite regress is impossible, known intuitively.
Notice it says 'بديهة' meaning it is self-evident.
2
Understand the modes of causality
Al-Iji lists three modes: whole-to-whole, individual-to-individual circular, and non-circular. Understand that the impossibility applies to all.
This sets the scope of the argument.
3
Analyze the fourth argument
Read the fourth argument: the cause of the whole does not have to cause each part. If it did, the cause (which is part of the chain) would be causing itself, which is impossible.
Focus on the logical necessity.
4
See the implication
The argument concludes that a regress is avoided because the cause of the whole is not the cause of each part, thus no part can be its own cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثامن : التسلسل محال أن يستند الممكن إلى علة بديهة على أي وجه فرض أعني سواء فرض في تعليل المجموع بالمجموع، تعليل الآحاد بالآحاد على سبيل الدور ، أو لا على سبيل الدور الرابع أن العلة الموجدة للكل لا يجب أن تكون موجدة لكل واحد من أجزائه حتى يلزم من كون العلة الموجدة للسلسلة جزءاً منها كون ذلك الجزء موجداً لنفسه، فإن الواجب إذا أثر في ممكن حصل مجموعها، وذلك المجموع ممكن لتوقفه على الممكن الذي هو جزؤه، فلا بد له من موجد ويمتنع أن يكون ذلك الموجد موجداً لكل . لامتناع كون الواجب أثراً لشيء. والجواب : أن الكلام في العلة الموجدة المستقلة بالتأثير والإيجاد ولا يمكن أن يكون بعض السلسلة المفروضة علة موجدة لها
The Fifth Observation - Eighth Proposition: Infinite regress is impossible for a contingent to depend on a cause, intuitively, in any mode assumed, whether it is assumed in the causation of the whole by the whole, or the causation of individuals by individuals in a circular manner, or not in a circular manner. The fourth: that the cause that gives existence to the whole does not necessarily have to give existence to each one of its parts, such that from the fact that the cause of the chain is a part of it, that part would be causing its own existence. For when the Necessary influences a contingent, their sum results, and that sum is contingent due to its dependence on the contingent which is its part, so it must have a causer, and it is impossible for that causer to be the causer of each one, because it is impossible for the Necessary to be an effect of something. The answer: that the discussion is about the independent efficient cause in influencing and giving existence, and it is impossible for some part of the supposed chain to be an efficient cause for it.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a statement of a key proof against infinite regress. It begins by asserting the impossibility of an infinite chain of contingent beings depending on a cause, stating that this is intuitively known. It then clarifies that this applies whether the causation is considered between the whole chain and another whole, or between individual parts in a circular or non-circular manner. The fourth argument is then presented: the cause of the entire chain does not have to be the cause of each individual part. If it did, then since the cause of the chain is itself a part of the chain (in a series of causes and effects), it would be causing itself, which is absurd. The text explains that when the Necessary Being acts on a contingent, the result is a composite that is itself contingent, requiring another cause. It is impossible for that cause to be the cause of each part because that would make the Necessary an effect. The answer given is that the discussion concerns the independent efficient cause, which by definition has no part in the chain as a co-cause. The possibility of a part being the independent cause of the whole is thus ruled out.

Key Terms
التسلسل
العلة الموجدة
الواجب
الممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fourth argument claims that the cause of the whole must cause each part.
Correction: Actually, the argument says the opposite: the cause of the whole does not need to cause each part, which prevents a regress.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth argument conclude?
A) The cause of the whole must cause each part
B) The cause of the whole does not need to cause each part
C) Regress is possible in circular form
D) The chain is not contingent

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument demonstrates that infinite regress is impossible because the cause of the whole chain does not need to cause each part, preventing a self-causation problem.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that the whole chain of contingent beings needs a cause outside itself. Do you ever feel trapped in an endless cycle of causes—work, entertainment, worries—without a final purpose? What would it mean to genuinely anchor your existence in the One Cause that is not part of the chain?
?
If every moment of your life depends on the Necessary Being, can you think of one area where you still act as if you are independent? What would change if you truly believed that even your next breath is a contingent effect requiring a Cause?
?
The argument shows that if the cause were a part, it would cause itself—an impossibility. Is there anything in your life that you treat as both cause and effect, relying on something created to give you meaning while also claiming to serve Allah? How can you realign your reliance to break that cycle?

Answer to the Fourth Argument: The Independent Efficient Cause
Al-Iji gives the answer to the fourth argument by specifying the independent efficient cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the answer to the fourth argument: that the discussion concerns only the independent efficient cause, not any cause.

Core Idea
The answer restricts the discussion to the independent cause, which cannot be a part of the chain.

The fourth argument against infinite regress raised a potential objection: if the cause of the whole does not cause each part, how can it be a cause? Al-Iji's answer clarifies that the discussion is about the 'independent efficient cause' (العلة الموجدة المستقلة), which by definition acts alone and cannot be part of the chain. Thus, no part of the chain can be such a cause for the whole, preserving the impossibility of regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer
Read the answer: 'والجواب : أن الكلام في العلة الموجدة المستقلة بالتأثير والإيجاد ولا يمكن أن يكون بعض السلسلة المفروضة علة موجدة لها'. This is a concise response.
Notice the term 'المستقلة' (independent).
2
Understand the concept of independent cause
The answer specifies that the argument is about the cause that is independent in its effect and existence-giving. Such a cause has no need of other causes.
3
Apply to the chain
Since the independent cause cannot be a part of the chain (as parts depend on other parts), the chain cannot have such a cause within it, so regress is avoided.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثامن : التسلسل محال أن يستند الممكن إلى علة بديهة على أي وجه فرض أعني سواء فرض في تعليل المجموع بالمجموع، تعليل الآحاد بالآحاد على سبيل الدور ، أو لا على سبيل الدور الرابع أن العلة الموجدة للكل لا يجب أن تكون موجدة لكل واحد من أجزائه حتى يلزم من كون العلة الموجدة للسلسلة جزءاً منها كون ذلك الجزء موجداً لنفسه، فإن الواجب إذا أثر في ممكن حصل مجموعها، وذلك المجموع ممكن لتوقفه على الممكن الذي هو جزؤه، فلا بد له من موجد ويمتنع أن يكون ذلك الموجد موجداً لكل . لامتناع كون الواجب أثراً لشيء. والجواب : أن الكلام في العلة الموجدة المستقلة بالتأثير والإيجاد ولا يمكن أن يكون بعض السلسلة المفروضة علة موجدة لها
The Fifth Observation - Eighth Proposition: Infinite regress is impossible for a contingent to depend on a cause, intuitively, in any mode assumed, whether it is assumed in the causation of the whole by the whole, or the causation of individuals by individuals in a circular manner, or not in a circular manner. The fourth: that the cause that gives existence to the whole does not necessarily have to give existence to each one of its parts, such that from the fact that the cause of the chain is a part of it, that part would be causing its own existence. For when the Necessary influences a contingent, their sum results, and that sum is contingent due to its dependence on the contingent which is its part, so it must have a causer, and it is impossible for that causer to be the causer of each one, because it is impossible for the Necessary to be an effect of something. The answer: that the discussion is about the independent efficient cause in influencing and giving existence, and it is impossible for some part of the supposed chain to be an efficient cause for it.

This passage is a statement of a key proof against infinite regress. It then clarifies that this applies whether the causation is considered between the whole chain and another whole, or between individual parts in a circular or non-circular manner. التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A part of the chain can be the cause of the whole chain in some sense.
Correction: The answer clarifies that only an independent cause can be such, but a part of the chain is not independent.

Checkpoint
What does 'العلة الموجدة المستقلة' refer to?
A) The cause of the whole
B) The independent efficient cause
C) The cause of each part
D) The chain itself

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument is resolved by noting that only an independent cause is under discussion, and such a cause cannot be part of the chain it causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever placed your ultimate trust in a person, position, or possession as if it were the independent source of your security? How does this lesson expose that trust as misplaced because every created thing is just a link in the chain?
?
Consider the chain of causes in your own life—your health, wealth, relationships. Do you treat any of these as the final, independent cause of your happiness? What would change if you truly saw them as dependent links?
?
If the independent efficient cause is not part of the chain you can see, what does that demand from you in terms of your worship and gratitude? Can you name one specific attachment you need to break after internalizing this lesson?
