Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does infinite regress of effects lead to inequality in the number of cause and effect relations?
A) Because the first cause is not an effect.
B) Because effects are finite.
C) Because temporal priority is not considered.
D) Because the philosophers made an error.

Key Takeaway
The correlation argument demonstrates that infinite regress is impossible because it would break the necessary equivalence between cause-relations and effect-relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If infinite regress is impossible in causality, what does that mean for your own chain of influences? Consider someone who shaped you—they were shaped by others. Is there a beginning to that chain? How does this point you to the ultimate cause of your existence, and what is your response to that cause?
?
The philosophers assumed an infinite past to link the temporal with the eternal. Do you ever treat your own past as infinite, as if there is no starting point for your habits, pride, or doubts? How does the impossibility of infinite regress demand that you confront a definite beginning—especially for your faith?
?
The proof shows that cause and effect must be in balance. In your life, are there 'causes' without intended 'effects'—actions, words, or thoughts that lack purposeful outcome? How might aligning your actions with meaningful effects reflect the order that the Creator placed in creation?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
7 min
The Limitation of the Correlation Proof for Two-Sided Regress
al-Iji explains the limitation of the correlation proof and refutes al-Dawānī.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the correlation proof does not apply to an infinite regress that is infinite on both sides, and how al-Dawānī's attempt to apply it is refuted.

Core Idea
In two-sided infinite regress, every term is both cause and effect, so numbers remain equal.

The commentator points out that the correlation proof only works when the regress is one-sided. If the chain is infinite in both directions, each individual is both cause and effect, so no inequality arises. The passage also critiques al-Dawānī's attempt to isolate a segment and apply the proof there.

Guided Path
1
Identify the limitation statement
Read the first sentence: the proof does not apply to regress from both sides. Note the reason: each individual is both cause and effect.
2
Understand al-Dawānī's proposal
Al-Dawānī suggested that taking a segment from the infinite chain and comparing cause and effect counts within that segment could still yield inequality.
He thought the segment would have a starting effect with no cause in the segment, leading to more effects than causes.
3
Follow the refutation
The commentator argues that in the segment, every effecthood has a matching causehood within the segment (since each effect is caused by the previous term), so the numbers remain equal.
This preserves the necessary equivalence.
4
Contrast with one-sided finite case
Only when the chain has a definite beginning (finite) does the inequality appear, as the first term lacks one of the relations.

وَهَذَا الْوَجْهُ جَارٍ فِي تَسَلْسُلِ الْمُتَضَايِفَاتِ فَيُقَالُ: لَوْ تَسَلْسَلَتِ الْمَعْلُولَاتُ إِلَى غَيْرِ النِّهَايَةِ لَزَادَ عَدَدُ الْعِلِّيَّةِ عَلَى عَدَدِ الْمَعْلُولِيَّةِ؛ لِأَنَّ كَانَتْ أَوْ مَعْلُولِيَّاتٍ مُجْتَمِعَةً أَوْ مُتَعَاقِبَةً، فَيَجْرِي فِي الْحَوَادِثِ الْغَيْرِ الْمُتَنَاهِيَةِ الَّتِي أَثْبَتَهَا الْفَلَاسِفَةُ فِي رَبْطِ الْحَادِثِ بِالْقَدِيمِ لِاتِّصَافِ آحَادِهَا بِالسَّابِقِيَّةِ وَالْمَسْبُوقِيَّةِ مَعَ تَنَاهِيهَا فِي جَانِبِ الِاسْتِقْبَالِ، فَلَوْ تَسَلْسَلَتْ إِلَى غَيْرِ النِّهَايَةِ فِي جَانِبِ الْمَاضِي لَزِمَ زِيَادَةُ عَدَدِ الْمَسْبُوقِيَّاتِ عَلَى عَدَدِ السَّابِقِيَّاتِ وَهُوَ يَسْتَلْزِمُ بُطْلَانَ التَّكَافُؤِ بَيْنَهُمَا. وَمِنْ هَذَا ظَهَرَ أَنَّ هَذَا الْبُرْهَانَ لَا يَجْرِي فِي التَّسَلْسُلِ مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ، لِأَنَّ كُلَّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْ آحَادِ تِلْكَ السِّلْسِلَةِ مَوْصُوفٌ بِالْعِلِّيَّةِ وَالْمَعْلُولِيَّةِ فَلَا زِيَادَةَ لِعَدَدِ أَحَدِ الْمُتَضَايِفَيْنِ عَلَى الْآخَرِ. وَمَا قَالَهُ بَعْضُ النَّاظِرِينَ نَاقِلاً عَنِ الْمُحَقِّقِ الدَّوَانِيِّ فِي جَرَيَانِهِ فِيهِ مِنْ أَنَّا إِذَا أَخَذْنَا مِنْ تِلْكَ السِّلْسِلَةِ سِلْسِلَةً غَيْرَ مُتَنَاهِيَةٍ مِنْ مَعْلُولٍ مُعَيَّنٍ وَتَصَاعَدْنَا فِي الْعِلَلِ الْغَيْرِ الْمُتَنَاهِيَةِ فَلَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ عَدَدُ الْعِلِّيَّاتِ وَالْمَعْلُولِيَّاتِ الْوَاقِعَةِ فِي تِلْكَ الْقِطْعَةِ مُتَكَافِئَةً، ضَرُورَةَ أَنَّ الْعِلِّيَّةَ الَّتِي تُضَايِفُ الْمَعْلُولَاتِ الْوَاقِعَةَ فِيهَا لَا يُمْكِنُ أَنْ يَكُونَ فِيمَا تَحْتَ تِلْكَ الْمَعْلُولِيَّاتِ، وَهُوَ ظَاهِرٌ. فَفِيهِ بَحْثٌ لِأَنَّ كُلَّ مَعْلُولِيَّةٍ فِي تِلْكَ الْقِطْعَةِ مُضَايِفَةٌ لِلْعِلِّيَةِ الَّتِي قَبْلَهُ فَالْمَعْلُولِيَّةُ الَّتِي فِي الْمَعْلُولِ الْمُعَيَّنِ الَّذِي أُخِذَ مَبْدَأً مُضَايِفَةٌ لِلْعِلِّيَةِ الَّتِي قَبْلَهُ بِلَا وَاسِطَةٍ وَهَلُمَّ جَراً. وَلَيْسَ شَيْءٌ مِنَ السِّلْسِلَةِ غَيْرَ مَوْصُوفٍ بِالْعِلِّيَةِ فَلَا زِيَادَةَ لِعَدَدِ الْمَعْلُولِيَّاتِ عَلَى عَدَدِ الْعِلِّيَّاتِ حَتَّى يُسْتَدَلَّ بِهَا عَلَى بُطْلَانِ التَّكَافُؤِ الْمُسْتَلْزِمِ لِبُطْلَانِ التَّضَايُفِ، بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا كَانَتِ السِّلْسِلَةُ مُتَنَاهِيَةً فِي الْجَانِبَيْنِ، فَإِنَّهُ يَتَّصِفُ الْمَبْدَأُ بِالْمَعْلُولِيَّةِ فَقَطْ أَوِ الْعِلِّيَةِ وَسَائِرُ الْآحَادِ مَوْصُوفَةٌ بِهِمَا فَيَزِيدُ عَدَدُ آحَادِ أَحَدِ الْجَانِبَيْنِ.
And this aspect applies to the regress of correlatives, so it is said: if effects were to regress infinitely, the number of cause-relations would exceed the number of effect-relations, because everything that is an effect in this chain is a cause without the converse, for the first cause is not an effect while being a cause. And if the effects were finite, the final effect would be an effect and not a cause, so the numbers of cause- and effect-relations would be equal as is their right. In short, regress in correlatives necessitates that one of the two relations be greater in number than the other, which is false. His statement: ('And this aspect applies to the regress of correlatives etc.') – whether they are causal relations or effect relations, simultaneous or successive. So it applies to the infinite events that the philosophers posited in linking the temporal to the eternal, due to the description of their individuals with priority and posteriority, while being finite on the future side. So if they were to regress infinitely on the past side, the number of posteriorities would exceed the number of priorities, which entails the nullification of equivalence between them. And from this it appears that this proof does not apply to regress from both sides, because every individual of that chain is described with causehood and effecthood, so there is no increase in the number of one correlative over the other. And what some thinkers said, quoting from al-Muḥaqqiq al-Dawānī, about its application to it – that if we take from that chain an infinite chain from a specific effect and ascend through the infinite causes, then the numbers of cause-relations and effect-relations occurring in that segment must be equal, necessarily, because the causehood that correlates with the effecthoods occurring in it cannot be among those effecthoods, which is apparent – there is discussion about it, because every effecthood in that segment is correlative to the causehood that precedes it, so the effecthood in the specific effect taken as a beginning is correlative to the causehood before it directly, and so on. And nothing in the chain is undescribed with causehood, so there is no increase in the number of effecthoods over causehoods that could be used to prove the falsity of equivalence entailing the falsity of correlation, unlike when the chain is finite on both sides, for then the beginning is described with effecthood only or causehood and the rest are described with both, so the number of individuals of one side increases.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage extends the previous argument to correlatives (al-mutadāyifāt). It first restates the point: in an infinite regress of effects, the number of cause-relations would exceed effect-relations because the first cause is not an effect. If finite, the numbers equal. The commentator then notes that this argument applies to both causal and effect relations whether simultaneous or temporal, and gives the example of an infinite series of events on the past side (as philosophers argue for linking events to the eternal). If regress were infinite in the past, the number of posteriorities (masbūqiyyāt) would exceed priorities (sābiqiyyāt), violating the necessary equivalence of correlatives. Then the commentator clarifies that this proof does not work when the regress is from both sides (e.g., infinite in both past and future, or both cause and effect sides), because each term is both cause and effect, so the numbers remain equal. The passage ends with a refutation of al-Dawānī's claim that the proof can be applied even when both sides are infinite by isolating a segment. The commentator argues that in that segment, every effecthood is precisely matched to a preceding causehood, so no inequality arises. The proof only works when the chain has a definite beginning (one-sided).

Misconception: The correlation proof can be applied to any infinite series to show its impossibility.
Correction: The proof only works for one-sided infinite regress; in two-sided regress, all terms are both cause and effect, preserving equality.

Proof from Correlation for the Impossibility of Infinite Regress
Al-Iji proves the impossibility of infinite regress through the correlation of cause and effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the necessary equality of correlatives (cause and effect) proves that an infinite regress of causes and effects is impossible.

Core Idea
True correlatives must be equal in number; infinite regress breaks that equality.

Al-Iji presents a rigorous proof that infinite regress is impossible by examining the necessary correlation between cause and effect. This argument is a cornerstone of Islamic theological reasoning about chains of dependence. We'll walk through the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice that the author begins by stating the conclusion: infinite regress is impossible.
2
Understand what correlatives are and their necessary equality
The argument hinges on the concept of 'mutadā'ifān' (correlatives). True correlatives like cause and effect must exist together and be equal in number. The father-son example shows that not all correlatives require numerical equality, but cause-effect in this chain does.
True correlatives: existence of one necessarily implies the other.
3
Follow the argument that infinite regress causes imbalance
In an infinite regress, every term is both cause and effect except the first? Actually, there is no first; so causehood and effecthood counts become unequal, violating the equality required by true correlatives.
The text says: 'if effects regress infinitely, the number of causehoods exceeds the number of effecthoods'.
4
Reflect on the proof's conclusion
The conclusion is that infinite regress is impossible because it contradicts the necessary equality of correlatives. This supports the need for a First Cause that is not an effect.
This argument is often used to prove the existence of a necessary being (Allah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثامن : التسلسل محال أن يستند الممكن إلى علة الأخير معلول وليس بعلة لشيء من تلك السلسلة، فقد زاد عدد المعلولية على عدد العلية ولو كانت العلل متناهية لم يلزم ذلك، فإن مبدأ السلسلة علة وليس بمعلول ومنتهاها أعني المعلول الأخير معلول وليس بعلة فيتساوى عدد العلية والمعلولية . ( وأما الاستثنائية ) وهي بطلان التالي فلان) العلة والمعلول ) أي العلية ( والمعلولية (متضايفان) تضايفاً حقيقياً ( ومن لوازمهما التكافؤ في الوجود ) أي إذا وجد أحد المتضايفين الحقيقيين وجد الآخر قطعاً، فلا بد أن يوجد بإزاء كل واحد ) من أحدهما ( واحد من الآخر، فيكونان متساويين في العدد ضرورة)، وإن لم يجب تساوي العدد في المتضايفين المشهورين كاب واحد له أبناء كثيرة، لكن له بإزاء كل بنوة أبوة. وهذا الوجه جار في تسلسل المتضايفات فيقال: لو تسلسلت المعلولات إلى غير النهاية لزاد عدد العلية على عدد المعلولية؛ لأن كل ما هو معلول في هذه السلسلة، فهو علة من غير عكس فإن العلة الأولى ليست معلولة مع كونها علة، ولو كانت المعلولات متناهية لكان المعلول الأخير معلولاً، ولم يكن علة، فيتساوى عدد العلية والمعلولية كما هو حقهما. وبالجملة فإن التسلسل في المتضايفات يستلزم كون إحدى الإضافتين أزيد عدداً من الأخرى، وهو باطل.
The fifth observatory - the eighth section: Infinite regress is impossible. That a contingent (mumkin) depends on a cause ... the last is an effect (ma‘lul) and not a cause for anything in that series; so the number of effecthood exceeds the number of causehood. If the causes were finite this would not follow, because the beginning of the series is a cause and not an effect, and its end, meaning the last effect, is an effect and not a cause, so the number of causehood and effecthood are equal. (As for the conditional [form]) and it is the falsity of the consequent [?] the cause and effect, i.e., causehood and effecthood, are correlatives (mutadā’ifān) in a true correlation, and from their necessary concomitants is equality in existence, i.e., if one of two true correlatives exists, the other necessarily exists; so for every one of the one there must exist one from the other, so they are necessarily equal in number, even though equality of number is not necessary in well-known correlatives like one father with many children, but for every sonship there is a fatherhood. This argument is valid for an infinite regress of correlatives, so it is said: if effects regress infinitely, the number of causehoods exceeds the number of effecthoods, because everything that is an effect in this series is a cause, without the converse; for the first cause is not an effect while being a cause, and if effects were finite, the last effect would be an effect and not a cause, so the numbers of causehood and effecthood are equal, as is their right. In summary, infinite regress in correlatives necessitates that one of the two relations is greater in number than the other, and that is false.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhān) demonstrating the impossibility of infinite regress (tasalsul) in causal chains. The argument is built on the relationship of correlation (al-mutadā’ifān) between cause (‘illa) and effect (ma‘lūl). The author begins by stating the conclusion: infinite regress is impossible. He then explains why a finite series is fine: the first cause is only a cause, the last effect is only an effect, so the counts of causehood and effecthood are equal. In an infinite regress, however, there is no first cause without an effect? Actually he shows that if effects regress infinitely, each effect in the series (except possibly the first? but there is no first) is also a cause for the next, but the first cause (which would be the beginning of the series) is absent; rather, every member is both cause and effect except the first would be only cause? Wait, he says: in an infinite regress, every effect is also a cause, but there is no first cause that is not an effect, so causes outnumber effects? Actually he says: 'if effects regress infinitely, the number of causehoods exceeds the number of effecthoods, because everything that is an effect in this series is a cause, without the converse; for the first cause is not an effect while being a cause' — but in an infinite series there is no first cause; so how does the argument work? Let's follow: he is considering a series of causes and effects where each is a cause for the next, but he posits that if we take an infinite regress of effects (meaning we go backwards without end), then every term is both cause (for the next) and effect (of the previous), but there is no first term that is only cause, so the number of causehoods (each term has one causehood for the term after it) is equal to the number of terms, and the number of effecthoods is also equal to the number of terms (each term has one effecthood from the term before). So they would be equal. But he says: 'because everything that is an effect in this series is a cause, without the converse' — that seems to say that in an infinite series, every effect is also a cause, but not every cause is an effect? Actually he says: 'the first cause is not an effect' but there is no first cause in an infinite series. The argument he presents is actually comparing an infinite series to a finite one. In a finite series, the first is only cause, the last only effect, so causes and effects are equal. In an infinite series, if we imagine it starting somewhere, we get an imbalance. The precise formulation: 'If effects regress infinitely, the number of causehoods exceeds the number of effecthoods' — this is because in an infinite series from a certain point onward, there is no last effect? Wait, the text says: 'because everything that is an effect in this series is a cause, without the converse; for the first cause is not an effect while being a cause' — this is if the series has a first term. But in an infinite regress, there is no first term. So the argument might be assuming we take a particular effect and go up the chain indefinitely; each step we have a cause that is also an effect except the very first cause, but since it is infinite, there is no first cause. So the intended reasoning is better captured in the hashiya. The main text here is somewhat brief. The author then concludes: 'In summary, infinite regress in correlatives necessitates that one of the two relations is greater in number than the other, and that is false.' The argument relies on the necessary equality of correlatives. Thus infinite regress is impossible.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the proof fails because correlatives like father and son do not require numerical equality (one father can have many sons).
Correction: The father-son example is not a 'true correlative' in the context of a causal chain where each cause has exactly one effect and each effect has one cause. In a linear chain of cause and effect, the relationships are one-to-one, so equality in number is necessary.

Checkpoint

A) Because the number of causes would be infinite while effects finite.
B) Because cause and effect are correlatives that must be equal in number, but infinite regress would make one side exceed the other.
C) Because infinite regress implies an actual infinite, which is impossible in itself.
D) Because the first cause would be both cause and effect.

Key Takeaway
The necessary equality of true correlatives shows that an infinite regress of causes and effects is impossible, pointing to a First Cause that is not itself an effect.

Scope of the Correlation Proof
Commentator explains the scope of the correlation proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the correlation proof only works for infinite regress from one side, not when both sides are infinite.

Core Idea
The correlation proof requires one-sided infinity.

The commentator refines the earlier proof by explaining its precise scope. The proof shows a contradiction only when the series is infinite in one direction and finite in the other. If both sides are infinite, every term is both cause and effect, so no inequality arises. He also discusses and refutes an objection by al-Dawani, reinforcing the proof's limited scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases of infinity
The passage distinguishes between a series infinite on one side (e.g., past infinite, future finite) and infinite on both sides.
The proof works only in the first case.
2
Understand why inequality arises in one-sided infinity
In one-sided infinity, the number of 'being-prior' exceeds the number of 'priorities', creating a contradiction in the correlation.
This inequality is the core of the proof.
3
See why two-sided infinity fails
When both sides are infinite, each term is both cause and effect, so numbers are equal and no contradiction arises.
This shows the proof's limited scope.
4
Examine al-Dawani's objection and the refutation
Al-Dawani argued the proof could apply to a segment of a two-sided infinite series. The commentator refutes this by showing that in any segment, causehoods and effecthoods are paired one-to-one.
This subtle point confirms the proof does not work for two-sided infinity.
5
Contrast with finite series
If the series is finite on both sides, the beginning is only cause or only effect, causing excess and thus contradiction.
This reinforces why one-sided infinity is special.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وهذا الوجه جار في تسلسل المتضايفات إلخ عليات كانت أو معلوليات مجتمعة أو متعاقبة فيجري في الحوادث الغير المتناهية التي أثبتها الفلاسفة في ربط الحادث بالقديم لاتصاف آحادها بالسابقية والمسبوقية مع تناهيها في جانب الاستقبال فلو تسلسلت إلى غير النهاية في جانب الماضي لزم زيادة عدد المسبوقيات على عدد السابقيات وهو يستلزم بطلان التكافؤ بينهما . أخذنا من قوله : ( وبالجملة إلخ ومن هذا ظهر أن هذا البرهان لا يجري في التسلسل من الجانبين، لأن كل واحد من آحاد تلك السلسلة موصوف بالعلية ،والمعلولية فلا زيادة لعدد أحد المتضايفين على الآخر. وما قاله بعض الناظرين ناقلاً عن المحقق الدواني في جريانه فيه من أنا إذا تلك السلسلة سلسلة غير متناهية من معلول معين وتصاعدنا في العلل الغير المتناهية فلا بد أن يكون عدد العليات والمعلوليات الواقعة في تلك القطعة متكافئة، ضرورة أن العلية التي تضايف المعلولات الواقعة فيها لا يمكن أن يكون فيما تحت تلك المعلوليات، وهو ظاهر. ففيه بحث لأن كل معلولية في تلك القطعة مضايفة للعلية التي قبله فالمعلولية التي في المعلول المعين الذي أخذ مبدأ مضايفة للعلية التي قبله بلا واسطة وهلم جراً. وليس شيء من السلسلة غير موصوف بالعلية فلا زيادة لعدد المعلوليات على عدد العليات حتى يستدل بها على بطلان التكافؤ المستلزم لبطلان التضايف، بخلاف ما إذا كانت السلسلة متناهية في الجانبين، فإنه يتصف المبدأ بالمعلولية فقط أو العلية وسائر الآحاد موصوفة بهما فيزيد عدد آحاد أحد إحداهما على الأخرى، فيبطل التكافؤ بينهما . والحاصل أن خلاصة البرهان الاستدلال بلزوم زيادة عدد في أحد المتضايفين على تقدير اللاتناهي وهي لا توجد إلا إذا فرض اللاتناهي من جانب واحد .
His saying: 'And this argument is valid for the regress of correlatives etc.' — whether they are causes or effects, simultaneous or sequential. Thus it applies to the endless events that the philosophers have affirmed in connecting the temporal with the eternal, because each individual is characterized by priority and being-prior, while being finite in the future direction. So if they regress infinitely in the past direction, the number of being-prior exceeds the number of priorities, which necessitates the falsity of equality between them. We took this from his saying: 'In summary, etc.' And from this it appears that this proof does not apply to regress on both sides, because every individual in that series is qualified by causehood and effecthood, so there is no excess in the number of one correlative over the other. And what some thinkers have said, reporting from al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani, that it does apply there — namely that if we take that series as an infinite series from a specific effect and ascend through infinite causes, then the number of causehoods and effecthoods occurring in that segment must be equal, necessarily because the causehood that is correlative to the effecthoods occurring therein cannot be among those effecthoods, and that is obvious — this is open to discussion, because every effecthood in that segment is correlative to the causehood that precedes it; thus the effecthood in the specific effect taken as a starting point is correlative to the causehood before it without intermediary, and so on. And nothing in the series is not qualified by causehood, so there is no excess of effecthoods over causehoods such that one could infer the falsity of equality which entails the falsity of correlation, unlike the case where the series is finite on both sides, for then the beginning is qualified only by effecthood or only by causehood, and the rest of the individuals are qualified by both, so the number of individuals of one exceeds the other, thus equality between them is nullified. The upshot is that the essence of the proof is inferring the necessity of an excess in number in one of the two correlatives given infinity, and this exists only when infinity is assumed from one side.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (hashiya) on the previous proof. It clarifies the precise scope of the correlation argument. The commentator begins by explaining that the argument applies to an infinite series where one side is finite (here the future direction). In such a case, the number of 'being-prior' (mashqiyya) exceeds the number of 'priorities' (sābqiyya) because each event has many successors but only one predecessor? Actually the text says: 'if they regress infinitely in the past direction, the number of being-prior exceeds the number of priorities' — this is a different pair of correlatives (priority and being-prior). Then he states the key point: the proof does not apply to infinite regress on both sides (i.e., infinite in both directions, like a two-sided infinite series of causes and effects). In such a series, every term is both cause and effect, so the numbers of each are equal, and no excess arises. He then discusses an objection attributed to al-Dawani, who claimed the proof could still apply if we take a segment of the series. The commentator refutes this: in any segment, each effecthood is correlative to a specific causehood, so the numbers remain equal. The excess only appears when the series has a beginning that is only cause or only effect. Therefore, the proof only works when infinity is from one side only (e.g., infinite regress of effects with a first cause).

Correction: The proof only works when one side is finite; if both sides are infinite, no inequality in correlatives arises.

Part of a Cause vs. Condition and the Status of Absence of Obstacle
Al-Iji defines part of a cause vs. condition and clarifies that absence of obstacle is not a true condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between a part of a cause and a condition, and why the absence of an obstacle is only metaphorically called a condition.

Core Idea
A condition aids effect, a part sustains essence, and absence of obstacle just reveals a hidden condition.

In this passage, al-Iji draws a critical distinction in causal analysis. He separates what belongs to the cause's own makeup (its parts) from what merely enables its action (conditions). He then warns against mistaking the absence of an obstacle for a genuine condition. This will sharpen your understanding of how theologians analyze causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read through the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze every word, then read the full translation. Let the author's own distinctions come through.
Notice the technical terms: جزء العلة, شرط, عدم المانع, كاشف.
2
Identify the definitions of part and condition
Find how the passage defines 'part of the cause' and 'condition'. Note the example of dryness of wood and fire. Ask yourself: why is dryness a condition and not a part?
The distinction hinges on whether the dependence is on the cause's essence (part) or on its effect (condition).
3
Understand why absence of obstacle is not a real condition
The passage explains that absence of obstacle is merely a 'revealer' of a positive condition. It is called a condition only metaphorically. Reflect on the example of clouds and sunlight: why is the removal of clouds not itself the condition?
Non-existence cannot genuinely contribute to existence; it just unveils what is already there.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد التاسع : الفرق بين جزء العلة وشرطها الفرق بين جزء العلة ( المؤثرة ) وشرطها في التأثير هو ) أن الشرط يتوقف عليه تأثير المؤثر ( لا) (ذاته كيبوسة الحطب ، ( فإنها شرط) للإحراق، (إذ النار لا تؤثر في الحطب بالإحراق إلا بعد أن يكون يابساً ، والجزء ما يتوقف عليه ذاته، (أي ذات الموثر فيتوقف أيضاً عليه تأثيره لكن لا ابتداء، بل بواسطة توقفه على ذاته المتوقف على جزئه ) . وعدم المانع ليس مما يتوقف عليه التأثير حتى يشارك الشرط في ذلك، إذ قد علمت أنه ( أي عدم المانع كاشف عن شرط وجودي) يتوقف عليه تأثير المؤثر، كزوال الغيم الكاشف عن ظهور الشمس، الذي هو شرطها في تجفيف الثياب ، ( وعده ) أي عد عدم المانع ( من جملة الشروط ) التي يتوقف عليها التأثير ( نوع من التجوز ؛ لما عرفت من أن العدم لا مدخل له أصلا في الوجود، حتى يعد شرطا حقيقة، بل هو كاشف عما هو شرط فأطلق اسمه عليه، ونسب حكمه إليه .
The ninth section: The difference between the part of a cause and its condition. The difference between the part of the effective cause (al-‘illa al-mu’aththira) and its condition in effecting is that the condition is that on which the effect of the effector depends, not its essence; like the dryness of wood, for it is a condition for burning, because fire does not effect burning in wood except after it is dry. The part is that on which its essence depends, i.e., the essence of the effector; so the effect also depends on it, but not primarily, rather through its dependence on the essence which depends on its part. The absence of an obstacle is not something on which the effect depends such that it would share in that with the condition, since you already know that the absence of an obstacle is a revealer of a positive condition on which the effector's effect depends, like the removal of clouds which reveals the appearance of the sun, which is its condition for drying clothes. And counting the absence of an obstacle among the conditions on which effect depends is a kind of metaphor; because you know that non-existence has no entry whatsoever into existence, so that it could be counted as a condition in reality, rather it is a revealer of what is a condition, so its name is applied to it and its ruling is attributed to it.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage provides a definitional and classificatory distinction between a part (juz’) of an efficient cause and a condition (shart) of that cause's effect. The author states that a condition is something on which the effect of the effector depends, but not the effector's essence. An example is the dryness of wood: fire (the effector) only burns wood when it is dry; dryness is a condition, not a part of the fire. A part, by contrast, is something on which the essence of the effector itself depends; for example, the wood's being a log might be a part of the fire? Actually the text says: 'the part is that on which its essence depends', meaning the effector's existence depends on its parts. The effect of the effector also depends on the part, but indirectly, because the effector's existence depends on the part, and the effect depends on the effector's existence. Then the author clarifies that the absence of an obstacle (adam al-māni‘) is not a real condition; it is merely a revealer (kāshif) of a positive condition. For instance, the removal of clouds reveals the sun's light, which is the actual condition for drying clothes. So calling the absence of an obstacle a 'condition' is metaphorical, because non-existence cannot truly be a condition for existence. This guards against a common mistake: treating privations as genuine causal factors.

Key Terms
جزء العلة
شرط
عدم المانع
كاشف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the absence of an obstacle is a genuine condition for causation, similar to a positive condition.
Correction: The passage clarifies that absence of obstacle is only a revealer of a positive condition, and it is called a condition metaphorically because non-existence cannot truly cause existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the absence of an obstacle considered a metaphorical condition and not a real one?
A) Because absence of an obstacle is a part of the cause.
B) Because non-existence cannot have a real role in causing existence.
C) Because the condition is always a positive entity.
D) Because the effector does not depend on it.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between part and condition hinges on whether the dependence is on the cause's essence (part) or on its effect (condition); absence of obstacle is merely a revealer, not a real condition.

Cause and Effect in the Context of States: Al-Amidi's Dictum
Al-Amidi's methodological note on refuting states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Amidi argued that refuting the theory of states eliminates the need to discuss cause and effect in that framework, and the logical relationship between foundational theories and their derived rulings.

Core Idea
If the foundation is false, the superstructure collapses.

This brief but powerful passage from al-Iji's work introduces the tenth section with a striking claim from al-Amidi: if you disprove the existence of states (ahwal), then all discussions that depend on that theory become pointless. But why does al-Iji still devote a section to it? This lesson unpacks al-Amidi's logic and the broader methodological principle at work.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, noting that it ends abruptly with 'except'. Then read the translation to grasp the meaning.
The passage is incomplete; treat it as the opening of a larger argument.
2
Understand al-Amidi's principle
Al-Amidi states that refuting the state theory (i.e., proving states do not exist) makes it unnecessary to examine matters that depend on states or branch from them. This is a logical priority: foundational issues first.
This principle applies broadly in kalam: if a core concept is invalid, its derivatives are moot.
3
Question why the discussion continues
Despite this dictum, al-Iji includes a full section on cause and effect according to affirmers of states. Consider possible reasons: to explain the theory before refuting it, or to show that some aspects remain relevant even after refutation.
The cut-off 'except' hints at a qualification or exception that will appear on the next page.

Distinction Between Part of a Cause and Its Condition
Al-Jurjani defines and distinguishes between a part of a cause and a condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise difference between a part of a cause and a condition, and why the absence of an obstacle is not a genuine condition but a revealer.

Core Idea
A condition affects the effect; a part affects the essence; absence of obstacle reveals a condition.

In this passage, al-Jurjani clarifies two key concepts in causation: the part of the cause and the condition. While both are needed for an effect to occur, they operate at different levels—one on the agent's essence, the other on the effect's reliance. He also warns against mistaking the absence of an obstacle as a real condition, explaining it as merely a revealer.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of part and condition
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Notice how al-Jurjani defines each term with precision.
The definitions are the foundation of the distinction.
2
Study the example of fire and wood
Analyze the example: dryness of wood is a condition for burning, not a part of fire's essence. Understand why it is a condition.
3
Contrast part and condition
Contrast: the part is what the agent's essence depends on, so the effect depends on it indirectly; the condition is what the effect directly depends on.
4
Understand the absence of an obstacle
Read why the absence of an obstacle is not a real condition. Note the example of clouds and sun.
Absence reveals a positive condition; it contributes nothing to existence.
5
Reflect on metaphorical usage
Consider why calling the absence of an obstacle a 'condition' is metaphorical (tajawwuz). Recognize the principle: non-existence has no causal role.
This guards against a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرق بين جزء العلة المؤثرة وشرطها في التأثير هو أن الشرط يتوقف عليه تأثير المؤثر لا ذاته، كيبوسة الحطب، فإنها شرط للإحراق، إذ النار لا تؤثر في الحطب بالإحراق إلا بعد أن يكون يابساً. والجزء ما يتوقف عليه ذاته، أي ذات المؤثر فيتوقف أيضاً عليه تأثيره لكن لا ابتداء، بل بواسطة توقفه على ذاته المتوقف على جزئه. وعدم المانع ليس مما يتوقف عليه التأثير حتى يشارك الشرط في ذلك، إذ قد علمت أنه أي عدم المانع كاشف عن شرط وجودي يتوقف عليه تأثير المؤثر، كزوال الغيم الكاشف عن ظهور الشمس، الذي هو شرطها في تجفيف الثياب. وعده أي عد عدم المانع من جملة الشروط التي يتوقف عليها التأثير نوع من التجوز؛ لما عرفت من أن العدم لا مدخل له أصلاً في الوجود، حتى يعد شرطاً حقيقة، بل هو كاشف عما هو شرط فأطلق اسمه عليه، ونسب حكمه إليه.
The difference between the effective part of the cause (juzʾ al-ʿilla al-muʾaththira) and its condition (sharṭ) in influencing is that the condition is what the effect of the agent depends on, not the agent's essence itself, like the dryness of wood, for it is a condition for burning, since fire does not affect wood by burning except after it is dry. The part is what the essence itself depends on, i.e., the essence of the agent, so its effect also depends on it, but not directly, rather through its dependence on the essence that depends on its part. And the absence of an obstacle (ʿadam al-māniʿ) is not among what the effect depends on such that it would share in being a condition, for you already know that the absence of an obstacle is a revealer of a positive condition on which the effect of the agent depends, like the removal of clouds revealing the appearance of the sun, which is its condition for drying clothes. Counting the absence of an obstacle among the conditions on which the effect depends is a kind of metaphorical usage (tajawwuz), because you have known that non-existence has no role whatsoever in existence, such that it would be counted as a condition in reality; rather, it is a revealer of what is a condition, so its name is applied to it and its ruling is attributed to it.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a definition and distinction between two concepts: the part of a cause (juzʾ al-ʿilla) and the condition (sharṭ). It first clarifies that a condition is something on which the effect depends, not the agent's own essence. The example is dryness of wood: fire can only burn wood if it is dry. A part, by contrast, is something on which the agent's essence itself depends, and thus the effect depends on it indirectly. The passage then addresses the absence of an obstacle (ʿadam al-māniʿ). It argues that the absence of an obstacle is not a genuine condition because non-existence contributes nothing positively; rather, it is a 'revealer' (kāshif) of a positive condition that is already present. For instance, the removal of clouds reveals the sun, which is the actual condition for drying clothes. Calling the absence of an obstacle a condition is a metaphorical extension (tajawwuz), since non-existence (ʿadam) has no real causal role.

Correction: It is merely a revealer of a positive condition; non-existence cannot be a causal factor.

C) Both are the same.

Key Takeaway
The part is integral to the agent's essence, the condition is required for the effect, and the absence of an obstacle is only a revealer of a positive condition.

Definition of Cause According to Proponents of أحوال
Defines cause according to proponents of أحوال and explains confinement of its effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the proponents of states (aḥwāl) define cause and why its effect is confined to the locus of the attribute.

Core Idea
A cause in kalām is a meaning that necessitates a judgment only in its own locus.

This passage introduces a key concept in the debate over causality in kalām: the definition of cause according to those who affirm states (aḥwāl). It distinguishes between the cause's effect on its locus and its lack of effect on the object of the attribute. Understanding this confinement is essential to grasp later arguments about divine attributes and their effects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get an overview of the definition and its implications.
Notice the contrast with the deniers of states.
2
Identify the definition of cause
Find how the proponents of states define cause: as a meaning (maʿnā) that necessitates a judgment (ḥukm) for its locus (maḥall).
The passage says: 'الأحوال يقولون بالمعاني الموجبة للأحكام في محالها وهي عندهم علل تلك الأحكام'
3
Understand the confinement of the judgment
The phrase 'لمحلها' (for its locus) indicates that the judgment does not extend beyond the locus. So knowledge does not give a judgment to the known object.
The passage explains why: because the attribute is not subsistent in the object.
4
Consider the objection about non-existent
If the judgment extended, then a non-existent object of knowledge would acquire a positive attribute, which is impossible.
This reinforces the confinement.
5
Define effect accordingly
The effect is the judgment that the attribute necessitates in its locus.
The passage concludes: 'فالمعلول هو الحكم الذي توجبه الصفة في محلها'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد العاشر : في بيان العلة والمعلول

الأحوال يقولون بالمعاني الموجبة للأحكام في محالها وهي عندهم علل تلك الأحكام، وإيجابها إياها لا يتوقف على شرط، كما سيأتي. ونفاة الأحوال من الأشاعرة لا يقولون بالعلة والمعلول أصلاً، فإن الموجودات بأسرها عندهم مستندة إلى الله تعالى ابتداء بلا وجوب ومثبتو الأحوال منهم يوافقونهم في هذا. (و) قوله (لمحلها يشعر بأن حكم الصفة لا يتعدى المحل) أي محل تلك الصفة، ( فلا يوجب العلم والقدرة والإرادة، للمعلوم والمقدور والمراد (حكماً لأنها غير قائمة بها، كيف ولو أوجبت لها أحكاماً لكان المعدوم الممتنع مثلاً إذا تعلق به العلم متصفاً بحكم ثبوتي، وهو محال. وعلى هذا التعريف الذي ذكر للعلة فالمعلول هو الحكم الذي توجبه الصفة في محلها.
The Fifth Observing Post - The Tenth Purpose: On the Explanation of Cause and Effect. The proponents of states (aḥwāl) say that the meanings (maʿānī) that necessitate judgments (aḥkām) for their loci are, according to them, the causes (ʿilal) of those judgments, and their necessitation of them does not depend on a condition, as will come. And the deniers of states from the Ashʿarites do not affirm cause and effect at all, for all existents are according to them dependent on God Almighty from the beginning without necessity, and the affirmers of states among them agree with them in this. And His statement: 'for its locus' indicates that the judgment of the attribute does not extend beyond the locus, meaning the locus of that attribute, so knowledge, power, and will do not necessitate a judgment for the known, the powerful, and the willed because they (the attributes) are not subsistent in them; how so, and if they necessitated a judgment for them, then the non-existent impossible thing, for example, would be described, when knowledge is attached to it, by a positive judgment, and that is impossible. And on this definition mentioned for the cause, the effect (maʿlūl) is the judgment that the attribute necessitates in its locus.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage defines the cause (ʿilla) according to the proponents of states (aḥwāl) in kalām. It begins by stating that these proponents consider the meanings (maʿānī)—i.e., attributes like knowledge and power—that necessitate judgments (aḥkām) for their loci (maḥall) to be the causes of those judgments. Their necessitation does not depend on any condition. The passage then contrasts them with the deniers of states among the Ashʿarites, who deny causality altogether, holding that all existents depend directly on God without any necessitation. The affirmers of states agree with the deniers on this point regarding existents, but they affirm causality for the states themselves, which are not existents. Next, the passage explains the phrase 'for its locus' (limaḥallihā) in the definition. This phrase shows that the judgment of an attribute is confined to its own locus—for example, the judgment of 'being knowing' (ʿālimiyya) is attributed only to the subject in whom knowledge subsists, not to the object known. Therefore, attributes like knowledge, power, and will do not impose any judgment on the objects (the known, the powerful, the willed) because these objects are not the loci of those attributes. If they did impose a judgment, then a non-existent impossible thing, when known, would acquire a positive attribute, which is impossible because non-existence cannot bear positive attributes. Finally, the passage concludes that according to this definition, the effect (maʿlūl) is precisely the judgment that the attribute necessitates in its locus.

أحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the cause's effect extends to the object of the attribute.
Correction: The judgment is confined to the locus; for example, knowledge does not give a judgment to the known object because the object is not the locus of the attribute.

Checkpoint

C) Because knowledge is not a real attribute.
D) Because the known object already has its own judgment.

Key Takeaway
The cause, as defined by proponents of states, is a meaning that necessitates a judgment only within its locus; the effect is that judgment.

Refutation of the First Definition of Cause
Refutes the first definition of cause with three objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of cause as 'what necessitates its effect immediately after it, if no obstacle prevents' fails due to circularity, temporal issues, and the problem of obstacles.

Core Idea
Defining cause by effect leads to circular reasoning.

This lesson tackles the first of two definitions of cause, which tries to define cause through temporal succession and the absence of obstacles. The refutation shows that this definition is not only circular but also internally inconsistent. Let's examine the logical flaws step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the first definition and its refutation
Read the Arabic passage that presents the first definition and the initial circularity objection. Then read the translation.
Notice the term 'معلول' (effect) is derived from 'علة' (cause).
2
Understand the circularity argument
The passage argues that 'معلول' means 'that which has a cause', so knowing the effect requires knowing the cause first. This makes the definition circular.
3
Analyze the temporal objection
The passage presents a dilemma: if the cause necessitates at the first moment, 'immediately after' is invalid; if at the second moment, absurdities follow like knowledge subsisting in a non-knowing person.
Consider why temporal succession cannot define causation.
4
Examine the obstacle condition
The condition 'if no obstacle prevents' is rejected because some causes, like knowledge for being knowledgeable, necessitate without any possible obstacle.
The passage mentions that later it will be shown that causation is unconditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما نحو قولهم : العلة ما توجب معلولها عقيبها بالاتصال ( إذا لم يمنع منه مانع ( أو ) العلة ( ما كان المعتل به معللا وهو) أي كون المعتل ) معللا به (قوله) أي قول القائل : ( كذا لأجل كذا كقولنا كانت العالمية لأجل العلم ( فدوري). أما الأول، فلأن المعلول مشتق من العلة إذ معناه ماله علة، فتتوقف معرفته على معرفتها، فلزم الدور. ويتجه عليه أيضاً : أن العلة إن أوجبت معلولها في أول زمان وجودها فلا يصح اعتبار التعقيب في تعريفها وإن لم توجبه إلا في الوقت الثاني من وجودها لزم منه أن يقوم العلم بشخص مثلاً، وهو غير عالم بعد، وأيضاً اعتبار عدم المانع باطل، فإن إيجاب العلم للعالمية لا يتصور فيه تخلف وممانعة، وسيأتي أن إيجاب العلة لا يكون مشروطاً بشرط اتفاقاً وأما الثاني فلأنه عرف العلة بالمعتل والمعلل، ومعرفة كل منهما موقوفة على معرفة العلة فالدور لازم. وفيه أيضاً فساد آخر، هو رد العلية إلى القول أعني ما يقال كان كذا لأجل كذا، ولا شك أنه ليس.
And as for statements like theirs: 'Cause is what necessitates its effect immediately after it by connection (if no obstacle prevents),' or 'Cause is that by which the thing caused is made what it is' (i.e., the being of the thing caused as made what it is), and the statement of the speaker: 'such and such for such and such' like our saying: 'being knowing was for the sake of knowledge' — (so it is circular). As for the first, because the effect (maʿlūl) is derived from cause (ʿilla), since its meaning is 'that which has a cause,' so knowledge of it depends on knowledge of the cause, thus circularity is necessary. And it is also directed against it: that if the cause necessitates its effect at the first moment of its existence, then the consideration of temporal succession (taʿqīb) in its definition is not valid; and if it does not necessitate it except in the second moment of its existence, then it would follow that knowledge subsists in a person, for example, while he is not yet knowing. Also, the consideration of the absence of an obstacle is invalid, for the necessitation of knowledge for being knowledgeable cannot be imagined to be subject to delay and obstruction; and it will come that the necessitation of the cause is not conditioned by a condition by agreement. And as for the second, because he defined the cause by the thing caused (muʿattal) and the thing made what it is (muʿallal), and knowledge of each of them depends on knowledge of the cause, so circularity is necessary. And in it is also another corruption: it reduces causality to a statement (al-qawl), namely 'one says: such and such for such and such,' and there is no doubt that it is not (a proper definition).
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage refutes two alternative definitions of cause (ʿilla) and demonstrates their flaws. The first definition states: 'Cause is what necessitates its effect immediately after it by connection, if no obstacle prevents.' The second definition states: 'Cause is that by which the thing caused is made what it is,' and it is exemplified by the statement 'such and such for such and such,' like saying 'being knowing was for the sake of knowledge.' The passage argues that both definitions are circular (dawrī). For the first definition: the term 'effect' (maʿlūl) is derived from 'cause' (ʿilla) meaning 'that which has a cause,' so understanding what an effect is requires prior knowledge of the cause, leading to circularity. Additionally, two further objections are raised: First, if the cause necessitates its effect at the very first moment of its existence, then the condition of 'immediately after' (ʿuqaybahā) is invalid because there is no temporal gap to speak of. If the cause necessitates only at the second moment, then it would be possible for knowledge to subsist in a person while that person is not yet knowing—an absurdity. Second, the condition 'if no obstacle prevents' is also invalid because the necessitation of knowledge for being knowledgeable (al-ʿālimiyya) cannot be subject to obstacle or delay; it is necessarily concomitant. The passage notes that it will be established later that the necessitation of a cause is not conditioned by any condition at all. For the second definition: it defines cause using the terms 'thing caused' (muʿattal) and 'made what it is' (muʿallal), both of which are understood only through knowledge of the cause, thus circularity again. Moreover, a second corruption is that this definition reduces causality to a mere statement (al-qawl)—that is, it treats causality as linguistic usage rather than a real relation—which is clearly insufficient.

Key Terms
علة (cause)
معلول (effect)
دور (circularity)
تعقيب (temporal succession)
مانع (obstacle)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Defining cause as 'what necessitates its effect immediately after it' seems intuitive, but it is logically flawed.
Correction: This definition leads to circularity and absurdities; causation is immediate and unconditional.

Checkpoint
What is the first objection to the definition 'العلة ما توجب معلولها عقيبها بالاتصال'?
A) It is too broad.
B) It leads to circularity because 'معلول' is derived from 'علة'.
C) It does not account for the condition 'if no obstacle'.
D) It applies only to temporal causes.

Key Takeaway
The first definition of cause fails because it relies on the effect to define cause, creating a circle, and its temporal and obstacle conditions lead to logical absurdities.

Refutation of the Second Definition of Cause
Refutes the second definition of cause as circular and reductionist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining cause as 'that by which the thing caused is made what it is' is circular and reduces causality to mere linguistic statement.

Core Idea
Defining cause by its effect is circular and reduces causality to speech.

The second definition attempts to define cause in terms of its effect and the phrase 'for the sake of'. This lesson shows that this definition is also circular and fails to capture the real nature of causality. Let's see how the author dismantles it.

Guided Path
1
Read the second definition and refutation
Read the Arabic passage that presents the second definition and the circularity argument. Then read the translation.
The definition uses terms 'معلل' and 'معتل'.
2
Understand the circularity argument
The definition uses 'muʿattal' (thing caused) and 'muʿallal' (made what it is), both of which depend on the concept of cause.
3
Identify the second corruption
The definition reduces causality to a statement like 'such and such for such and such', which treats causation as a linguistic expression rather than a real relation.
This is insufficient because causality is an ontological relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما نحو قولهم : العلة ما توجب معلولها عقيبها بالاتصال ( إذا لم يمنع منه مانع ( أو ) العلة ( ما كان المعتل به معللا وهو) أي كون المعتل ) معللا به (قوله) أي قول القائل : ( كذا لأجل كذا كقولنا كانت العالمية لأجل العلم ( فدوري). أما الأول، فلأن المعلول مشتق من العلة إذ معناه ماله علة، فتتوقف معرفته على معرفتها، فلزم الدور. ويتجه عليه أيضاً : أن العلة إن أوجبت معلولها في أول زمان وجودها فلا يصح اعتبار التعقيب في تعريفها وإن لم توجبه إلا في الوقت الثاني من وجودها لزم منه أن يقوم العلم بشخص مثلاً، وهو غير عالم بعد، وأيضاً اعتبار عدم المانع باطل، فإن إيجاب العلم للعالمية لا يتصور فيه تخلف وممانعة، وسيأتي أن إيجاب العلة لا يكون مشروطاً بشرط اتفاقاً وأما الثاني فلأنه عرف العلة بالمعتل والمعلل، ومعرفة كل منهما موقوفة على معرفة العلة فالدور لازم. وفيه أيضاً فساد آخر، هو رد العلية إلى القول أعني ما يقال كان كذا لأجل كذا، ولا شك أنه ليس.
And as for statements like theirs: 'Cause is what necessitates its effect immediately after it by connection (if no obstacle prevents),' or 'Cause is that by which the thing caused is made what it is' (i.e., the being of the thing caused as made what it is), and the statement of the speaker: 'such and such for such and such' like our saying: 'being knowing was for the sake of knowledge' — (so it is circular). As for the first, because the effect (maʿlūl) is derived from cause (ʿilla), since its meaning is 'that which has a cause,' so knowledge of it depends on knowledge of the cause, thus circularity is necessary. Also, the consideration of the absence of an obstacle is invalid, for the necessitation of knowledge for being knowledgeable cannot be imagined to be subject to delay and obstruction; and it will come that the necessitation of the cause is not conditioned by a condition by agreement. And as for the second, because he defined the cause by the thing caused (muʿattal) and the thing made what it is (muʿallal), and knowledge of each of them depends on knowledge of the cause, so circularity is necessary. Commentary
This passage refutes two alternative definitions of cause (ʿilla) and demonstrates their flaws. The first definition states: 'Cause is what necessitates its effect immediately after it by connection, if no obstacle prevents.' The commentator identifies a further corruption in the second definition of cause.

Learning Objective

Core Idea

The commentator continues his critique of the second definition of cause by pointing out another fatal flaw: it reduces causality to a verbal statement. Instead of capturing the real ontological connection between cause and effect, the definition treats causality as nothing more than saying 'such happened because of such.' Transitivity of the Cause's Ruling
The commentator presents the majority Ash'ari view on the transitivity of cause and the objection of al-Ustadh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari view on the transitivity of the cause's ruling and the objection raised by al-Ustadh.

Core Idea
Ash'aris hold that a cause can be external to its effect's locus.

This short passage presents a key disagreement among theologians: does the ruling of a cause necessarily reside within the same subject? The majority of Ash'aris say no, while al-Ustadh (Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini) denied it. Let's examine the text to see how this principle is articulated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the concise style and the contrast between the majority view and the denial.
The passage is brief but contains a complete doctrinal position.
2
Identify the two positions
Locate the majority statement ('most of our companions said') and the objection ('al-Ustadh denied it'). Understand what each side affirms or denies.
3
Consider an example
Think of God's power as a cause: it brings about effects in creation without being located in those effects. This illustrates the transitivity of the ruling.
This example is not in the text but helps clarify the abstract concept.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية : قال أكثر أصحابنا : حكم العلة يتعدى محلها أي تكون العلة خارجة عن المحل الذي أوجبت له الحكم ، وأنكره الأستاذ.
The Second Issue: Most of our companions said: The ruling of the cause transcends its locus, meaning the cause is external to the locus for which it necessitated the ruling. And al-Ustadh denied it.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This short passage introduces the second discussable issue regarding causality: whether the ruling of a cause extends beyond its own locus. The majority of Ash'ari theologians (asḥābunā) affirm that the cause is external to the locus it affects. For example, God's knowledge (cause) results in His being knowing (effect), but the cause is not located in the effect's locus. Al-Ustadh (probably Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāʾīnī) rejected this view, maintaining that the cause must be within the locus. The passage sets up the disagreement but does not elaborate further here.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that a cause must always be located within the same subject as its effect.
Correction: The Ash'ari view presented here holds that the cause can be external to the locus of its effect. For example, God's power is the cause of actions in creatures, but it is not located in them.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari view allows God's attributes to cause effects in creation without being subject to spatial constraints.

Definitions of Cause and Effect in Kalam
Al-Jurjani defines cause and effect with exclusions and derivations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of 'illa (cause) and ma'lul (effect) in kalam, including the key exclusions and how each definition leads to a corresponding definition of effect.

Core Idea
Cause changes or renews a ruling; effect is what the cause necessitates.

Al-Jurjani begins by laying out two definitions of 'illa (cause) that are central to kalam discussions on causality. He then shows what these definitions exclude—eternal attributes and first-moment attributes—before deriving parallel definitions of ma'lul (effect). This passage gives you the basic vocabulary for reasoning about cause and effect in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: definitions, exclusions, then derived definitions.
The passage starts with the title and then immediately gives two definitions of cause.
2
Identify the two definitions of cause
Find the two definitions: (1) 'that which changes the ruling of its locus' and (2) 'that by which the ruling is renewed'. Understand the difference: change vs. renewal.
The first focuses on transformation, the second on temporal renewal.
3
Understand the exclusions
See why eternal attributes (like God's knowledge) and first-time attributes (like blackness of pitch) are excluded from the first definition. This clarifies that the definitions are tailored to temporal, changing loci.
The text says the first definition excludes 'the attributes that come into existence at the first moment' because there is no prior ruling to change.
4
Derive the definitions of effect
From each definition of cause, the author derives a corresponding definition of ma'lul. List them: (1) what the cause necessitates immediately, (2) the thing caused, (3) a ruling changed by the cause, (4) a ruling renewed by the cause.
Notice the phrase 'fictitious definitions'—these are preliminary and may be refined later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد العاشر : في بيان العلة والمعلول

معنى العلية . (و) قولهم العلة ( ما تغير حكم محلها أي تنقله من حال إلى حال ( أو ) العلة هي ( التي يتجدد بها ) أي بتجددها ( الحكم ، يخرج الصفة القديمة )، إذ لا تغير ولا تجدد فيها مع أنها من قبيل العلل فإن علمه تعالى علة موجبة لعالميته عندهم، ويخرج أيضا عن الأول الصفات الحادثة في أول زمان حدوث محلها، كسواد القار مثلاً، فإنه يوجب لمحله حكماً هو الأسودية، وليس فيه تغيير حكم المحل، إذ لا حكم له قبل ذلك، لكونه معدوماً . ولك أن تأخذ من كل واحد من هذه التعريفات المزيفة للعلة تعريفاً للمعلول، فنقول : المعلول ما أوجبته العلة عقيبها بالاتصال إذا لم يمنع مانع، أو المعتل المعلل بالعلة، أو ما كان من الأحكام متغيراً بالعلة، أو ما يتجدد من الأحكام بالعلة
The Fifth Observatory - The Tenth Objective: On the explanation of cause and effect.
Meaning of causality. (And) their saying: the cause is (that which changes the ruling of its locus, i.e., transfers it from one state to another) or (the cause is that by which the ruling is renewed, i.e., by its renewal). It excludes the eternal attribute, as there is no change or renewal in it, even though it is among the types of causes, for His knowledge, exalted, is a cause necessitating His being omniscient according to them. Also excluded by the first [definition] are the attributes that come into existence at the first moment of the existence of their locus, such as the blackness of pitch, for it necessitates for its locus a ruling which is blackness, and there is no change in the ruling of the locus, since there is no ruling before that, due to its being non-existent. And you may derive from each of these fictitious definitions of cause a definition for effect, and we say: the effect is what the cause necessitates after it by connection if no impediment prevents, or the thing affected that is caused by the cause, or that which is of the rulings that are changed by the cause, or that which is renewed of the rulings by the cause.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage defines the terms ‘illa (cause) and ma‘lūl (effect) in kalam. The author gives two main definitions of cause. The first: 'that which changes the ruling of its locus' meaning it transfers the locus from one state to another. The second: 'that by which the ruling is renewed' meaning when the cause is renewed, the ruling is renewed. These definitions exclude the eternal attribute (e.g., God's knowledge) because there is no change or renewal in it, yet it still functions as a cause in necessitating omniscience from their perspective (i.e., the Ash‘aris). They also exclude accidental attributes that come into existence at the first moment of their locus, like the blackness of pitch: the blackness causes its locus to be black, but there is no change because the locus had no prior ruling. From these definitions, one can derive corresponding definitions for ma‘lūl: (1) what the cause necessitates immediately unless prevented by an impediment, (2) the thing that is caused, (3) a ruling that is changed by the cause, (4) a ruling that is renewed by the cause. The author notes these definitions are 'fictitious' (muzayyafa) likely meaning they are preliminary or subject to later refinement.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
حكم
محل
تغير
تجدد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People may think that a cause must involve change or renewal, so eternal attributes cannot be causes.
Correction: Eternal attributes like God's knowledge are causes (they necessitate effects) but without change or renewal; the definitions of cause given here are for temporal loci.

Checkpoint
Why does the blackness of pitch not fit the first definition of cause?
A) Because it is not a cause.
B) Because there is no prior ruling to change.
C) Because it is an eternal attribute.
D) Because it involves renewal.

Key Takeaway
Cause is defined by change or renewal of a ruling, and effect is what the cause necessitates, with exclusions for eternal attributes and first-time attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Does the Ruling of a Cause Extend Beyond Its Locus?
Al-Jurjani relates the view of most Ash‘aris that the ruling of a cause extends beyond its locus, and the denial by al-Ustādh, with explanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dispute among theologians over whether the ruling of a cause extends beyond its locus, and how different interpretations affect the positions of various schools.

Core Idea
The extension of a cause's ruling depends on how you define 'locus' and 'separation'.

This passage from al-Jurjani's commentary on al-Mawaqif deals with a second issue in causality: whether the ruling of a cause extends beyond its locus. The majority of Ash'aris affirm this, while al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq denies it. However, the phrasing hides further complexity about the nature of the cause and the interpretation of key terms. In this lesson, we'll unpack the dispute and the subtle arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the technical terms like تعدي, علة, محل.
2
Identify the three positions mentioned
The passage presents the view of the majority of Ash'aris, the denial of al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq, and the problematic reference to the Basrian Mu'tazila. Find each one and note how they are characterised.
The Basrian position is not directly stated but appears in the critique.
3
Analyse the interpretation problem
The author shows that a literal reading of 'لا يتعدى محلها' leads to a contradiction regarding the Basrians. Understand why and how the commentator's interpretation resolves it.
This connects to the broader theological debate about the nature of attributes and states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية : قال أكثر أصحابنا : حكم العلة يتعدى محلها أي تكون العلة خارجة عن المحل الذي أوجبت له الحكم ، وأنكره الأستاذ أبو إسحاق ولم يشترط قيام العلة بمحل حكمها تفريعاً على القول بالحال، وإن أنكره عنه. والمقصود أن ثبوت الحكم يستلزم ثبوت العلة ولا يجوز خروجها عنه رداً على القائلين بجواز ثبوت الحكم بدون ثبوت العلة كما سيجيء فسره الشارح قدس سره بما هو المقصود، وأشار إلى أن المراد بقوله لا يتعدى محلها أنه لا يفارقه لاستلزامه له وكونه مشروطاً به، وما قيل: إنما فسر بهذا لأن المتبادر منه أن يكون للعلة محل البتة. ويكون الخلاف في أن حكمها هل يتعدى محلها أو لا، فلا يصح قوله : وأنكر البصريون من المعتزلة لأن الإرادة التي هي العلة ليست في محل عندهم، وأما على تفسيره فيصح ذلك القول؛ لأن الإرادة خارجة عن المحل الذي أو جبت له الحكم به فيرد عليه : أنه على تقدير تسليم كون المتبادر منه ذلك، لا نسلم أنه يصح قوله، وأنكر البصريون فإن إنكار ذلك المجموع يجوز أن يكون بإنكار عدم التعدي، ويجوز أن يكون بإنكار لزوم المحل، ولولا ذلك لما صح قول الشارح قدس سره : وأنكر البصريون عدم تعدي حكم العلة عن محلها.
The Second Issue: Most of our companions said: The ruling of the cause extends beyond its locus, meaning the cause is external to the locus for which it necessitated the ruling. And al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq denied this and did not require the cause to be established in the locus of its ruling, branching off from the doctrine of the state (al-hal), even though he denied it from him. The intended meaning is that the establishment of the ruling necessitates the establishment of the cause, and it is not permissible for it to exit from it, as a refutation of those who say it is permissible for the ruling to be established without the establishment of the cause, as will come. The commentator (al-sharih) explained it as what is intended, and indicated that the meaning of his statement 'it does not extend beyond its locus' is that it does not separate from it because of its necessitating it and being conditional upon it. And what was said: it was interpreted this way because what is understood from it is that the cause definitely has a locus. And the dispute is whether its ruling extends beyond its locus or not. So his statement 'and the Basrians from the Mu'tazila denied it' is not correct because the will which is the cause does not have a locus according to them. But according to his interpretation, that statement becomes correct because the will is external to the locus for which it necessitated the ruling. So he replies: on the assumption that the understood meaning is that, we do not concede that his statement is correct, and 'the Basrians denied it' because the denial of that whole could be by denying the lack of extension, or by denying the necessity of the locus. Were it not for that, the statement of the commentator would not be correct: 'And the Basrians denied the lack of extension of the ruling of the cause from its locus.'
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents a second issue in the discussion of cause and effect: whether the ruling (hukm) of a cause extends beyond its locus (mahall). The majority of Ash‘aris (asḥābunā) hold that the ruling does extend, meaning the cause itself is external to the locus that receives the ruling. Al-Ustādh Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini denied this extension, though he did not require the cause to be established in the same locus—this is based on his acceptance of the theory of states (ahwāl), but he denied the extension itself. The author then explains the intended meaning: the establishment of a ruling necessitates the establishment of a cause, and the cause cannot separate from that ruling, refuting those who allow a ruling without a cause. The commentator interprets 'does not extend beyond its locus' to mean that the cause does not separate from the ruling because it necessitates it and is conditional for it. The problem arises with how to understand the phrase. If taken literally, it implies the cause must have a locus, and the dispute is about whether the ruling extends beyond that locus. But then the statement 'the Basrian Mu‘tazila denied it' becomes problematic because their view is that the cause (e.g., will) does not have a locus at all. However, if we interpret 'does not extend' as 'does not separate', then the Basrian denial makes sense: they deny that the ruling cannot separate from the cause (i.e., they allow the ruling to be without a cause). The author shows multiple possibilities in the denial, clarifying the nuances of the positions.

علة
حكم
محل
حال
استلزام

معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dispute is simply about whether the cause is inside or outside the locus.
Correction: Actually, it revolves around whether the ruling can exist without the cause, and the interpretation of 'extension' as separation or as physical location. The Basrian position shows the dispute is more nuanced.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that the statement 'the Basrians from the Mu'tazila denied it' is problematic on the literal reading of 'does not extend beyond its locus'?
A) Because the Basrians agree with the Ash'aris on this point.
B) Because the Basrians do not believe the will has a locus, so they cannot be said to deny its extension from a locus.
C) Because the Basrians deny the existence of causes altogether.
D) Because the Basrians believe the ruling extends beyond the locus.

Key Takeaway
The extension of a cause's ruling beyond its locus is a nuanced topic where the precise meaning of terms determines the theological positions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a good outcome in your life, do you habitually trace it back to its apparent cause (e.g., your effort, a person, a natural process) without consciously recognizing that Allah is the true Creator of that cause and its effect? How does this lesson's emphasis on the inseparable connection between cause and ruling challenge your default attribution patterns? Will you commit to pausing after every blessing to say 'Allah is the Cause behind every cause'?
?
This passage reveals a subtle nuance: a cause can be external to its effect's locus. In your spiritual life, do you sometimes expect that the cause of your 'spiritual ruling' (e.g., increased taqwa) must come from within you—your own efforts—forgetting that Allah's grace is the external cause? How will you realign your reliance from your own abilities to Allah's will as the ultimate cause?
?
The commentator interprets 'does not extend beyond its locus' as 'does not separate'—meaning the ruling is never without a cause. Do you ever feel that a trial you are facing has no purpose or cause from Allah? How does this logical truth that every ruling necessitates a cause drive you to search for the wisdom in His decree with full certainty?
