The Meaning of 'La Yata'adda Mahallaha' and the Basrian Objection
The commentator clarifies the intended meaning of the phrase and defends it against the Basrian objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct interpretation of the phrase 'la yata'adda mahallaha' in the context of causality and how it addresses the Basrian objection.

Core Idea
The ruling is inseparable from its cause; the cause need not have a locus.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies a key phrase in the discussion of causality: 'la yata'adda mahallaha.' The phrase might be taken to mean that the cause has a fixed locus, but the commentator argues it actually means inseparability. This interpretation also helps defend against the Basrian objection that the will (cause) has no locus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First read the Arabic and English to get the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the main claim
Find the statement that the ruling necessitates the cause and is inseparable from it.
This is the basis of the commentator's interpretation.
3
Understand the two interpretations
Note the apparent meaning (cause has a locus) versus the commentator's interpretation (inseparability).
4
Analyze the Basrian objection
See why the Basrians deny the statement and how the commentator's interpretation resolves it.
The Basrians hold that the will has no locus, so the apparent meaning would be problematic.
5
Examine the final clarification
Note the definition of 'outside the locus' and how it avoids a counter-objection from the Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقصود أن ثبوت الحكم يستلزم ثبوت العلة ولا يجوز خروجها عنه رداً على القائلين بجواز ثبوت الحكم بدون ثبوت العلة كما سيجيء فسره الشارح قدس سره بما هو المقصود، وأشار إلى أن المراد بقوله لا يتعدى محلها أنه لا يفارقه لاستلزامه له وكونه مشروطاً به، وما قيل: إنما فسر بهذا لأن المتبادر منه أن يكون للعلة محل البتة. ويكون الخلاف في أن حكمها هل يتعدى محلها أو لا، فلا يصح قوله : وأنكر البصريون من المعتزلة لأن الإرادة التي هي العلة ليست في محل عندهم، وأما على تفسيره فيصح ذلك القول؛ لأن الإرادة خارجة عن المحل الذي أوجبت له الحكم به فيرد عليه : أنه على تقدير تسليم كون المتبادر منه ذلك، لا نسلم أنه يصح قوله، وأنكر البصريون فإن إنكار ذلك المجموع يجوز أن يكون بإنكار عدم التعدي، ويجوز أن يكون بإنكار لزوم المحل، ولولا ذلك لما صح قول الشارح قدس سره : وأنكر البصريون عدم تعدي حكم العلة عن محلها . حينئذ لا
قوله : ( خارجة عن المحل إلخ) أي لا تكون حالة فيه كما هو المتبادر من الخروج عن المحل سواء كانت حالة في جزئه، أو في أمر مباين له أو لا تكون حالة أصلاً، فلا يرد أن العلة لیست خارجة عن المحل عند المعتزلة القائلين بتعدي الحكم في توابع الحياة لكونها حاصلة في جزئه، فلا يتضمن هذا التفسير الرد عليهم .
And the intended meaning is that the affirmation of the ruling necessitates the affirmation of the cause, and it is not permissible for it to be separated from it, in refutation of those who allow the affirmation of a ruling without the affirmation of the cause, as will come. The commentator (may his secret be sanctified) interpreted it as the intended meaning, and indicated that what is meant by his statement 'it does not extend beyond its locus' is that it does not separate from it because of its necessitation of it and its being conditional upon it. And it was said: he only interpreted it this way because the immediate understanding from it is that the cause has a locus at all. And the disagreement is about whether its ruling extends beyond its locus or not. So his statement 'and the Basrians of the Mu'tazila denied it' is not valid, because the will which is the cause is not in a locus according to them. But according to his interpretation, that statement is valid, because the will is outside the locus for which it necessitated the ruling. So it is objected to him: assuming the immediate understanding is that, we do not concede that his statement 'and the Basrians denied' is valid, for the denial of that whole may be by denial of non-extension or by denial of necessity of locus. And were it not for that, the statement of the commentator would not be valid: 'and the Basrians denied the non-extension of the ruling of the cause beyond its locus.' Then not...
His statement: '(outside the locus etc.)' meaning it is not a state in it as is immediately understood from being outside the locus, whether it is a state in a part of it, or in something separate from it, or not a state at all. So it is not objected that the cause is not outside the locus according to the Mu'tazila who affirm the extension of the ruling in the accidents of life because it is present in a part of it, so this interpretation does not entail refuting them.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is an explanation and defense of the commentator's interpretation of the phrase 'لا يتعدى محلها'. It first states that the ruling necessarily depends on the cause and cannot be separated. Then it explains that the phrase means inseparability, not that the cause must have a locus. The apparent meaning might suggest the cause has a locus, but the commentator's interpretation avoids the Basrian objection that the will (cause) has no locus. The passage then considers a counter-objection: if the immediate understanding is that the cause has a locus, then the Basrian denial would be valid? The answer clarifies that the denial of the whole could be either denial of non-extension or denial of necessity of locus, so the commentator's statement remains valid. Finally, it explains that 'outside the locus' means not being a state in it, which can include various possibilities, so it does not conflict with the Mu'tazilite view on the accidents of life (توابع الحياة) where the cause is present in a part of the whole.

Interpreting 'Wa Lam Yashtarit' and Defending the Cause's Locus
Commentary on 'wa lam yashtarit' and defense against objection from accidents of life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's interpretation of 'wa lam yashtarit' and how he defends it against the objection from the accidents of life.

Core Idea
The cause's 'outside the locus' means non-subsistence, not spatial separation.

This passage continues the discussion of whether a cause must subsist in the locus of its ruling. The commentator clarifies the phrase 'wa lam yashtarit' and addresses a key objection from the Mu'tazila regarding the accidents of life, showing that his interpretation holds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the commentator's argument flow before you interpret.
Notice the two parts: the initial explanation and the objection-answer.
2
Identify the two components of the denial
The passage says that the statement 'عدم تعدي حكم العلة عن محلها' contains two things: necessity of the locus and non-extension. Find these and see how the Basrians and Ustadh deny different components.
This is key to understanding the sectarian positions.
3
Understand the objection and answer
The objection from the accidents of life (توابع الحياة) seems to challenge the interpretation. The answer is that 'outside' means non-subsistence, not spatial separation. See how this resolves the objection.
The passage explicitly states: 'المراد بالخروج عدم القيام'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ولم يشترط إلخ) أشار به إلى أن المنقول منه مجرد عدم اشتراط القيام من غير تعيين شيء من الاحتمالات الثلاثة المذكورة.
قوله : أي) لا تكون العلة خارجة عن المحل الذي أوجبت له الحكم) إنما فسر كلام المصنف بهذا؛ لأن المتبادر منه أن يكون للعلة محل البتة، ويكون الخلاف في أن حكمها هل يتعدى محلها أم لا؟ فلا يصح ،قوله وأنكره البصريون من المعتزلة لأن الإرادة التي هي ليست في محل عندهم وأما على تفسيره فيصح ذلك القول لأن الإرادة خارجة عن المحل الذي أوجبت له الحكم. ثم إن ما ذكره الشارح تحرير لمحل النزاع بعبارة ظاهرة في المراد، ولو أردنا تطبيق كلام المصنف عليه قلنا القول بعدم تعدي حكم العلة عن محلها يتضمن بظاهره شيئين، وجوب المحل وعدم التعدي فإنكار المجموع إما بإنكار الأمر الأول وهو قول البصريين وإما بإنكار الثاني، وهو قول الأستاذ وسائر المعتزلة فإن قلت : التفسير المذكور لا يصح إذ يستلزم أن لا يتحقق الخلاف بين الأصحاب والمعتزلة في توابع الحياة، لأنها توجب للمجموع حكماً إذا قامت بجزء منه، لا شك أن العلة ليست بخارجة عن محل الحكم الذي هو المجموع بل متحققة فيه . قلت : المراد بالخروج عدم القيام فيتحقق في الصورة المذكورة أيضاً لأن العلم مثلا ليس بقائم بالمجموع، فلا حاجة إلى ما قيل من أن التفسير المذكور وإن لم يجر بالقياس إلى المجموع، لكن يجري بالقياس إلى الجزء الآخر الذي ثبت له الحكم أيضاً، فإن العلة القائمة بهذا خارجة عن ذلك الجزء الذي أوجبت له الحكم على أن هذا إنما يتم إن ثبت قولهم بثبوت الحكم لكل جزء عند قيام علته لجزء مخصوص، كما قالوا بثبوته للمجموع .
His statement: '(and he did not stipulate etc.)' he indicated by it that what is transmitted from him is just the absence of the condition of subsistence without specifying any of the three mentioned possibilities.
His statement: 'i.e. the cause is not outside the locus for which it necessitated the ruling' he only interpreted the author's statement this way because the immediate understanding is that the cause has a locus at all, and the disagreement is about whether its ruling extends beyond its locus or not. So his statement 'and the Basrians of the Mu'tazila denied it' is not valid, because the will is not in a locus according to them. But according to his interpretation, that statement is valid because the will is outside the locus for which it necessitated the ruling. Then what the commentator mentioned is a rendering of the point of contention in a phrase clear in meaning. And if we wanted to apply the author's statement to it, we would say: the statement denying the extension of the cause's ruling beyond its locus apparently includes two things: necessity of the locus and non-extension. So the denial of the whole is either by denial of the first matter, which is the view of the Basrians, or by denial of the second, which is the view of the Ustadh and the rest of the Mu'tazila. If you say: the mentioned interpretation is not valid because it entails that there is no actual disagreement between the supporters (Ash'aris) and the Mu'tazila regarding the accidents of life, since they necessitate a ruling for the whole when they subsist in a part of it, and there is no doubt that the cause is not outside the locus of the ruling which is the whole, but rather it is realized in it. I say: what is meant by 'outside' is non-subsistence, so it is realized in the mentioned case as well, because knowledge, for example, does not subsist in the whole. So there is no need for what was said that the mentioned interpretation, although it does not apply with respect to the whole, applies with respect to the other part for which the ruling is also established, for the cause subsisting in this part is outside that part for which it necessitated the ruling, provided that this is only complete if their view is established that the ruling is established for each part when its cause subsists in a specific part, as they said its establishment for the whole.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the phrase 'ولم يشترط' and a further defense of the interpretation. First, it notes that the transmitted statement indicates only the absence of condition, not a specific relationship (the three possibilities refer to the earlier discussion of how the cause relates to the locus). Then it repeats the interpretation of 'لا تكون العلة خارجة عن المحل' and restates that the interpretation resolves the Basrian objection. It then analyzes the statement 'عدم تعدي حكم العلة عن محلها' as containing two components: necessity of the locus and non-extension. Thus, the denial of the whole by the Basrians is denial of the first component, while the Ustadh and others deny the second. Then an objection is raised: this interpretation would negate the disagreement regarding the accidents of life (توابع الحياة), because there the cause (e.g., knowledge) subsists in a part of the living being, and its ruling extends to the whole. Since the cause is not outside the whole, the interpretation seems to fail. The answer is that 'outside' means non-subsistence, not spatial separation. Knowledge does not subsist in the whole (it only subsists in the part), so the interpretation still holds. The passage ends by noting that this answer depends on accepting that the ruling applies to each part of the whole, which is the Mu'tazilite view.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'خارجة عن المحل' means the cause is spatially separate from its locus.
Correction: It means the cause does not subsist in the locus of the ruling, as the passage states: 'المراد بالخروج عدم القيام'.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, what does 'خارجة عن المحل' mean?
A) Not subsisting in the locus.
B) Being spatially separate.
C) Not being a state.
D) Being in a different substance.

Key Takeaway
The commentator clarifies that 'outside the locus' means not subsisting, not spatial separation, and this resolves the objection from the accidents of life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever acted as if God's will is confined by the same 'locus' limitations as created causes—demanding that His actions be explainable within your own spatial or material framework? How might this lesson break that assumption?
?
When you attribute your knowledge or ability to yourself, do you mistakenly assume it subsists in your whole being equally? How does the idea that knowledge only subsists in a part (like the heart) reshape your understanding of self-attribution?
?
If the Basrians deny that a cause needs a locus, what does that imply about your daily assumption that every effect must have a physically located cause? Are you ready to accept that God's causing can be without locus?

The Ustadh's Denial of the State
Clarification of the Ustadh's denial of the state.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ustadh's denial of the state and the commentator's concessional approach to it.

Core Idea
The Ustadh's denial of the state is concessional, not absolute.

This passage clarifies a pronoun reference and explains that the Ustadh's denial of the state was made as a concession for the sake of argument. We will see how the commentator handles this delicate point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the pronouns and the term 'الحال'.
The passage is short but packed with technical terms.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage says the Ustadh denied the state, but his statement is 'على سبيل التنزل' (by way of concession). Ask yourself why the commentator says this.
3
Understand the concessional nature
The commentator is granting the affirmation of the state for the sake of argument, not necessarily agreeing with it. This is a common tactic in theological debates.
This helps avoid confusion about the Ustadh's actual position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإن أنكره أي الأستاذ إلخ قيل: إرجاع الضمير المستتر إلى الاستاذ بخصوصه لا
أي الأستاذ الحال وكلامه هاهنا على سبيل التنزل وتسليم ثبوت الحال . (و) أنكر أيضاً ( البصريون من المعتزلة عدم تعدي حكم العلة عن محلها، وجوزوا أن لا تكون العلة قائمة بمحل حكمها، حيث قالوا : الله مريد بإرادة حادثة) ؛ لحدوث المرادات، (قائمة بذاتها ) ، لا بذاته تعالى لاستحالة قيام الحوادث ولا بمحل آخر لاستحالة قيام صفة الشيء بغيره .
قوله : ( أن تكون العلة قائمة إلخ بأن لا يكون لها ،محل كما تدل عليه قائمة بذاتها، وهذا كقولهم في سائر الصفات فإنها قائمة بنفسها لكونها عين الذات، وكقول أفلاطون أن علمه تعالى صور قائمة بذاتها، فما هو غير قائم بذاته في عالم الإمكان قائم بذاته في ضبط الوجوب . قوله : ( الاستحالة قيام الحوادث أي بذاته تعالى دون المتجددات؛ لأن الاتصاف بها انتزاعي، وليس بتحقيقي حتى يلزم من قيامها به حدوث القديم أو قدم الحادث، فلا يرد الإشكال بقيام المريدية المتجددة بذاته تعالى لحدوث الإرادة .
His statement: '(and he denied it, i.e. the Ustadh etc.)' it was said: the returning of the implicit pronoun to the Ustadh in particular is not...
i.e. the Ustadh denied the state, and his statement here is by way of concession and granting the affirmation of the state. (And) also (the Basrians of the Mu'tazila denied the non-extension of the cause's ruling beyond its locus, and allowed that the cause is not subsistent in the locus of its ruling, where they said: God is willing by a created will) due to the creation of the objects of will, (subsistent by itself), not by His essence (may He be exalted) because of the impossibility of accidents subsisting in Him, nor in another locus because of the impossibility of an attribute of a thing subsisting in another.
His statement: '(that the cause is subsistent etc.)' meaning that it has no locus, as indicated by 'subsistent by itself', and this is like their statement regarding the other attributes that they are subsistent by themselves because they are identical with the essence, and like the statement of Plato that His knowledge (exalted) is forms subsistent by themselves. So what is not subsistent by itself in the world of possibility is subsistent by itself in the realm of necessity. His statement: '(the impossibility of accidents subsisting)' i.e. in His essence (exalted) without the renewals; because the attribution to them is mental/extrinsic, not real, such that its subsistence would entail the temporality of the eternal or the eternity of the temporal. So the problem does not arise from the subsistence of the renewing will in His essence due to the creation of the will.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of two related denials: the Ustadh's denial of the state (حال) and the Basrian denial of the necessity that the cause subsist in the locus of its ruling. First, it clarifies the pronoun reference: some say the pronoun in 'أنكره' refers to the Ustadh specifically, but the commentator states that the Ustadh denied the state and that his statement is concessional. Then it quotes the Basrian view: they allow that the cause need not subsist in the locus of its ruling, giving the example of God's will. God is willing by a created will that is subsistent by itself (قائمة بذاتها), not in God's essence (because accidents cannot subsist in God) and not in another locus (because an attribute of one thing cannot subsist in another). The commentary then explains that 'subsistent by itself' means it has no locus, similar to the view of those who say attributes are identical with the essence, or Plato's forms. Finally, it addresses the objection that accidents subsisting in God would entail the temporality of the eternal; the answer is that the attribution of these accidents to God is mental (انتزاعي), not real, so no contradiction arises.

Key Terms
الأستاذ
الحال
التنزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Ustadh absolutely denied the state.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that his denial was concessional; he granted the state for argument's sake.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the commentator say the Ustadh's statement is 'على سبيل التنزل'?
A) Because the Ustadh changed his mind.
B) Because the Ustadh was conceding the existence of the state for the sake of argument.
C) Because the Ustadh explicitly affirmed the state.
D) Because the pronoun reference is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
The Ustadh's denial of the state is a concessional move to facilitate discussion, not a final rejection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself denying an idea in a debate, is it because you truly reject it, or are you making a concessional move to avoid deeper engagement? How can this lesson push you toward greater honesty in your own arguments?
?
Think of a time you conceded a point you didn't believe just to keep a conversation going. Did that help or hinder the pursuit of truth? How does the Ustadh's intentional concession challenge you to examine the motives behind your own concessions?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

The Basrian View on God's Will
Explanation of the Basrian view on God's will as a created attribute subsisting by itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Basrian Mu'tazilite view that God's will is a created attribute subsisting by itself, and why they reject its subsistence in God or another locus.

Core Idea
God's will is a created attribute that subsists by itself, not in God or anything else.

The Basrians of the Mu'tazila held a distinctive view on divine will: it is a created attribute that subsists by itself. This passage explains their reasoning and addresses an objection about accidents subsisting in God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the Basrian claim
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that the Basrians allow the cause (the will) to not subsist in the locus of its ruling. They say God is willing by a created will subsistent by itself.
Key phrase: 'إرادة حادثة قائمة بذاتها'.
2
Understand why not in God nor another locus
The passage gives two reasons: accidents cannot subsist in God (because that would imply change), and an attribute of one thing cannot subsist in another. So the will must be independent.
3
Examine the extended commentary
The commentary analogizes to the view that attributes are identical with the essence (like the Ash'ari view) and Plato's forms. Then it addresses the objection: how can an accident subsist in God? The answer is that the attribution is mental (انتزاعي), not real.
This part defends the Basrian position against the common objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإن أنكره أي الأستاذ إلخ قيل: إرجاع الضمير المستتر إلى الاستاذ بخصوصه لا
أي الأستاذ الحال وكلامه هاهنا على سبيل التنزل وتسليم ثبوت الحال . (و) أنكر أيضاً ( البصريون من المعتزلة عدم تعدي حكم العلة عن محلها، وجوزوا أن لا تكون العلة قائمة بمحل حكمها، حيث قالوا : الله مريد بإرادة حادثة) ؛ لحدوث المرادات، (قائمة بذاتها ) ، لا بذاته تعالى لاستحالة قيام الحوادث ولا بمحل آخر لاستحالة قيام صفة الشيء بغيره .

Why do the Basrians say that God's will is 'قائمة بذاتها'?
A) Because it is identical with God's essence.
B) Because it cannot subsist in God nor in another locus.
C) Because it is a state.
D) Because it is created.

Key Takeaway
The Basrians argue that God's will is a created attribute that subsists by itself, independent of God's essence and any other locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a sudden change in your life, do you instinctively feel that God's will towards you has changed? How does the Basrian view—that God's will is created and subsists by itself without implying change in God—help you see that God's essence remains steadfast even as His will addresses each moment? Write down one specific situation this week where you can consciously remind yourself of this truth.
?
The lesson teaches that the attribution of accidents to God is mental (انتزاعي), not real. Is there an area of your faith where you treat God's attributes as mere intellectual concepts without allowing them to transform your heart? Which attribute—like mercy, justice, or will—do you need to move from mental acknowledgment to heartfelt reliance? Commit to one concrete action that reflects that attribute today.
?
The Basrians argued that God's will is created and independent of His essence. Does this challenge your comfort zone? If you find yourself resisting this view, what does that reveal about your need to control how God works? Are you willing to submit to the mystery of God's transcendence, even when your mind struggles? Pray for humility and ask God to increase your trust in His wisdom beyond your understanding.

Mu'tazilite View on Accidents of Life
Sanusi states the Mu'tazilite view on the extension of accidents of life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite consensus that accidents of life (like knowledge) when present in any part of a living being make the entire being qualified by them, unlike accidents like color.

Core Idea
Accidents of life affect the whole; other accidents stay in their place.

The Mu'tazilites built their theology on rational principles, including specific views on how accidents behave. This passage presents their consensus that the accidents that depend on life, such as knowledge and power, extend their effect to the entire living being. This is contrasted with accidents like color, which remain confined to their specific location. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping Mu'tazilite arguments about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: a general rule followed by an exception.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage distinguishes between 'accidents of life' (knowledge, power, will) and other accidents (colors). Identify how each category is treated.
The rule for accidents of life is extension to the whole; for others, it is specificity to the locus.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask why this distinction matters for theology. The Mu'tazilites used this to argue about God's knowledge and power being identical with His essence, and other attributes.
This principle influences later discussions on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة بأسرهم : (توابع الحياة كالعلم والقدرة ) والإرادة، وسائر ما يشترط في قيامه بمحله الحياة ( إذا قامت بجزء من الحي أوجبت للمجموع حكمها ، فكان المجموع ( عالماً قادراً ) إذا قام العلم والقدرة بجزء واحد من أجزائه ( بخلاف غيرهما أي غير توابع الحياة ( كالألوان ) عند من يثبت لها أحكاماً، فإن حكمها لا يتعدى محلها، بل يختص به واختلفوا في الحياة) هل يتعدى حكمها محلها أو لا ، ( فألحقها الحذاق منهم بالقسم الثاني )، وقالوا : إذا قام الحياة بجزء من شيء كان الحي بها هو ذلك الجزء، لا جملة ذلك الشيء، (فإنها) أي
الضمير يلائمه السياق، لأن المراد بالأصحاب هو الأشاعرة على ما هو الظاهر ، وقوله : تفريعاً على القول بالحال قيد للكل أعني قول أكثر الأصحاب بما ذكر وإنكار الأستاذ له، فالوجه أن يرجع إلى الأكثر، لا الأستاذ على ما وقع في الشرح وأنت خبير بأنه إذا رجع إلى الأكثر يخرج الأستاذ، وقد اعترف بأن قوله : تفريعاً قيد للكل على أنه لا شك أن أكثر الأصحاب يشتمل القائلين بالحال منا، كالقاضي وإمام الحرمين فلا وجه لإرجاع ضمير أنكره إليه إلا بطريق الاستخدام فالأقرب أن يرجع إلى المنكر للحال فتأمل .
قوله : ( بإرادة حادثة لحدوث المرادات وحدوث العلة أعني الإرادة وإن كان يستلزم تجدد المعلول أعني المريدية إلا أنها من قبيل الأحوال، وسيجيء في الإلهيات تجويزهم تجدد الأحوال في ذاته تعالى؛ إذ التجدد راجع إلى التعلقات .
قوله : ( فألحقها الحذاق) إشارة إلى الاستهزاء بهم، فإن دليلهم الذي أشار إليه الشارح محل تعجب واستهزاء كما لا يخفى على من له أدنى مسكة واقتصر على ذكر التسلسل في قوله : وإلا لزم التسلسل مع أنه يحتمل الدور والتسلسل واشتراط الشيء بنفسه، لأنه أخفى فساداً، ولأن التسلسل قد يراد به عدم تناهي التوقفات سواء كانت في مواد متناهية أو غير متناهية، فيشمل الدور والتسلسل المتعارف .
And the Mu'tazila all of them said: the accidents of life like knowledge and power, and will, and all that requires life for its subsistence in its locus (when they subsist in a part of the living being, they necessitate their ruling for the whole, so the whole is knowing, powerful) when knowledge and power subsist in a single part of its parts. (Unlike other than them i.e. other than the accidents of life, like colors) for those who affirm rulings for them, for their ruling does not extend beyond their locus, but is specific to it. And they disagreed concerning life itself) whether its ruling extends beyond its locus or not. (So the intelligent ones among them attached it to the second category) and they said: when life subsists in a part of something, the living being by it is that part, not the whole of that thing. (For it) i.e.
The pronoun fits the context, because what is meant by 'the supporters' is the Ash'aris as is apparent. And his statement: 'as a branch on the view of the state' is a condition for all, i.e. the view of most of the supporters as mentioned and the Ustadh's denial of it. So the correct view is that it refers to the majority, not the Ustadh, as occurred in the commentary. And you are aware that if it refers to the majority, the Ustadh is excluded. And he acknowledged that his statement 'as a branch' is a condition for all, and there is no doubt that 'most of the supporters' includes those among us who affirm the state, like al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn. So there is no justification for returning the pronoun of 'denied it' to him except by way of usage. So the closest is that it refers to the denier of the state, so reflect.
His statement: '(by a created will due to the creation of the objects of will and the creation of the cause i.e. the will, although it entails the renewal of the effect i.e. the attribute of being willing, except that it is of the type of states, and it will come in the divine matters their permitting the renewal of states in His essence (exalted); for renewal pertains to relations.'
His statement: '(so the intelligent ones attached it)' is an indication of mockery of them. For their proof that the commentator pointed to is a source of wonder and mockery, as is not hidden to one who has the least wit. And he confined himself to mentioning the regress in his statement: 'otherwise regress would follow' even though it may involve circularity, regress, and self-conditioning, because it is more hidden in corruption, and because regress may be intended to mean infinite stops whether in finite or infinite matters, so it includes the well-known circularity and regress.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage covers several points. First, it presents the Mu'tazilite consensus that the accidents of life (توابع الحياة) such as knowledge, power, and will, when they subsist in a part of a living being, make the whole living being qualified by them (e.g., the whole is knowing). In contrast, other attributes like color are specific to their locus. Then it mentions a disagreement among Mu'tazilites about life itself: some ('the intelligent ones' – used sarcastically) say life does not extend beyond its locus, so only the part where life subsists is living. The commentary then discusses the pronoun reference in the earlier text: it argues that the pronoun in 'أنكره' refers to the denier of the state (الحال), not the Ustadh, because the context indicates 'most of the supporters' includes Ash'aris who affirm the state. Next, the commentary explains the Basrian view of God's will as a created attribute, noting that the renewal of the effect (the attribute of being willing) is of the type of states (أحوال) and will be discussed later. Finally, the commentator mocks the 'intelligent ones' (الحذاق) for their proof, which relies on a regress argument. He points out that their proof is flawed because it could also involve circularity or self-conditioning, which are even more corrupt; the term 'regress' itself can cover infinite stops, including circularity.

Key Terms
توابع الحياة
العلم
القدرة
الإرادة
الألوان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilite view, what happens when knowledge subsists in a part of a living being?
A) The whole becomes knowing.
B) Only that part is knowing.
C) Nothing changes.
D) The being becomes powerful instead.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites held that accidents of life (like knowledge) affect the entire living being, unlike accidents such as color.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Mu'tazilites are correct that knowledge in a part makes the whole knowing, consider your own knowledge of God. Does your knowledge of God affect your entire being, or is it confined to a part of your life? What would it mean for your whole self to be 'knowing' of God? Are you willing to let that knowledge transform every aspect of your life, or do you keep it segregated?
?
The Mu'tazilites contrast accidents of life with colors that stay in place. Think about your actions: are they like colors that only affect one area, or like accidents of life that spread their effect? What is the 'life' in your soul that spreads goodness to all parts of your character? Are you letting your faith permeate your whole existence, or is it compartmentalized?
?
If the ruling of an accident of life extends from a part to the whole, what does that say about the state of your heart? A single, true recognition of God's greatness—if it is alive—can it affect your entire life? What is the knowledge that resides in a part of you right now, and is it alive enough to transform the whole?

Disagreement on Life and the Sarcastic Remark
Sanusi presents the Mu'tazilite disagreement on life and mocks their proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite disagreement on whether life's ruling extends beyond its locus, and the sarcastic critique of the 'intelligent ones' proof relying on regress.

Core Idea
Life's extension is disputed; the 'intelligent ones' proof is mocked for ignoring circularity.

The Mu'tazilites were not unanimous on all points. While they agreed that accidents of life extend their ruling, they disagreed on life itself. Some held that life, like color, does not extend beyond its locus. This led to a sarcastic remark from the commentator, who criticizes their proof as laughable and flawed. In this lesson, you will read the statement of disagreement and the mocking commentary.

Guided Path
1
Read the disagreement statement
The passage first presents the split: some Mu'tazilites (called 'the intelligent ones' sarcastically) believed life's ruling does not extend beyond its locus, so only the part where life subsists is living.
2
Understand the sarcastic remark
The commentator mocks these 'intelligent ones' and their proof, which relies on a regress argument. The proof is criticized for ignoring that the same reasoning could also involve circularity or self-conditioning.
The commentator says their proof is 'a source of wonder and mockery'.
3
Analyze the proof's flaw
The proof uses a regress argument, but the commentator points out that it could also involve circularity or self-conditioning, which are even more corrupt. The term 'regress' is broad enough to cover all these flaws.
This shows the importance of precise reasoning in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة بأسرهم : (توابع الحياة كالعلم والقدرة ) والإرادة، وسائر ما يشترط في قيامه بمحله الحياة ( إذا قامت بجزء من الحي أوجبت للمجموع حكمها ، فكان المجموع ( عالماً قادراً ) إذا قام العلم والقدرة بجزء واحد من أجزائه ( بخلاف غيرهما أي غير توابع الحياة ( كالألوان ) عند من يثبت لها أحكاماً، فإن حكمها لا يتعدى محلها، بل يختص به واختلفوا في الحياة) هل يتعدى حكمها محلها أو لا ، ( فألحقها الحذاق منهم بالقسم الثاني )، وقالوا : إذا قام الحياة بجزء من شيء كان الحي بها هو ذلك الجزء، لا جملة ذلك الشيء، (فإنها) أي
الضمير يلائمه السياق، لأن المراد بالأصحاب هو الأشاعرة على ما هو الظاهر ، وقوله : تفريعاً على القول بالحال قيد للكل أعني قول أكثر الأصحاب بما ذكر وإنكار الأستاذ له، فالوجه أن يرجع إلى الأكثر، لا الأستاذ على ما وقع في الشرح وأنت خبير بأنه إذا رجع إلى الأكثر يخرج الأستاذ، وقد اعترف بأن قوله : تفريعاً قيد للكل على أنه لا شك أن أكثر الأصحاب يشتمل القائلين بالحال منا، كالقاضي وإمام الحرمين فلا وجه لإرجاع ضمير أنكره إليه إلا بطريق الاستخدام فالأقرب أن يرجع إلى المنكر للحال فتأمل .
قوله : ( بإرادة حادثة لحدوث المرادات وحدوث العلة أعني الإرادة وإن كان يستلزم تجدد المعلول أعني المريدية إلا أنها من قبيل الأحوال، وسيجيء في الإلهيات تجويزهم تجدد الأحوال في ذاته تعالى؛ إذ التجدد راجع إلى التعلقات .
قوله : ( فألحقها الحذاق) إشارة إلى الاستهزاء بهم، فإن دليلهم الذي أشار إليه الشارح محل تعجب واستهزاء كما لا يخفى على من له أدنى مسكة واقتصر على ذكر التسلسل في قوله : وإلا لزم التسلسل مع أنه يحتمل الدور والتسلسل واشتراط الشيء بنفسه، لأنه أخفى فساداً، ولأن التسلسل قد يراد به عدم تناهي التوقفات سواء كانت في مواد متناهية أو غير متناهية، فيشمل الدور والتسلسل المتعارف .
And the Mu'tazila all of them said: the accidents of life like knowledge and power, and will, and all that requires life for its subsistence in its locus (when they subsist in a part of the living being, they necessitate their ruling for the whole, so the whole is knowing, powerful) when knowledge and power subsist in a single part of its parts. (Unlike other than them i.e. other than the accidents of life, like colors) for those who affirm rulings for them, for their ruling does not extend beyond their locus, but is specific to it. And they disagreed concerning life itself) whether its ruling extends beyond its locus or not. (So the intelligent ones among them attached it to the second category) and they said: when life subsists in a part of something, the living being by it is that part, not the whole of that thing. (For it) i.e.
The pronoun fits the context, because what is meant by 'the supporters' is the Ash'aris as is apparent. And his statement: 'as a branch on the view of the state' is a condition for all, i.e. the view of most of the supporters as mentioned and the Ustadh's denial of it. So the correct view is that it refers to the majority, not the Ustadh, as occurred in the commentary. And you are aware that if it refers to the majority, the Ustadh is excluded. And he acknowledged that his statement 'as a branch' is a condition for all, and there is no doubt that 'most of the supporters' includes those among us who affirm the state, like al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn. So there is no justification for returning the pronoun of 'denied it' to him except by way of usage. So the closest is that it refers to the denier of the state, so reflect.
His statement: '(by a created will due to the creation of the objects of will and the creation of the cause i.e. the will, although it entails the renewal of the effect i.e. the attribute of being willing, except that it is of the type of states, and it will come in the divine matters their permitting the renewal of states in His essence (exalted); for renewal pertains to relations.'
His statement: '(so the intelligent ones attached it)' is an indication of mockery of them. For their proof that the commentator pointed to is a source of wonder and mockery, as is not hidden to one who has the least wit. And he confined himself to mentioning the regress in his statement: 'otherwise regress would follow' even though it may involve circularity, regress, and self-conditioning, because it is more hidden in corruption, and because regress may be intended to mean infinite stops whether in finite or infinite matters, so it includes the well-known circularity and regress.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage covers several points. First, it presents the Mu'tazilite consensus that the accidents of life (توابع الحياة) such as knowledge, power, and will, when they subsist in a part of a living being, make the whole living being qualified by them (e.g., the whole is knowing). In contrast, other attributes like color are specific to their locus. Then it mentions a disagreement among Mu'tazilites about life itself: some ('the intelligent ones' – used sarcastically) say life does not extend beyond its locus, so only the part where life subsists is living. The commentary then discusses the pronoun reference in the earlier text: it argues that the pronoun in 'أنكره' refers to the denier of the state (الحال), not the Ustadh, because the context indicates 'most of the supporters' includes Ash'aris who affirm the state. Next, the commentary explains the Basrian view of God's will as a created attribute, noting that the renewal of the effect (the attribute of being willing) is of the type of states (أحوال) and will be discussed later. Finally, the commentator mocks the 'intelligent ones' (الحذاق) for their proof, which relies on a regress argument. He points out that their proof is flawed because it could also involve circularity or self-conditioning, which are even more corrupt; the term 'regress' itself can cover infinite stops, including circularity.

الحذاق
التسلسل
الدور
اشتراط الشيء بنفسه

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the 'intelligent ones' are praised; they are actually mocked.
Correction: The term 'الحذاق' (intelligent ones) is used sarcastically; the commentator clearly mocks their proof.

Checkpoint
What is the commentator's attitude toward the 'intelligent ones'?
A) He praises them.
B) He mocks them.
C) He agrees with them.
D) He ignores them.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites disagreed on life's extension, and their argument was mocked for oversimplifying logical flaws.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological argument you find weak, do you immediately mock its proponents? The commentator sarcastically called them 'intelligent ones.' How does that approach affect your pursuit of truth, and will you commit to examining arguments thoroughly before dismissing them?
?
The 'intelligent ones' thought their proof avoided regress, but overlooked circularity and self-conditioning. Are there areas in your own beliefs where you might be confident in a flawed argument? What step will you take to rigorously examine your own reasoning about faith?

Causality and Created Things

Basrian View on God's Will as a Created Attribute
Sanusi presents the Basrian view on God's will as created and the state of being willing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Basrian Mu'tazilite view that God's will is a created attribute (حادثة) and that the renewal of the effect (being willing) is a state (حال).

Core Idea
God's will is created; the effect renewal is a state, not an attribute in His essence.

The Basrian Mu'tazilites held that God's will is a created accident (حادثة) because its objects (the created things) are created. This leads to a discussion of whether God's attribute of being willing (المريدية) is renewed, and they conclude it is a state (حال) that does not imply change in God's essence. This lesson unpacks that subtle argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the key terms: إرادة حادثة (created will), المريدية (being willing), أحوال (states).
2
Understand the reasoning
The Basrians argue: since the objects of will (مرادات) are created, the will itself must be created. This entails the renewal of the effect (being willing), but that renewal is of the type of states (أحوال), not a change in God's essence.
They distinguish between the attribute of will (a created accident) and the state of being willing (a non-existent entity?).
3
Connect to broader theology
The passage mentions that this topic will be revisited in the section on divine matters (الإلهيات), where they permit the renewal of states in God's essence—though renewal pertains to relations, not essence.
This shows how the concept of states allows them to avoid change in God.

?

?

?

Does this Basrian concept help you see God's immutability while still acknowledging His dynamic interaction with creation? If not, what remains troubling?

Denial of Transitivity by al-Ustadh and Basrian Mu'tazilah
Al-Ustadh and Basrian Mu'tazilah deny the transitivity of the cause's ruling, allowing the cause to subsist in itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ustadh and the Basrian Mu'tazilah denied the transitivity of a cause's ruling and how the example of Allah's will illustrates their position.

Core Idea
A cause's ruling may not extend beyond its locus; even the cause can subsist independently.

This passage introduces two key positions that challenge the principle of transitivity in causal reasoning. Al-Ustadh acceptingly discusses the theory of states, while the Basrian Mu'tazilah go further by denying that a cause's ruling must occupy the same locus as its effect. Their argument using Allah's will shows how a cause can subsist in itself, not in its effect's locus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two distinct parts: al-Ustadh's statement and the Basrian argument.
2
Identify the two positions
Al-Ustadh mentions the state (al-ḥāl) concessively, accepting it for argument. The Basrians explicitly deny that a cause's ruling must apply beyond its locus. Contrast their attitudes.
The Basrians allow the cause not to be in the locus of its ruling.
3
Analyze the Basrian example
They use Allah's will as an example: it is an originated will that subsists in itself, not in Allah's essence or another locus. This shows the cause (will) is independent of its effect (being willing).
Consider why they avoid placing the will in Allah's essence.
4
Reflect on implications
Think about how this challenges the standard view that a cause's ruling extends to its effect. What does this mean for causal theories in kalam?
This denial preserves divine transcendence by avoiding accidents in Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الأستاذ الحال وكلامه هاهنا على سبيل التنزل وتسليم ثبوت الحال . (و) أنكر أيضاً ( البصريون من المعتزلة عدم تعدي حكم العلة عن محلها، وجوزوا أن لا تكون العلة قائمة بمحل حكمها، حيث قالوا : الله مريد بإرادة حادثة)؛ لحدوث المرادات، (قائمة بذاتها )، لا بذاته تعالى لاستحالة قيام الحوادث ولا بمحل آخر لاستحالة قيام صفة الشيء بغيره .
Al-Ustadh said [regarding] the state (al-ḥāl), and his speech here is by way of concession and acceptance of the existence of the state. And also the Basrians among the Mu'tazilah denied the transitivity of the ruling of the cause beyond its locus, and they allowed that the cause may not be subsisting in the locus of its ruling, as they said: Allah is willing (murīd) by an originated will (irāda ḥāditha) because of the origination of the objects of will (murādāt), [the will] subsisting in itself (qāʼim bi dhātihā), not in His essence (dhāt) — due to the impossibility of the subsistence of originated accidents in Him — nor in another locus, due to the impossibility of the subsistence of an attribute of a thing in something else.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents two positions denying the transitivity of a cause's ruling beyond its locus. First, it mentions that al-Ustadh (Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini) speaks here about the theory of states (al-ḥāl) in a concessive manner, accepting its existence for argument's sake. Then it states that the Basrian Mu'tazilah also denied that the ruling of a cause extends beyond its locus, but they went further by allowing that the cause itself might not even subsist in the locus of its ruling. They illustrate this with the case of Allah's will: He is willing by an originated will that subsists in itself, not in His essence (to avoid originated accidents in Him) nor in any other locus (since an attribute of a thing cannot subsist in another). Thus, the cause (the will) is not in the same locus as its effect (the attribute of being willing, which is a state). This establishes that, according to them, a cause can be independent of its effect's locus.

Key Terms
العلة
المعلول
الحال
تعدي الحكم
محل الحكم
قائم بذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Basrian Mu'tazilah denied transitivity entirely, meaning a cause can never affect anything beyond itself.
Correction: They only denied transitivity for the cause's ruling, not for the cause's existence or essence. The cause itself can still be independent, but its ruling (the attribute of being a cause) does not extend to the locus of its effect.

Checkpoint
According to the Basrian Mu'tazilah, why does Allah's will subsist in itself rather than in His essence?
A) Because the will is eternal like His essence
B) Because it is impossible for originated accidents to subsist in His essence
C) Because the will is a necessary attribute
D) Because the will is identical with His essence

Key Takeaway
Al-Ustadh and the Basrian Mu'tazilah both challenge the transitivity of a cause's ruling, but the Basrians go further by allowing the cause to be independent of its effect's locus, as illustrated by Allah's will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that the Basrian Mu'tazilah argued Allah's will is an originated accident subsisting in itself to protect His transcendence, ask yourself: Have I ever unintentionally imagined God as having human-like changes or limitations? Will I now actively correct that mental image with the understanding that originated accidents cannot subsist in His essence?
?
Al-Ustadh made a concessive argument, accepting a position for the sake of debate without fully endorsing it. In your own journey of seeking knowledge, are there topics you have conceded or accepted without truly understanding? What is one such topic, and what step will you take this week to investigate it with sincerity?
?
The Basrian argument emphasizes that an attribute of a thing cannot subsist in something else. Reflect on how you view the relationship between God and His attributes. Do you ever treat God's attributes as separate entities rather than aspects of His essence? How can this passage help you affirm tawheed in your heart and daily worship?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

Mu'tazilah Classification of Attributes: Transitory vs. Non-Transitory
Mu'tazilah classify attributes as transitory or non‑transitory, and argue life is non‑transitory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilī classification of attributes into transitory and non‑transitory, and the special case of life.

Core Idea
In Mu'tazilī theology, not all attributes spread like fire—some stay put.

The Mu'tazilah developed a detailed theory about how attributes relate to their loci. They distinguished between attributes that require the locus to be alive (concomitants of life) and those that do not. This passage presents their classification and then highlights a disagreement about life itself, with the majority taking a position that avoids an infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two categories
The passage begins by stating the unanimous view on concomitants of life (knowledge, power, will). Note the contrast with colors.
Also notice the disagreement about life after the semicolon.
2
Understand the concept of 'transitivity of ruling'
The key idea is whether the attribute's effect (the description it gives) spreads from the part to the whole, or stays confined. Concomitants of life spread; colors do not.
For life, they argue it cannot spread, otherwise infinite regress.
3
Reflect on the logical argument about life
The clever ones argue that if life were a concomitant of life, its subsistence would require another life, leading to infinite regress. Therefore life must be non‑transitive.
This shows how theological arguments avoid logical absurdities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة بأسرهم : توابع الحياة كالعلم والقدرة والإرادة، وسائر ما يشترط في قيامه بمحله الحياة ( إذا قامت بجزء من الحي أوجبت للمجموع حكمها ، فكان المجموع عالماً قادراً إذا قام العلم والقدرة بجزء واحد من أجزائه ( بخلاف غيرهما أي غير توابع الحياة كالألوان عند من يثبت لها أحكاماً، فإن حكمها لا يتعدى محلها، بل يختص به واختلفوا في الحياة هل يتعدى حكمها محلها أو لا ، فألحقها الحذاق منهم بالقسم الثاني ، وقالوا : إذا قام الحياة بجزء من شيء كان الحي بها هو ذلك الجزء، لا جملة ذلك الشيء، فإنها أي الحياة ليست من توابع الحياة أي ليس قيامها بمحل مشروطاً بقيام الحياة بذلك المحل، وإلا لزم التسلسل فهي كالألوان في أن حكمها لا يتعدى محلها.
And the Mu'tazilah as a whole said: The concomitants (tawābiʿ) of life, such as knowledge, power, will, and everything whose subsistence in its locus is conditioned by life — when they subsist in a part of the living being, they necessitate their ruling for the whole, so the whole is knowing and powerful if knowledge and power subsist in one of its parts. Unlike other things, i.e., other than the concomitants of life, such as colors — according to those who affirm rulings for them — for their ruling does not extend beyond their locus, but rather is specific to it. And they disagreed about life: does its ruling extend beyond its locus or not? The clever ones (al-ḥudhdhāq) among them joined it to the second category, and they said: If life subsists in a part of a thing, then the living being through it is that part, not the whole of that thing. Because [life] is not among the concomitants of life, i.e., its subsistence in a locus is not conditioned by the subsistence of life in that locus; otherwise there would be a regress (tasalsul). Thus it is like colors in that its ruling does not extend beyond its locus.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilah classification of attributes and their rulings regarding transitivity. It first states the unanimous Mu'tazilī view on the concomitants of life (tawābiʿ al-ḥayāh) like knowledge, power, and will. These are attributes whose very subsistence in a locus requires that locus to be alive. According to them, if such an attribute subsists in one part of a living being, it causes the entire being to be described by that attribute. For example, if knowledge subsists in one part, the whole is knowing. Then they contrast this with non-concomitants like colors, whose ruling remains restricted to the specific part in which they inhere and does not spread to the whole. The passage then notes a disagreement about life itself: is life a concomitant of life (i.e., does its subsistence require another life, leading to regress) or not? The 'clever ones' (al-ḥudhdhāq) among the Mu'tazilah concluded that life is not a concomitant of life, meaning its subsistence in a locus does not require that locus to already have life. If it did, it would entail an infinite regress. Therefore, like colors, the ruling of life (i.e., being alive) applies only to the part in which life subsists, not to the whole. This classification is important for understanding the Mu'tazilī theory of attributes and causality.

Key Terms

القدرة
الإرادة

تعدي الحكم
محل الحكم
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all attributes are transitory in Mu'tazilī thought.
Correction: Only the concomitants of life are transitory; other attributes like colors and life itself are non‑transitory.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilah, what is the ruling of life's transitivity?
A) Life's ruling extends to the whole like other concomitants
B) Life's ruling is restricted to its part, similar to colors
C) Life's ruling does not exist
D) Life's ruling extends only if life is in multiple parts

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilah distinguish between transitory attributes that spread from part to whole (concomitants of life) and non‑transitory ones that remain confined (like colors and life itself), the latter to avoid infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The clever Mu'tazilah argued that life, by not being transitory, avoids infinite regress. Consider your own spiritual life: are there aspects where you assume that because you are 'alive' in faith in one area, it automatically spreads to all others? What part of your soul remains untouched, and what will you do today to bring life to that dead part?
?
If life itself is not transitory, then your existence in any given moment is a direct gift from the Creator, not a chain of causes. How does this realization change the way you perceive your dependence on Allah every single second? Will you acknowledge this dependence in your prayers today?
?
The passage distinguishes between attributes that spread and those that are confined. Is your love for Allah a transitory attribute that spreads to all your actions, or is it confined to certain rituals? Which actions in your daily life are not yet influenced by that love, and how will you invite it in?

Asha'irah Proof Against Transitivity of Cause's Ruling
Ashʿarite proof against transitivity of cause's ruling using knowledge attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ashʿarite argument that the ruling of a cause cannot extend beyond its locus, specifically using the attribute of knowledge as an example.

Core Idea
The ruling of a cause must inhere in its locus, just as an accident must subsist in a substance.

This passage presents a key Ashʿarite proof against the transitivity of a cause's ruling. The argument uses the attribute of knowledge to show that if the ruling could extend beyond its locus, absurd consequences would follow. By examining the options, the Ashʿarites conclude that the ruling must remain in its original locus.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the structure of the argument. The passage presents a conditional and then two alternatives.
Pay attention to the logical connectors (إما...أو).
2
Identify the main premise
The argument starts with the assumption that knowledge does not subsist in the locus of its ruling. What are the two options that follow?
3
Analyze the first option
The first option is that knowledge subsists in itself. The passage gives two reasons why this is invalid: (1) knowledge is an accident, which cannot subsist independently, and (2) even if it could, its relation to all loci is equal, leading to absurd consequences.
Consider why equality of relation leads to impossibility.
4
Analyze the second option
The second option is that knowledge subsists in another locus. This leads to the absurdity that one person would be knowing by knowledge in another person.
5
Understand the conclusion
Since both alternatives are impossible, the only coherent possibility is that knowledge subsists in its own locus, proving that transitivity is impossible.
This reinforces the Ashʿarite position on causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج أصحابنا على أن حكم العلة لا يجوز أن يتعدى محلها : ( بأن صفة العلم لو لم تقم بمحل الحكم الذي هو العالمية ( لقامت إما بنفسها ويبطله أنها عرض)، (و) يبطله أيضاً أن نسبته أي نسبة العلم على تقدير قيامه بنفسه (إلى) جميع المحال سواء)، وحينئذ إما أن يوجب العالمية في جميع الأشخاص، وهو ظاهر الاستحالة، أو يوجبها في بعض دون بعض فيلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح، ( أو بمحل آخر) غير محل الحكم، ( فيكون زيد عالماً بعلم قائم بعمرو، وهو باطل بالضرورة.
Our companions (the Ashʿarites) argued that the ruling of the cause cannot extend beyond its locus: [They said] that if the attribute of knowledge were not to subsist in the locus of the ruling—which is being knowing (al-ʿālimiyya)—then it would either subsist in itself, which is invalidated by its being an accident (ʿaraḍ); and it is also invalidated by the fact that its relation, i.e., the relation of knowledge—on the assumption of its subsistence in itself—is equal to all loci. Then either it would necessitate being knowing in all persons, which is clearly impossible, or it would necessitate it in some without others, which would entail preference without a preferrer (tarjīḥ bi lā murajjiḥ); or [it would subsist] in another locus, different from the locus of the ruling, in which case Zayd would be knowing by a knowledge subsisting in ʿAmr, which is necessarily false.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents the Asha'irah proof that the ruling of a cause cannot extend beyond its locus. The proof focuses on the attribute of knowledge. The argument proceeds: if knowledge were not to subsist in the very locus of its ruling (i.e., the place where the attribute of being knowing inheres), then there are only two possibilities: either knowledge subsists in itself, or it subsists in another locus. The first option (subsisting in itself) is invalid because knowledge is an accident, and an accident cannot subsist independently. Furthermore, even if one assumes it could subsist in itself, its relation to all potential loci would be equal. Thus, either it would make all persons knowing (absurd) or only some without a reason (unjustified preference). The second option (subsisting in another locus) leads to the absurdity of one person being knowing by knowledge that inheres in another person (e.g., Zayd knowing by knowledge in ʿAmr), which is self-evidently false. Therefore, the only coherent option is that knowledge must subsist in the same locus as its ruling, proving that transitivity is impossible.

Key Terms
العلة
حكم العلة
تعدي
محل الحكم
العالمية
العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof relies solely on the impossibility of an accident subsisting in itself.
Correction: The proof also uses the equality of relation argument, which leads to further absurdities even if subsistence in itself were possible.

Checkpoint
What is the first option considered for knowledge if it does not subsist in the locus of its ruling?
A) It subsists in another attribute
B) It subsists in itself
C) It subsists in the eternal
D) It subsists in the intellect

Key Takeaway
The Ashʿarite proof demonstrates that the ruling of a cause must inhere in its locus, as any alternative leads to logical absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something, do you truly believe your actions are the cause, or do you see them as mere loci through which Allah's decree manifests? How would your gratitude change if you internalized that the ruling cannot extend beyond its locus?
?
The proof shows that attributing knowledge to another person (Zayd knowing by knowledge in ʿAmr) is absurd. Where in your life have you attributed an outcome to a secondary cause (e.g., wealth, health, a person) as if that cause had independent power, forgetting that the true locus of all rulings is only with Allah?
?
If you sincerely believed that no cause's ruling can ever transfer to another locus, how would that change your reliance on means like medicine, income, or relationships? Would you still use them, but with a different heart? What is one concrete step you can take today to shift your trust from means to the Creator of causes?

Objection to Ash'ari Proof and Its Refutation
Objection and refutation regarding causes subsisting in themselves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an objection to the Ash'ari proof about causes is raised and how it is refuted by distinguishing between substances and accidents.

Core Idea
Not all causes are accidents; substances can be self-subsisting causes.

In the previous lesson, the Ash'ari argument that causes cannot subsist in themselves was presented. An objector now challenges this by pointing out that some causes, like the existence of a substance, do subsist in themselves. The commentator responds by clarifying that the argument only applies to accidents, not to substances.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the objector's claim that not all causes must be self-subsisting. He uses the example of the existence of a substance, which according to the Ash'aris is identical with the substance's essence, yet it serves as a cause for the substance being visible.
Notice the objector's use of 'jawhar' (substance) to challenge the Ash'ari premise.
2
Grasp the fallback argument
The objector also concedes for the sake of argument that no cause can subsist in itself, but argues that this does not necessarily lead to impossibility of transitivity.
This shows the objector attacking both the premise and the conclusion.
3
Follow the commentary's response
The commentator responds by pointing out that the Ash'ari argument was about accidents, not substances. He notes that the objector's example of substance does not refute the Ash'ari claim. The commentator also clarifies the meaning of 'cause' as necessitating and as relation in Ilāhiyyāt.
The key refutation is the mismatch in applicability: the Ash'ari argument only targets accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : العلم وكثير من العلل وإن استحال قيامها بنفسها، لكن ذلك غير لازم في جميع العلل لجواز أن يقوم بعضها بنفسه إذ وجود الجوهر عندكم علة لرؤيته وكونه مرئيا مع قيامه بنفسه؛ لأن وجود الجوهر عندكم عين ذاته، مع سلمنا امتناع قيام العلة بنفسها مطلقاً، لكن ليس يلزم امتناع التعدي مطلقاً.

قوله : ( لجواز أن يقوم بعضها بنفسه فلا يصح قوله ويبطله أنها عرض .
قوله: (وجود) الجوهر عندكم علة لرؤيته) أي لصحة رؤيته إذ العلة يجب أن تكون موجبة، وكونه علة موجبة لا ينافي ما في الإلهيات من أن المراد بالعلة المتعلق. قوله : ( وكونه مرئياً ) عطف تفسيري لرؤيته على أنه مصدر المجهول.
For it might be said: Knowledge and many causes, even if their subsistence in themselves is impossible, this is not necessary in all causes, because it is possible that some of them subsist in themselves, since the existence (wujūd) of the substance (jawhar) according to you is the cause of its being seen (ruʼya) and its being visible (marʼiyy) while it subsists in itself; because the existence of the substance according to you is identical with its essence. And even if we grant the impossibility of the cause subsisting in itself absolutely, still the impossibility of transitivity does not follow absolutely.

His saying: 'Because it is possible that some of them subsist in themselves' — so his saying 'and invalidated by its being an accident' is not valid.
His saying: 'The existence of the substance according to you is the cause of its being seen' — i.e., for the correctness of its being seen, since the cause must be necessitating (mūjiba). And its being a necessitating cause does not contradict what will come in the Ilāhiyyāt that the meaning of 'cause' is the relation (mutaʻalliq). His saying: 'And its being visible' is an explanatory conjunction (ʻaṭf tafsīrī) for 'its being seen' (ruʼyatih) on the basis that it is the verbal noun of the passive.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the previous Ash'ari proof, followed by the commentator's response. The objector argues that even if knowledge cannot subsist in itself, this does not apply to all causes. Some causes, like the existence of a substance, subsist in themselves (since the substance's existence is identical with its essence). This existence is considered a cause for the substance being seen and visible. Thus, the Ash'ari premise that causes cannot subsist in themselves is not universal. Moreover, even if we accept that no cause can be self-subsisting, it does not logically entail that transitivity of the ruling is impossible. The commentator then responds by glossing the objector's statement. He points out that the objector's claim 'it is possible that some of them subsist in themselves' undermines the Ash'ari argument that 'it is invalidated by its being an accident' – because the Ash'ari argument was specifically about accidents, not substances. The commentator also clarifies the meaning of the objector's example: existence (wujūd) being a cause for visibility means it is a cause for the correctness of being seen, and this does not contradict the later discussion in Ilāhiyyāt about the cause being a relation. Thus, the objection is refuted by noting the mismatch in applicability.

The Ruling of the Cause Does Not Extend Beyond Its Locus
The author defines life as not a tābi' and gives the Ash'ari proof against transferring the cause's ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that the ruling of a cause must reside in the same locus as the cause, and the logical proof against transferring it.

Core Idea
The ruling of a cause cannot transfer to another locus; otherwise absurdities follow.

In this passage, the author clarifies a key principle of causality in Kalām: the effect (ruling) of a cause must be in the same subject as the cause. He first distinguishes life from being a dependent (tābi'), then presents the Ash'ari proof using knowledge as an example. This establishes a fundamental rule that prevents logical contradictions in theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the nature of life
Read the first sentence where the author says life is not a tābi' of life. Grasp what it means: the subsistence of life in a subject does not require that subject to already possess life. Note the consequence: otherwise infinite regress.
This sets the stage for the general rule about causes.
2
Identify the Ash'ari proof structure
The proof assumes the contrary: that the ruling (being knowing) could be elsewhere. Then it explores two possibilities: the cause (knowledge) subsists in itself, or in another locus. Both are shown impossible.
The proof is a reductio ad absurdum (proof by contradiction).
3
Examine the two impossibilities
First, if knowledge subsists in itself, it's impossible because it's an accident and cannot be self-subsistent. Moreover, if it did, its relation to all loci would be equal, leading to either universal knowing (absurd) or arbitrary selection (preponderance without preponderator). Second, if knowledge is in another locus, then a person would be knowing by someone else's knowledge, which is absurd.
Both alternatives are impossible, so the original assumption is false: the ruling must be in the same locus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحياة ليست من توابع (الحياة) أي ليس قيامها بمحل مشروطاً بقيام الحياة بذلك المحل، وإلا لزم التسلسل فهي كالألوان في أن حكمها لا يتعدى محلها. (احتج أصحابنا على أن حكم العلة لا يجوز أن يتعدى محلها : ( بأن صفة العلم لو لم تقم بمحل الحكم الذي هو العالمية ( لقامت إما بنفسها ويبطله أنها عرض)، والعرض يتصور قيامه بنفسه، (و) يبطله أيضاً أن نسبته أي نسبة العلم على تقدير قيامه بنفسه (إلى) جميع المحال سواء)، وحينئذ إما أن يوجب العالمية في جميع الأشخاص، وهو ظاهر الاستحالة، أو يوجبها في بعض دون بعض فيلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح، ( أو بمحل آخر) غير محل الحكم، ( فيكون زيد عالماً بعلم قائم بعمرو، وهو باطل بالضرورة،
Life is not from the tawābi' (appendages) of life, meaning its subsistence in a locus is not conditional upon the subsistence of life in that locus; otherwise an infinite regress would follow. So it is like colors in that its ruling does not extend beyond its locus. (Our companions argued that the ruling of the cause cannot extend beyond its locus: because if the attribute of knowledge were not to subsist in the locus of the ruling, which is being knowing, it would either subsist in itself, which is invalidated by the fact that it is an accident, and an accident cannot be conceived to subsist by itself, and it is also invalidated by the fact that its relation—i.e., the relation of knowledge, assuming its subsistence by itself—to all loci is equal; then it would either necessitate being knowing in all persons, which is manifestly impossible, or necessitate it in some without others, necessitating preponderance without a preponderator, or it would be in another locus other than the locus of the ruling, so that Zayd would be knowing by knowledge subsisting in 'Amr, and that is necessarily false.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage begins with a definition and then presents a proof. First, the author defines life (al-ḥayāh) as not being a tābi' (dependent) of life, meaning that its existence in a subject (maḥall) does not require the prior existence of life in that subject; otherwise an infinite regress (tasalsul) would occur. He then compares life to colors: just as the color's ruling (i.e., its being colored) does not extend beyond its locus, so too life's ruling does not extend. Next, the author presents the proof of the Ash'aris (aṣḥābunā) that the ruling of a cause (ʿillah) cannot extend beyond its locus. The proof focuses on the attribute of knowledge (al-ʿilm): if the ruling (which is being knowing, al-ʿālamiyyah) were not in the same locus as the attribute, then the attribute would have to subsist either in itself or in another locus. Subsistence in itself is impossible because knowledge is an accident (ʿaraḍ) and accidents cannot subsist by themselves. Moreover, if it did subsist by itself, its relation to all loci would be equal, so it would either necessitate knowing in all persons (impossible) or only in some without a preponderator (tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ). Alternatively, if it subsists in another locus (e.g., in 'Amr), then Zayd would be knowing by knowledge in 'Amr, which is absurd. Thus, the ruling cannot extend beyond the cause's locus.

Key Takeaway
The ruling of a cause must remain within its own locus; otherwise, logical absurdities such as self-subsistence of accidents or transfer of knowledge between persons would occur.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If the ruling of a cause cannot extend beyond its locus, then your actions have consequences that are tied to you. Are there areas in your life where you try to shift responsibility for your shortcomings to others? What would it look like to fully own your actions before Allah?

Transfer of Ruling: Objection and Qualified Response
Objection and answer on causality: substance as self-subsistent cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari scholars respond to the objection that a self-subsistent cause (like substance) allows transfer of ruling, and the qualification that transfer is only permitted when the cause's locus is part of the ruling's locus.

Core Idea
Transfer of a ruling is allowed only when the cause's locus is part of the ruling's locus.

In this passage, the author addresses a strong objection to the earlier proof that cause and effect cannot share the same ruling. The objector points to the example of a substance (jawhar), which is self-subsistent and yet is a cause for visibility. How can the rule stand if there is a counterexample? The author responds with a crucial qualification, clarifying the exact conditions under which transfer is permissible. This lesson unpacks that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the objector's claim: that some causes (like substance) subsist by themselves, so the impossibility of transfer is not absolute.
The objector uses the example of substance being a cause for visibility while being self-subsistent.
2
Examine the author's qualified response
Notice how the author concedes the objection but restricts the transfer to cases where the cause's locus is part of the ruling's locus.
The author mentions 'appendages of life' as an example where transfer is allowed.
3
Analyze why the objector's counterexample fails
The author shows that the example of knowledge in 'Amr does not satisfy the condition because 'Amr is not part of Zayd.
Also, the objector's example is merely an illustration of a particular case, not a universal ruling.
4
Understand the logical structure
Map the argument: objection (substance is self-subsistent cause) → response (concession with qualification) → rebuttal of counterexample → denial of universality.
This shows the Ash'ari position is nuanced, not absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : العلم وكثير من العلل وإن استحال قيامها بنفسها، لكن ذلك غير لازم في جميع العلل لجواز أن يقوم بعضها بنفسه إذ (وجود الجوهر عندكم علة لرؤيته وكونه مرئيا مع قيامه بنفسه)؛ لأن وجود الجوهر عندكم عين ذاته، (مع سلمنا امتناع قيام العلة بنفسها مطلقاً، لكن ليس يلزم امتناع التعدي مطلقاً. (وإنما نجوزه) أي تعدي الحكم ( إذا كان ( محل العلة ( جزءاً لمحل الحكم)، كما صورناه في توابع الحياة . وما ذكرتم من كون زيد عالما بعلم قائم بعمرو (ليس كذلك، فإن عمراً ليس جزءاً لزيد حتى يتعدى الحكم منه إليه، ( وأيضاً فإنه ) أي ما ذكرتم ( تمثيل ) أي بيان للحكم الذي هو امتناع التعدي في مثال جزئي هو العلم، ( فلا يفيد الحكم الكلي
For if it is said: Knowledge and many causes, even if it is impossible for them to subsist by themselves, yet that is not necessary in all causes, because it is possible for some of them to subsist by themselves, since (the existence of the substance, according to you, is a cause for its being seen and being visible while it subsists by itself) because the existence of the substance, according to you, is identical to its essence, (and even if we concede the impossibility of the cause subsisting by itself absolutely, yet the impossibility of transfer is not necessary absolutely. (And we only allow it)—i.e., the transfer of the ruling—(when the locus of the cause is a part of the locus of the ruling), as we exemplified in the appendages of life. And what you mentioned about Zayd being knowing by knowledge subsisting in 'Amr is not like that, for 'Amr is not a part of Zayd such that the ruling could transfer from him to him, (and also it) i.e., what you mentioned (is an illustration)—i.e., a clarification of the ruling which is the impossibility of transfer in a particular example, namely knowledge, (so it does not benefit the universal ruling.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues that the earlier proof does not apply to all causes because some causes, like the substance (jawhar), are self-subsistent. The objector claims that the existence of the substance itself is the cause of its visibility, and the substance subsists by itself, not in a locus. Therefore, it is possible for a cause to exist without a locus, so the impossibility of the ruling transferring is not absolute. The author responds by qualifying the position: transfer of the ruling is only allowed when the cause's locus is part of the ruling's locus, as in the case of the appendages of life (tawābiʿ al-ḥayāh). The example of knowledge (ʿilm) in 'Amr is not analogous because 'Amr is not a part of Zayd. Moreover, the objector's example is merely an illustration (tamthīl) of the impossibility of transfer in a particular case (knowledge), and does not constitute a universal ruling. Thus, the objection fails to overturn the general principle.
