The Impossibility of Coexisting Absences: Refined Objection on Knowledge and Ignorance
The author refines the objection by arguing that knowledge and ignorance are opposites and cannot both be absent, and further analyzes the composite ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the absence of knowledge and the absence of ignorance cannot coexist, and how the distinction between simple and composite ignorance addresses a key objection in the debate on privative causation.

Core Idea
Knowledge and ignorance are opposites; the absence of one implies the presence of the other.

In this lesson, we continue the response to the objection that a privative attribute could cause a positive state. The opponent tries to use ignorance as an example, arguing that absence of knowledge (ignorance) could cause ignorance. But the author shows that knowledge and ignorance are mutually exclusive opposites like day and night—they cannot both be absent from the same locus. This leads to a refined analysis of different types of ignorance and their logical relations.

Guided Path
1
Understand the key opposition
The passage begins by stating that absence of knowledge is ignorance, and absence of ignorance is knowledge. This establishes that knowledge and ignorance are opposites (تضاد وتناف), so they cannot both be absent from the same thing.
Notice that the author uses both 'opposition' (تضاد) and 'conflict' (تناف) to emphasize mutual exclusivity.
2
Follow the opponent's reformulation
The opponent reformulates the argument by considering composite ignorance (الجهل المركب), claiming that if non-existent knowledge can cause worldliness, then non-existent composite ignorance can cause ignorance. This leads to the possibility of both absences coexisting.
Composite ignorance is a positive belief in something false, not a mere absence.
3
Analyze the response: conditional types
The response argues that even if we concede the opponent's premise, the conditional becomes coincidental (اتفاقية) because there is no necessary relation between the antecedent and consequent. In contrast, with simple ignorance (الجهل البسيط) the conditional is necessary (لزومية).
This distinction is crucial: simple ignorance is the pure absence of knowledge, so its relation to ignorance is necessary.
4
Conclude: absences cannot coexist
Because knowledge and ignorance are opposites, the absence of one entails the presence of the other. Therefore, the two absences cannot coexist in the same locus. The opponent's objection fails.
The author thus defends the original position against the refined objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكرتموه من هذا القبيل، مع أنه غير تام في نفسه، وإليه أشار بقوله : ( وأيضاً : فلا نسلم اجتماع العدمين، إذ عدم العلم جهل، وعدم الجهل علم، وبينهما ) أي بين العلم والجهل (تضاد وتناف، فإن قلت : نحن نقول : لو جاز أن تكون العالمية معللة بعلم عدمي لجاز أن تكون الجاهلية معللة بجهل عدمي، فإذا اجتمع هذان العدميان في محل كان عالماً جاهلا بشيء واحد من جهة واحدة . قلت : لا نسلم أنه إذا كان مسمى العلم عدمياً وموجباً لكون محله عالماً كان الجهل مسمی أيضاً عدمياً موجباً لكون محله جاهلاً ، سلمناه لكن لا نسلم إمكان اجتماع هذين . قوله : (قلنا إلخ ) حاصله أنه فرق بين لا علم له وعلمه لا والنزاع في الثاني دون الأول. قوله : ( وأيضاً فلا نسلم اجتماع إلخ) يعني أن مقدم الشرطية أعني قوله فإذا عدما محال فيجوز أن يستلزم المحال إذ عدم كل منهما يستلزم وجود الآخر فلا يمكن اجتماع عدميهما. قوله: (وتناف) حمل التضاد على المعنى اللغوي ليتم التقريب إذ تحقق التضاد لا يقتضي امتناع ارتفاعهما بخلاف التنافي . قوله : (فإن قلت إلخ) تحرير للاستدلال المذكور بحيث يندفع المنعان. وحاصله الاستدلال بالعلم والجهل المركب يعني لو جاز تعليل العالمية بالعلم المعدوم لجاز تعليل الجاهلية بالجهل المركب المعدوم؛ إذ لا فرق بين العالمية والجاهلية لكون كل منهما حكماً ثبوتياً ولا بين عليتهما لكونهما معدومين، فإذا اجتمع هذان العدميان أي اتصف محل واحد بهما لزم كونه عالماً وجاهلاً معاً، فاندفع المنع الأول لاعتبار ثبوتهما لشيء واحد، والثاني لعدم كون أحدهما عدماً للآخر. قوله : ( قلت لا نسلم إلخ) حاصله : أنه حينئذ تكون الشرطية اتفاقية؛ إذ لا علاقة بين المقدم والتالي بخلاف ما إذا اعتبر الجهل البسيط، فإنها حينئذ تكون لزومية، كما عرفت . مع ورود المنع الثاني لأن العلم والجهل متقابلان وإن لم يكن أحدهما عدماً للآخر. قوله: (وأيضاً فلا نسلم اجتماع العدمين فيه بحث لأن الظاهر أن كلامهم في العلم والجهل المركب ويجوز اجتماع عدميهما. قوله : (تضاد وتناف) فسر التضاد بالتنافي الذي هو أعم ليمكن حمله على المذهبين وهما كون التقابل بينهما تقابل التضاد وتقابل العدم والملكة . قوله: (فإن قلت: نحن نقول إلخ) هذا إشارة إلى رد الجواب الأول بأنه ليس بصحيح إذ يمكن تقرير الكلام ،هكذا، وإلا فلا جهة له أصلاً لأن جوابه قد فهم، بل صرح به في قوله : وأيضاً نسلم إلخ .
And what you mentioned is of this kind, although it is not complete in itself. To it he gestured by his saying: (And also: we do not concede the coexistence of two absences, for the absence of knowledge is ignorance, and the absence of ignorance is knowledge, and between them) i.e., between knowledge and ignorance (there is opposition and conflict. If you say: we say: if it were permissible for worldliness (العالمية) to be caused by non-existent knowledge, then it would be permissible for ignorance (الجاهلية) to be caused by non-existent ignorance; then if these two absences coexist in a locus, it would be knowing and ignorant of one thing from one aspect. I say: we do not concede that when the name of knowledge is non-existent and causes its locus to be knowing, that ignorance is also named non-existent and causes its locus to be ignorant; if we concede that, we do not concede the possibility of the coexistence of these two. He said: (We said, etc.) Its summary: he distinguishes between 'he has no knowledge' and 'his knowledge is not', and the dispute is about the second, not the first. He said: (And also we do not concede, etc.) Means that the premise of the conditional, i.e., his saying 'when they are absent', is impossible, so it is permissible that impossibility follow, for the absence of each entails the existence of the other, so their two absences cannot coexist. He said: (And conflict) he interprets opposition (التضاد) in a linguistic sense to complete the approximation, for the realization of opposition does not necessitate the impossibility of their removal, unlike conflict (التنافي). He said: (If you say, etc.) It is a formulation of the aforementioned argument such that the two objections are repelled. Its summary: the argument is by composite knowledge and ignorance, i.e., if it were permissible for worldliness to be caused by non-existent knowledge, then it would be permissible for ignorance to be caused by composite non-existent ignorance, for there is no difference between worldliness and ignorance in that each is a positive judgment, nor between their causes in that both are non-existent. Then if these two absences coexist, i.e., one locus is described by both, it necessitates its being knowing and ignorant together. Thus the first objection is repelled because of considering their affirmation for one thing, and the second because one is not the absence of the other. He said: (I say we do not concede, etc.) Its summary: in that case, the conditional is coincidental, for there is no relation between the antecedent and consequent, unlike when simple ignorance is considered, for then it would be lizumiya (necessary), as you know, along with the occurrence of the second objection because knowledge and ignorance are opposites even if one is not the absence of the other. He said: (And also we do not concede the coexistence of two absences) There is discussion in it, because apparently their speech is about composite knowledge and ignorance, and it is permissible for their two absences to coexist. He said: (Opposition and conflict) He interpreted opposition as conflict in a broader sense to allow it to bear upon both doctrines: that the opposition between them is opposition of contraries and opposition of privation and possession. He said: (If you say: we say, etc.) This is a reference to the rejection of the first answer, that it is not correct, because the speech can be formulated thus, and otherwise there is no basis for it at all, because its answer has been understood, but he stated it in his saying: 'and also we concede, etc.'
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage continues the response to the objection from knowledge and ignorance. The author first notes that the opponent's example is not complete. He then introduces a further argument from the commentator: the phrase 'and also: we do not concede the coexistence of two absences' means that the absence of knowledge is ignorance and the absence of ignorance is knowledge, so knowledge and ignorance are opposites (تضاد وتناف). Therefore, they cannot both be absent from a locus, because the absence of one entails the presence of the other. The opponent then reformulates the objection using composite (مركب) knowledge and ignorance, claiming that if non-existent knowledge can cause worldliness, then composite non-existent ignorance can cause ignorance, and if both non-existent descriptions coexist in a locus, it would be both knowing and ignorant. The response is that even if we concede that composite ignorance can also cause ignorance, the conditional is coincidental (اتفاقية) because there is no necessary relation between the antecedent and consequent, unlike the case of simple ignorance (الجهل البسيط) which is the mere absence of knowledge. The commentator further explains that the opponents are discussing composite knowledge and ignorance, and it is possible for their absences to coexist. The discussion clarifies that the real issue is whether a privative attribute can be a cause, and the author carefully distinguishes different senses of absence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a person can be neither knowing nor ignorant about something, i.e., that the absences of knowledge and ignorance can coexist.
Correction: The passage shows that knowledge and ignorance are opposites (tadad/tanaf), so the absence of knowledge is ignorance and the absence of ignorance is knowledge. Thus, one of them must always be present, and two absences cannot coexist.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that the absence of knowledge is ignorance?
A) Because they are synonyms
B) To show that knowledge and ignorance are opposites such that the absence of one is the presence of the other
C) To prove that ignorance is existent
D) To reject the opponent's claim

Key Takeaway
Knowledge and ignorance are opposites, so the absence of one implies the presence of the other; therefore, two absences cannot coexist in the same locus.

Why a Non-Existent Attribute Cannot Cause a Positive Judgment
The author argues against a non-existent attribute causing a positive judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical reason why a non-existent attribute cannot serve as the cause (illa) for a positive judgment, based on the impossibility of contradictory attributes coexisting.

Core Idea
A non-existent attribute cannot cause a positive judgment without causing contradiction.

This passage delves into a subtle but crucial point in the theology of divine attributes: can a lack or absence of something be the cause of a positive quality? The author argues that this leads to absurd consequences—specifically, the possibility of a single subject possessing contradictory attributes. We'll walk through the argument, from the initial statement of the problem to the resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the gist of the argument.
The passage includes a statement of the issue, an objection, and a response.
2
Identify the two positions
Notice the author's statement of the issue: a subject described by a non-existent attribute that causes a positive judgment. He then rejects the alternative view as obviously false.
3
Trace the objection
Follow the opponent's reasoning: if non-existent knowledge causes being a knower, then non-existent ignorance would cause being ignorant.
4
Understand the contradiction
See how combining these leads to a subject being both knower and ignorant of one thing from one aspect.
This is the key consequence that makes the original position untenable.
5
Analyze the author's response
The author first denies the equivalence between knowledge and ignorance being both non-existent causes, and then even if conceded, denies the possibility of their combination.
The response relies on the opposition between knowledge and ignorance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يتصف محل بصفة عدمية ويكون ذلك موجباً لحكم ثبوتي في ذلك المحل، لا أنه يجوز أن تسلب صفة عن محل ويكون ذلك السلب موجباً له حكم تلك الصفة، فإنه ظاهر البطلان. وما ذكرتموه من هذا القبيل، مع أنه غير تام في نفسه، وإليه أشار بقوله : ( وأيضاً : فلا نسلم اجتماع العدمين، إذ عدم العلم جهل، وعدم الجهل علم، وبينهما ) أي بين العلم والجهل (تضاد وتناف، فإن قلت: نحن نقول : لو جاز أن تكون العالمية معللة بعلم عدمي لجاز أن تكون الجاهلية معللة بجهل عدمي، فإذا اجتمع هذان العدميان في محل كان عالماً جاهلا بشيء واحد من جهة واحدة . قلت : لا نسلم أنه إذا كان مسمى العلم عدمياً وموجباً لكون محله عالماً كان الجهل مسمی أيضاً عدمياً موجباً لكون محله جاهلاً ، سلمناه لكن لا نسلم إمكان اجتماع هذين
A subject is described by a non-existent attribute and that causes a positive judgment in that subject, not that it is permissible to negate an attribute from a subject and that negation causes the judgment of that attribute, for that is obviously false. What you have mentioned is of this kind, although it is not complete in itself, and to this he pointed by his saying: (Also: we do not concede the conjunction of two non-existents, for the absence of knowledge is ignorance, and the absence of ignorance is knowledge, and between them) i.e. between knowledge and ignorance (there is opposition and conflict. If you say: we say: if it were permissible for being a knower to be caused by a non-existent knowledge, then it would be permissible for being ignorant to be caused by a non-existent ignorance. So if these two non-existents are combined in a subject, it would be a knower and ignorant of one thing from one aspect. I say: we do not concede that if the meaning of knowledge is non-existent and causes its subject to be a knower, then ignorance is also non-existent and causes its subject to be ignorant. We concede that, but we do not concede the possibility of their combination.)
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the problem concerning whether a non-existent attribute can be a cause (illa) for a positive judgment (hukm thubuti). The author begins by defining the issue: a subject is described by a non-existent attribute, and that causes a positive judgment. He immediately rejects the alternative—that the negation of an attribute could cause the judgment of that attribute—as obviously false. Then he references an objection from the opponent, which is introduced by 'his saying' (qawlihi). The objection argues that if a non-existent knowledge (ilm 'adami) could cause someone to be a knower (alim), then a non-existent ignorance (jahl 'adami) could cause someone to be ignorant. This would lead to a contradiction: the same subject would be both a knower and ignorant of the same thing. The author then gives the answer: first, he does not concede that if knowledge is non-existent and causes being a knower, then ignorance must also be non-existent and cause being ignorant. Even if that is conceded, he does not concede the possibility of their combination (ijtima'ahuma) because of the opposition between knowledge and ignorance. The argument thus shows that a non-existent attribute cannot be a cause, as it leads to impossible consequences.

المعلول
الصفة العدمية
الحكم الثبوتي
العدم
العلم
الجهل
التضاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A non-existent can have no causal power at all.
Correction: The debate is about a non-existent attribute causing a positive judgment; the argument shows that if it did, it would lead to contradiction, not that a non-existent cannot be a cause in any sense.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main flaw in allowing a non-existent attribute to cause a positive judgment?
A) It leads to the possibility of contradictory attributes in the same subject.
B) It violates the principle of non-contradiction regarding existence.
C) It confuses the negation of an attribute with the attribute itself.
D) It assumes that a non-existent can be a cause, which is impossible.

Key Takeaway
A non-existent attribute cannot cause a positive judgment because it would allow contradictory attributes like knowledge and ignorance to coexist in the same subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

When you attribute qualities to God, do you ever rely on mere negations (e.g., 'He is not unjust') without grounding them in positive affirmations from revelation? How does the author's insistence on avoiding contradictory non-existent causes challenge you to purify your understanding of God's attributes?
?
Think of a time you assumed a non-existent cause for a positive outcome in your life (e.g., 'I am successful because I am smart' when the evidence points elsewhere). How does this argument reveal the logical flaw in such assumptions, and how can you replace them with honest reflection?

Analysis of the Objection and the Meaning of Opposition
Commentator dissects the objection and clarifies the role of opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's step-by-step analysis of the objection concerning the relationship between knowledge and ignorance, and the precise meaning of opposition (tadadd) in this logical context.

Core Idea
Opposition (tadadd) here means mutual exclusion, not mere absence, allowing the argument to hold.

The commentator carefully dissects the previous objection, distinguishing between different types of negation and clarifying the role of opposition. This lesson will guide you through his detailed analysis, helping you grasp the logical moves and the importance of precise terminology in theological argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Then read the full English translation. Notice how the commentator breaks down each part of the objection.
The passage uses technical terms like shartiyya (conditional) and jahl murakkab (composite ignorance).
2
Identify the two preventions (man'ayn)
The commentator mentions two preventions in the original objection. Try to spot them in the text: one about the subject being described by both, and another about one being the absence of the other.
These are key to understanding the argument's structure.
3
Understand the reformulation with composite ignorance
The commentator rephrases the inference using 'composite ignorance' to show that if non-existent knowledge can be a cause, then non-existent composite ignorance can also be a cause, leading to a contradiction.
This is meant to repel the two preventions.
4
Grasp the accidental vs. necessary conditional
The response explains that the conditional becomes accidental (ittifaqiyya) when using composite ignorance, but necessary (luzumiyya) when using simple ignorance. This distinction is crucial.
It also shows the importance of the correct interpretation of opposition.
5
Reflect on the final refutation
The passage ends by indicating that there is no way to prove the possibility of combining the two non-existents, thus the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (قلنا إلخ ) حاصله أنه فرق بين لا علم له وعلمه ،لا والنزاع في الثاني دون الأول. قوله : ( وأيضاً فلا نسلم اجتماع إلخ) يعني أن مقدم الشرطية أعني قوله فإذا عدما محال فيجوز أن يستلزم المحال إذ عدم كل منهما يستلزم وجود الآخر فلا يمكن اجتماع عدميهما. قوله: (وتناف) حمل التضاد على المعنى اللغوي ليتم التقريب إذ تحقق التضاد لا يقتضي امتناع ارتفاعهما بخلاف التنافي . قوله : (فإن قلت إلخ) تحرير للاستدلال المذكور بحيث يندفع المنعان. وحاصله الاستدلال بالعلم والجهل المركب يعني لو جاز تعليل العالمية بالعلم المعدوم لجاز تعليل الجاهلية بالجهل المركب المعدوم؛ إذ لا فرق بين العالمية والجاهلية لكون كل منهما حكماً ثبوتياً ولا بين عليتهما لكونهما ،معدومين، فإذا اجتمع هذان العدميان أي اتصف محل واحد بهما لزم كونه عالماً وجاهلاً معاً، فاندفع المنع الأول لاعتبار ثبوتهما لشيء واحد، والثاني لعدم كون أحدهما عدماً للآخر. قوله : ( قلت لا نسلم إلخ) حاصله : أنه حينئذ تكون الشرطية اتفاقية؛ إذ لا علاقة بين المقدم والتالي بخلاف ما إذا اعتبر الجهل البسيط، فإنها حينئذ تكون لزومية، كما عرفت . مع ورود المنع الثاني لأن العلم والجهل متقابلان وإن لم يكن أحدهما عدماً للآخر. قوله: (وأيضاً فلا نسلم اجتماع العدمين فيه بحث لأن الظاهر أن كلامهم في العلم والجهل المركب ويجوز اجتماع عدميهما. قوله : (تضاد وتناف) فسر التضاد بالتنافي الذي هو أعم ليمكن حمله على المذهبين وهما كون التقابل بينهما تقابل التضاد وتقابل العدم والملكة . قوله: (فإن قلت: نحن نقول إلخ) هذا إشارة إلى رد الجواب الأول بأنه ليس بصحيح إذ يمكن تقرير الكلام ،هكذا، وإلا فلا جهة له أصلاً لأن جوابه قد فهم، بل صرح به في قوله : وأيضاً نسلم إلخ . العدميين مع ما بينهما من التقابل ولا سبيل إلى الدلالة على هذا الإمكان أصلاً.
His saying: (We said etc.) Its summary is that he distinguished between 'he has no knowledge' (la ilma lahu) and 'his knowledge' (ilmuhu), and the dispute is about the second, not the first. His saying: (Also we do not concede the conjunction etc.) It means that the antecedent of the conditional, i.e. his saying 'so if they are non-existent' is impossible, so it may imply the impossible, since the nonexistence of each entails the existence of the other, so their two nonexistences cannot be combined. His saying: (and opposition) He interpreted opposition (tadadd) in the linguistic sense to complete the approximation, since the realization of tadadd does not preclude their elevation (irtifa') unlike conflict (tanafi). His saying: (For if you say etc.) It is a reformulation of the aforementioned inference such that the two preventions are repelled. Its summary is the inference by composite knowledge and ignorance, meaning: if it were permissible to explain being a knower (al-alamiyya) by non-existent knowledge, then it would be permissible to explain ignorance by composite non-existent ignorance; for there is no difference between being a knower and being ignorant since each is a positive judgment, nor between their two causes since they are both non-existent. So if these two non-existents are combined, i.e. a single subject is described by both, it would entail its being simultaneously a knower and ignorant. Thus the first prevention is repelled because they are considered existent for one thing, and the second because one is not the nonexistence of the other. His saying: (I say we do not concede etc.) Its summary: that in that case the conditional is accidental; for there is no relation between the antecedent and consequent, unlike when simple ignorance is considered, for then it would be necessary, as you have known. Along with the occurrence of the second prevention because knowledge and ignorance are opposites, even if one is not the nonexistence of the other. His saying: (Also we do not concede the conjunction of the two non-existents) In it there is a discussion because the apparent meaning is that their speech is about composite knowledge and ignorance, and it is permissible for their two non-existents to be combined. His saying: (opposition and conflict) He interpreted opposition as conflict (tanafi) which is more general to allow carrying it on the two schools, namely that the opposition between them is the opposition of contradiction and the opposition of privation and possession. His saying: (For if you say: we say etc.) This is a reference to the refutation of the first answer, that it is not correct, because the speech can be formulated like this, otherwise it has no basis at all, because his answer was understood, nay he explicitly said in his saying: 'and also we concede etc.' The two non-existents with the opposition between them, and there is no way to indicate this possibility at all.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the objection and answer from the previous lesson. It breaks down each part of the original argument. The commentator first clarifies the difference between 'he has no knowledge' (a negation of knowledge) and 'his knowledge' (the attribute of knowledge), noting that the dispute is about the latter. Then he explains that the opponent's conditional 'if they are non-existent' is impossible, so it may imply an impossible conclusion. The term 'opposition' (tadadd) is interpreted in the linguistic sense to make the argument work, because real tadadd does not prevent both from being absent, unlike conflict (tanafi). The commentator then reformulates the inference using composite knowledge and ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab) to show that if non-existent knowledge can be a cause, so can non-existent composite ignorance, leading to a subject being both knowing and ignorant. This repels the two previous preventions. The response is that then the conditional is accidental, not necessary, because there is no real relation between the antecedent and consequent unless simple ignorance is considered. Finally, the commentator notes that there is a discussion about whether the two non-existents can combine, but the apparent meaning is that they are talking about composite entities, so their non-existents can combine. The passage ends by stating that there is no way to prove the possibility of that combination.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that opposition (tadadd) in this context means simple logical contradiction (e.g., true/false) or that it necessarily implies one is the absence of the other.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that tadadd here is taken in the linguistic sense of mutual exclusion (tanafi), which is broader and does not require one to be the absence of the other.

Two Meanings of Subsistence in Causality
Distinguishing two senses of subsistence for causal attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between two interpretations of 'subsistence' in causal theory and how it allows non-existent attributes to be causes.

Core Idea
Subsistence can mean attribution, not just existence — opening the door for non-existent causes.

This passage continues the discussion on causality in divine attributes. The opponent insists that a cause must subsist in its subject, which seems to exclude non-existent attributes. Sanusi distinguishes two meanings of 'subsistence'—existence and attribution—showing that even non-existents can be attributed and thus serve as causes. This nuance is essential for understanding how attributes like 'power over non-existence' can be real.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the opponent's objection and the response that follows.
The passage uses parentheses to mark the opponent's statement and the response.
2
Identify the two definitions of qiyam
The response breaks down 'subsistence' into two meanings: (1) existence in the subject (like accidents), and (2) attribution of the subject. Locate each in the text.
The first meaning is marked by 'إن أردت بالقيام وجوده له' and the second by 'أو اتصافه به'.
3
Apply the example of blindness
Notice how Zayd's blindness is used to illustrate that a non-existent attribute can be attributed to an existent subject. Explain why this supports the second meaning.
This example shows that attribution does not require existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني ( شرط العلة قيامها بالمحل الذي يوجب له الحكم ( ولا يتصور في العدم قيامه بمحل حتى يوجب له حكماً ثبوتياً ( قلنا : إن أردت بالقيام ) أي قيام الأمر الذي هو العلة بالمحل ) وجوده له مثل وجود الأعراض الموجودة بمحالها، ففيه النزاع )؛ كلامك حينئذ هو أن العلة يجب أن تكون صفة موجودة قائمة بمحل الحكم ، ( أو اتصافه به يعني وإن أردت بالقيام اتصاف المحل بالأمر الذي هو ( فقد يتصف ) المحل الموجود ( بالعدمي )؛ كاتصاف زيد بالعمى فجاز أن تكون العلة عدمية قائمة بمحلها بهذا المعنى .
The second aspect: (The condition of the cause is its subsisting in the subject that necessitates the judgment for it, and it is not conceivable for a non-existent to subsist in a subject such that it necessitates a positive judgment. We say: If by subsistence (qiyam) you mean) i.e. the subsistence of the thing that is the cause in the subject, its existence for it, like the existence of accidents existing in their subjects, then there is dispute in it; your speech then is that the cause must be an existent attribute subsisting in the subject of the judgment, (or its being attributed to it, i.e. if you mean by subsistence the subject's being attributed to the thing that is the cause, then the existent subject may be attributed by the non-existent, like Zayd's being attributed by blindness, so it is permissible for the cause to be non-existent, subsisting in its subject in this sense.)
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents the second aspect (al-wajh al-thani) of the argument. The condition is that the cause must subsist (qiyam) in the subject that necessitates the judgment. The opponent argues that a non-existent cannot subsist in a subject to necessitate a positive judgment. The response distinguishes two meanings of 'subsistence' (qiyam). First, if it means the existence of the cause in the subject, like the existence of accidents, then the debate remains—the opponent's claim is disputed. Second, if it means the subject being described by the cause (attribution), then even a non-existent can be attributed to an existent subject, like Zayd being described as blind. Blindness is a non-existent attribute (absence of sight) but Zayd is still attributed with it. Therefore, in this sense, a non-existent can be a cause that 'subsists' in the subject by attribution. This shows that the condition of subsistence does not necessarily require the cause to be existent in the first sense.

Key Terms

الوجود
الاتصاف
العدم
العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many students assume that 'subsistence' (qiyam) always entails the existence of the attribute in the subject.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'subsistence' can also mean mere attribution (ittisaf), which does not require the attribute to exist. A non-existent like blindness can be attributed to Zayd.

Checkpoint

B) The attribution of the subject to the cause
C) The non-existence of the cause
D) The necessity of the judgment

Key Takeaway
The condition that a cause must subsist in its subject can be satisfied by attribution alone, allowing non-existent attributes to be causes.

Does the Cause of Necessity Need to Be Existent?
Dialectical argument on cause existence and state theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether the cause (ʿilla) must be existent to necessitate a judgment, including the counter-argument using the state (ḥāl) of knowledge.

Core Idea
A cause can be a non-existent state, like knowledge causing knowledge of the knower.

In the 'Third Aspect' of the discussion on causality, the opponent argues that since necessity (ījāb) is a positive attribute, its bearer must be existent. The defender responds that both opposites can be absent in external existence, paving the way for a state to serve as cause. A counter-argument from the theory of states (aḥwāl) then tests this claim: knowledge causes one to be a knower, yet the knowledge itself is a state, not an existent. This lesson unpacks the logic of this exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source once to get the rhythm, then the literal translation. Note the dialectical structure: opponent's claim, response, counter-argument.
The passage uses parentheses from the original text; treat them as part of the argumentation.
2
Understand the opponent's main argument
Identify why the opponent thinks the cause must be existent. Notice the reasoning: necessity is a positive attribute, its opposite is non-existent, so the cause must be existent to possess it.
What is the 'opposite' (naqīḍ) they refer to?
3
Analyze the response and the counter-argument
The response denies that necessity requires existence in external reality; the two opposites can both be absent. Then the counter-argument from knowledge: what are the three possibilities for the cause of 'being a knower'? Trace each.
Pay attention to the third possibility: knowledge as a state (ḥāl), which is non-existent.
4
Connect to the broader theory of states
The counter-argument shows that a non-existent state can be a cause. Reflect on how this supports the theory of states (aḥwāl) in kalām.
How does this relate to divine attributes?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الثالث) : العلة موجبة للحكم، و الإيجاب صفة ثبوتية؛ لأن نقيضه) وهو اللاإيجاب (عدمي) لصدقه على المعدومات، فإذن لا بد أن تكون العلة موجودة ليمكن اتصافها بالإيجاب الوجودي، قلنا : قد عرفت ما فيه وهو أن النقيضين يجوز ارتفاعهما بحسب الوجود الخارجي، دون الصدق . ( فإن قيل : على سبيل المعارضة إن العلم يوجب كونه عالماً باتفاق مثبتي الأحوال، فنقول : ( الموجب للعالمية إما وجود العلم فيكون كل وجود كذلك )، لاتحاد مسمى الوجود في الكل، هذا خلف . ( أو العلم مع الوجود فتتركب العلة، وهو باطل اتفاقاً) من القائلين بالحال . ( أو العلم ) أي كونه عالماً (وأنه حال فليس بموجود). فثبت أن العلة قد لا تكون موجودةً . ( قلنا : ) الموجب للعالمية
The third aspect: The cause necessitates the judgment, and necessity (ijab) is a positive attribute; because its opposite, which is non-necessity, is non-existent, since it is true of non-existents. Therefore, the cause must be existent so that it can be described by existential necessity. We said: You have already known what is in it, which is that the two opposites may both be elevated (i.e., false) in terms of external existence, not in terms of truth. (For if it is said, by way of counter-argument, that knowledge causes one to be a knower by agreement of the affirmers of states, we say: (The cause of being a knower is either the existence of knowledge, then every existence would be like that, due to the unicity of the meaning of existence in all, this is a circular argument. (Or knowledge together with existence, then the cause would be composite, which is universally invalid) according to those who affirm states. (Or knowledge) i.e., being a knower (and it is a state, so it is not existent). So it is established that the cause may not be existent. (We said:) The cause of being a knower
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents the third aspect (al-wajh al-thalith) of the argument. The opponent argues that the cause necessitates (yujib) a judgment, and this necessity is a positive attribute (sifa thubutiyya); its opposite, non-necessity, is non-existent. Therefore, the cause must be existent so it can be described by the positive attribute of necessity. The response is that the two opposites (existence and non-existence of the attribute) can both be false in external existence, i.e., both can be absent, so the necessity can be attributed even if the cause is non-existent in external reality, as long as it is true at the level of state. Then a counter-argument is presented: knowledge causes one to be a knower, by agreement of those who affirm states (muthbiti al-ahwal). The response examines four possibilities for what causes being a knower: (1) the existence of knowledge, but then every existence would be equally a cause, which is absurd; (2) knowledge together with existence, making the cause composite, which is invalid; (3) knowledge itself as a state (hal), which is not existent; thus the cause may not be existent. The last part is cut off, but the implication is that the cause of being a knower is the state of knowledge, which is not existent, so the cause need not be existent.

Correction: The passage shows that the two opposites of existence and non-existence can both be absent in external reality; thus necessity can be attributed to a non-existent state, which is not an existent entity.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
A non-existent state, such as knowledge, can serve as the cause of a judgment, disproving the necessity for the cause to be existent.

Two Meanings of Subsistence Resolve the Objection
Objection that a cause must subsist in the subject, and the answer distinguishing two senses of 'subsistence'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of 'subsistence' (qiyām) and how this distinction resolves the objection that privations cannot be causes.

Core Idea
The term 'subsistence' has two meanings; only one allows privations to be causes.

In this passage, the author addresses a key objection against the possibility of privations serving as causes. The objector insists that a cause must subsist in the subject, which seems impossible for a non-existent privation. The response clarifies that 'subsistence' can mean either existence in the subject or being described by the attribute, and the latter applies to privations. Let's see how this works.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first sentence: the objector claims a cause must subsist in a substrate, which a privation cannot do.
This objection targets privative causes.
2
Identify the core ambiguity
The answer points out that 'subsistence' is ambiguous. Look for the two meanings introduced by 'إن أردت... وإن أردت'.
3
Understand the two meanings
The first meaning: existence in the subject (like accidents). The second: the subject being described by the attribute.
The second meaning allows a privation to subsist.
4
Apply to the example
Zayd is described by blindness—a privation. This shows a privation can 'subsist' in the second sense.
5
Draw the conclusion
The objection fails because it assumes only the first meaning. The answer shows privations can be causes under the second meaning.
This resolves the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: شرط العلة قيامها بالمحل الذي يوجب له الحكم، ولا يتصور في العدم قيامه بمحل حتى يوجب له حكماً ثبوتياً. قلنا: إن أردت بالقيام وجود الأمر الذي هو العلة بالمحل، كوجود الأعراض الموجودة بمحالها، ففيه النزاع؛ كلامك حينئذ هو أن العلة يجب أن تكون صفة موجودة قائمة بمحل الحكم. وإن أردت بالقيام اتصاف المحل بالأمر الذي هو العلة، فقد يتصف المحل الموجود بالعدمي، كاتصاف زيد بالعمى، فجاز أن تكون العلة عدمية قائمة بمحلها بهذا المعنى.
The second aspect: The condition of the cause is its subsistence (qiyām) in the substrate that necessitates the judgment for it, and it is inconceivable for a privation to subsist in a substrate so as to necessitate a positive judgment for it. We say: If by 'subsistence' you mean the existence of the attribute that is the cause in the substrate, like the existence of accidents existing in their substrates, then there is dispute in that; your statement then is that the cause must be an existent attribute subsisting in the substrate of the judgment. But if by 'subsistence' you mean the substrate's being described by the attribute that is the cause, then an existent substrate may be described by a privation, such as Zayd's being described by blindness; therefore, it is possible for the cause to be a privation subsisting in its substrate in this sense.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against the possibility of privations being causes, along with the response. The objector argues that a cause must 'subsist' in the subject—i.e., it must be an existent attribute inherent in the subject—because a privation cannot be said to subsist in anything and thus cannot necessitate a positive judgment. The answer clarifies the ambiguity in the term 'subsistence'. If 'subsistence' means the cause's existence in the subject, the objector is begging the question because existence of the cause is exactly what is disputed. If 'subsistence' means the subject's being described by the attribute, then a privation like blindness can indeed describe a subject (e.g., Zayd is blind). Hence, privations can function as causes in this sense, and the objection fails.

القيام
العدمي
المحل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'subsistence' only means existence in a subject.
Correction: The passage shows it can also mean being described by an attribute, which allows privations to be causes.

Checkpoint
What two meanings of 'subsistence' (قيام) does the response distinguish?
A) Existence and non-existence
B) Being described by an attribute and causing a judgment
C) Existence in the subject and being described by the attribute
D) Being a privation and being an accident

Key Takeaway
The objection that privations cannot be causes because they don't subsist in subjects fails once 'subsistence' is understood as being described by an attribute rather than existing in the subject.

The Objection on Cause's Necessitation and the Answer
Objection that a cause must be existent because it necessitates, and the answer about contradictories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that a cause must exist to necessitate is refuted by distinguishing between external existence and truth.

Core Idea
Necessitation does not require existence; truth can hold without external reality.

This lesson presents the third objection against privations as causes. The objector argues that since a cause necessitates, it must have the positive attribute of necessitation, hence it must exist. The answer uses a logical distinction between existence and truth to refute this. Let us examine this precise exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the structure: the objector states a premise, then a conclusion; the answer begins with 'قلنا' and provides a counterargument.
Pay attention to the key terms: العلة, الإيجاب, النقيضين, الصدق.
2
Understand the objector's reasoning
The objector says: Necessitation (ījāb) is a positive attribute because its opposite is a privation true of non-existents. Therefore the cause must be existent to have this positive attribute. Understand why this seems compelling.
The objector assumes that a positive attribute requires an existent subject.
3
Analyze the answer
The answer says: You already know that two contradictories can both be non-existent externally but not in truth. This means 'necessitation' does not need to be an existent attribute; it can be a conceptual relation. So the cause can be a privation.
The key is the distinction between external existence (الوجود الخارجي) and truth (الصدق).
4
Reflect on the defense of privations
Consider how this answer protects the position that privations can be causes. The attribute of necessitation does not force the cause to be existent. This is a subtle logical move.
This objection and answer illustrate the importance of careful distinctions in kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث: العلة موجبة للحكم، والإيجاب صفة ثبوتية؛ لأن نقيضه وهو اللاإيجاب عدمي لصدقه على المعدومات، فلا بد أن تكون العلة موجودة ليمكن اتصافها بالإيجاب الوجودي. قلنا: قد عرفت ما فيه، وهو أن النقيضين يجوز ارتفاعهما بحسب الوجود الخارجي دون الصدق.
The third aspect: The cause necessitates the judgment, and necessitation (ījāb) is a positive attribute, because its opposite, non-necessitation, is a privation, being true of non-existents. Therefore the cause must be existent so that it can be described by existent necessitation. We say: You have already seen what is in it, namely that two contradictories may both be non-existent with respect to external existence but not with respect to truth.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This is the third objection against privations being causes. The objector argues that a cause must 'necessitate' the judgment, and 'necessitation' is a positive attribute (since its negation is a privation true of non-existents). Therefore, the cause itself must be existent to bear this positive attribute. The answer is concise: the discussion earlier (in the context of contradictories) already addressed this—two contradictories can both be absent in external existence, even though in truth one must be true. That is, the attribute of necessitation does not require the cause to be existent; the cause could be a privation, and the 'necessitation' is understood as a relation that does not entail actual existence. Thus the objection is refuted.

Key Terms

الإيجاب
النقيضين
الصدق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a cause necessitates, it must have the attribute of necessitation, which is positive, so the cause must exist.
Correction: The answer shows that necessitation can be a truth about a relation without requiring the cause to exist as a positive entity; the two contradictories (necessitation and non-necessitation) can both be absent externally, so the cause does not need to bear an existent attribute.

Checkpoint

B) Contradictories can both be non-existent externally while still being true
C) The cause is not required to have attributes
D) Privation cannot be described by necessity

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because necessitation does not require the cause to exist; it can be a conceptual relation, as two contradictories may both be absent externally yet one still be true.

The Cause Can Be a Non-Existent State
Counter-objection that knowledge makes one knowledgeable, with the response showing the cause can be a state.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the proponents of states (aḥwāl) defend the possibility of a non-existent cause through the case of knowledge making one knowledgeable.

Core Idea
A cause need not be existent; it can be a state.

This passage tackles a central objection against the Ashʿarī doctrine of states (aḥwāl). The opponent argues that if knowledge makes one knowledgeable, then the cause must be existent. The response systematically examines three possibilities and shows that only a non-existent state can serve as the cause, thereby defending the coherence of states as real but non-existential attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
First read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. The opponent's point is based on agreement that knowledge causes being knowledgeable.
Notice the phrase 'على سبيل المعارضة' indicates a counterexample.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The response lists three possible necessitators: 1) existence of knowledge, 2) knowledge with existence, 3) knowledge as a state. Mark each in the text.
The third option is the key: 'العلم أي كونه عالماً وأنه حال'.
3
Understand why the first two are invalid
The first leads to absurdity (all existents would cause knowledge). The second would make the cause composite, invalid by consensus.
Think about why a composite cause is problematic for the proponents of states.
4
Grasp the conclusion about non-existent cause
The only remaining option is that the cause is a state (ḥāl), which is not existent. This shows the cause may be non-existent.
The conclusion is stated: 'فثبت أن العلة قد لا تكون موجودة'.
5
Analyze the final answer
The response ends by clarifying that the actual necessitator is existent knowledge, but this is distinct from 'knowledge with existence' and from 'being knowledge' (the state).
This distinction resolves the equivocation in the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: على سبيل المعارضة إن العلم يوجب كونه عالماً باتفاق مثبتي الأحوال. فنقول: الموجب للعالمية إما وجود العلم، فيكون كل وجود كذلك لاتحاد مسمى الوجود في الكل، هذا خلف. أو العلم مع الوجود، فتتركب العلة، وهو باطل اتفاقاً من القائلين بالحال. أو العلم أي كونه عالماً وأنه حال فليس بموجود. فثبت أن العلة قد لا تكون موجودةً. قلنا: الموجب للعالمية هو العلم الذي هو موجود، وفرق بينه وبين العلم مع الوجود وبينه وبين كونه علماً.
For if it is said, by way of counterexample, that knowledge necessitates being knowledgeable by agreement of the affirmers of states (aḥwāl). Then we say: The necessitator of being knowledgeable is either the existence of knowledge, then every existence would be like that because the concept of existence is the same in all, and this is a reductio ad absurdum; or knowledge along with existence, then the cause becomes composed, which is invalid by agreement among the proponents of the state; or knowledge—i.e., being knowledgeable—and it is a state (ḥāl), so it is not existent. Thus it is established that the cause may not be existent. We say: The necessitator of being knowledgeable is knowledge which exists, and there is a difference between it and knowledge together with existence, and between it and being knowledge (which is the state).
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents a counter-objection and its answer. The opponent argues from the agreed-upon fact that knowledge makes one knowledgeable, implying that the cause (knowledge) is existent. The response first examines the possibilities for what the necessitator (موجب) of being knowledgeable is. It cannot be the existence of knowledge because then any existent would make one knowledgeable, which is absurd. It cannot be knowledge plus existence because that would make the cause composite, which is invalid by consensus among advocates of states. So it must be knowledge insofar as it is a state (ḥāl), which is not existent in the external world but is a stable attribute. Thus the cause can be non-existent. The answer then clarifies: the actual necessitator is knowledge that exists (i.e., the attribute itself), and this is distinct from both 'knowledge with existence' (which is redundant) and 'being knowledge' (which is the state). So the counter-objection fails because it equivocates between these meanings.

Key Terms
العلة
الحال
العلم
العالمية
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A cause must always be an external existent entity; a non-existent thing cannot cause anything.
Correction: The passage shows that a state (ḥāl), though non-existent, can be the cause of an attribute like being knowledgeable, as the only non-absurd possibility among the three.

Checkpoint
What is the third possibility considered for the necessitator of being knowledgeable?
A) The existence of knowledge
B) Knowledge combined with existence
C) Knowledge as a state (ḥāl) that is not existent
D) The subject's essence

Key Takeaway
The cause of an attribute can be a non-existent state, as demonstrated by the case of knowledge making one knowledgeable, defending the Ashʿarī doctrine of states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Regularity and Reflexivity of the Rational Cause
Definition and defense of reflexivity of the rational cause for eternal states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two properties of the rational cause (regularity and reflexivity) and why the Asha'ira affirm reflexivity for eternal states against the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
A cause that does not always produce its effect is not a cause at all.

This lesson examines two essential features of the rational cause: regularity (its existence necessitates the effect) and reflexivity (its absence necessitates the absence of the effect). While both are accepted for temporal matters, a key dispute arises over God's eternal attributes. The Asha'ira argue that reflexivity must hold for all states, leading to a dilemma for the Mu'tazila who deny it.

Guided Path
1
Understand regularity
Regularity means that whenever the cause exists, its judgment necessarily exists. This is undisputed among all affirmers of states.
2
Understand reflexivity
Reflexivity means that whenever the cause is absent, its judgment is absent. This is agreed for temporal states, but disputed for eternal states.
3
Identify the dispute over eternal states
The Asha'ira extend reflexivity to God's eternal attributes, while the Mu'tazila deny it, claiming God can have being-knowledgeable without knowledge.
4
Analyze the dilemma against Mu'tazila
The passage presents two impossible consequences of the Mu'tazila's position: either being-knowledgeable is explained by a non-knowledge attribute (impossible) or it exists without any cause (also impossible).
This dilemma forces the Mu'tazila to accept reflexivity or face irrationality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الرابعة: العلة العقلية التي كلامنا فيها دون العلة الشرعية مطردة، يستلزم وجودها وجود حكمها، أي كلما وجدت العلة وجد الحكم على سبيل اللزوم وامتناع التخلف. وهذا أعني وجوب الاطراد مما لا خلاف فيه أصلاً بين مثبتي الأحوال. ومنعكسه يستلزم عدمها عدم حكمها، أي كلما انتفت العلة انتفى الحكم، ولا خلاف فيه في الأحوال الحادثة، فإنه مهما انتفى العلم والقدرة على واحد منا انتفى عنه العالمية والقادرية من مثبتي الأحوال. وأوجبه الأصحاب في الأحوال القديمة أيضاً، فلم يجوزوا عالمية الباري وقادريته بلا علم وقدرة. ومنعه المعتزلة وقالوا: لله تعالى عالمية وقادرية بلا علم وقدرة. ويلزمهم أحد أمرين: إما تعليل العالمية بغير العلم، كالقدرة مثلاً، وهو ضروري البطلان؛ إذ نعلم قطعاً أن غير العلم من الصفات، سواء كانت مشروطة بالحياة أو لا، لا توجب كون محلها عالماً. أو ثبوتها من غير علة، وهو أيضاً باطل؛ لأنه إذا جاز ثبوت العالمية بلا علم ولا علة مغايرة له، جاز أن تكون العالمية الثابتة مع وجود العلم غير معللة به، كما كانت ثابتة مع عدمه، وهذا خروج عن المعقول، ومخالف لما هو مسلم عند الخصم.
The fourth issue: The rational cause (al-‘illa al-‘aqliyya) which is our subject here, not the legal cause, is regular (muṭṭarida): its existence entails the existence of its judgment, i.e., whenever the cause exists, the judgment exists by way of necessity and impossibility of non-occurrence. This—I mean the obligation of regularity—is undisputed among the affirmers of states. Its converse (mun‘akis) entails that its non-existence entails the non-existence of its judgment, i.e., whenever the cause is absent, the judgment is absent, and there is no dispute about this in the case of temporal states; for whenever knowledge and power are absent from one of us, being-knowledgeable and being-powerful are absent from him according to the affirmers of states. The Companions (al-aṣḥāb) also affirmed it for eternal states, so they did not allow the Being-knowledgeable and Being-powerful of the Creator without knowledge and power. The Mu‘tazila denied it and said: Allah has Being-knowledgeable and Being-powerful without knowledge and power. They are compelled to one of two things: either to explain Being-knowledgeable by something other than knowledge, such as power, which is necessarily invalid because we know with certainty that other than knowledge from among the attributes, whether conditioned by life or not, does not make its subject knowledgeable; or it is established without a cause, which is also invalid; because if it is permissible for Being-knowledgeable to be established without knowledge and without a cause distinct from it, then it would be permissible for Being-knowledgeable established together with the existence of knowledge not to be caused by it, just as it was established without it. This is a deviation from reason and contrary to what is accepted by the opponent.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage defines two essential properties of the rational cause: regularity (al-‘illa muṭṭarida) meaning that whenever the cause exists, its judgment necessarily exists without fail, and reflexivity (mun‘akisa) meaning that whenever the cause is absent, its judgment is absent. The author notes that regularity is uncontroversial among those who affirm states (aḥwāl). Reflexivity is also agreed upon for temporal states (aḥwāl ḥāditha), e.g., when knowledge is absent, being-knowledgeable is absent. However, the Asha‘ira (al-aṣḥāb) extend reflexivity to eternal states, so they hold that God's being-knowledgeable requires an eternal attribute of knowledge. The Mu‘tazila deny this, claiming God has being-knowledgeable without any attribute of knowledge (since they say attributes are identical to the essence). The passage then presents a dilemma for the Mu‘tazila: either they explain being-knowledgeable by a different attribute like power—but that is absurd because we know power does not make one knowledgeable—or they say it exists without any cause at all. The latter is also absurd because if it can exist without a cause now, it could exist without a cause when knowledge exists, which would break causality altogether. Thus, reflexivity must hold for eternal states, implying distinct attributes in God.

Key Terms
العلة العقلية
المطردة
المنعكسة
الأحوال الحادثة
الأحوال القديمة
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila deny reflexivity entirely for all states.
Correction: The Mu'tazila actually accept reflexivity for temporal states; the dispute is only over eternal states.

Checkpoint
What is the second necessary consequence (لازم) that the passage imposes on the Mu'tazila for denying reflexivity in eternal states?
A) They must say God's attributes are identical to His essence
B) They must either explain being-knowledgeable by a non-knowledge attribute or accept existence without a cause
C) They must deny temporal states
D) They must accept the existence of knowledge as a distinct entity

Key Takeaway
Regularity and reflexivity are essential properties of the rational cause; the Mu'tazila's denial of reflexivity for eternal states leads to absurd consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah's knowledge is a real, distinct attribute that causes Him to know you personally? How does this change the way you relate to Him in your daily life?
?
When you struggle with doubt or a trial, do you ever question whether Allah's power is always sufficient to bring about His will? This lesson shows that a cause must always produce its effect. Will you commit to trusting that His power is always effective, even when you don't see immediate results?
?
The Mu'tazila rejected real attributes out of a desire to protect tawhid. But the passage warns that this leads to absurdities. Are there any beliefs you hold that, on closer inspection, might lead to contradictions? Will you examine them honestly, even if it means changing long-held views?

Definition and Properties of the Rational Cause: Transitivity and Reflection
The author defines the rational cause and states its two essential properties: transitivity and reflection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the rational cause and its two essential properties: transitivity (ittiraad) and reflection (in'ikaas), and how these apply to both created and eternal states.

Core Idea
The rational cause is transitive and reflective: its existence guarantees its effect, and its absence guarantees the absence of the effect.

This lesson introduces the rational cause, which is the subject of theological reasoning about divine attributes. Unlike the legal cause, the rational cause operates with strict necessity. The author, a prominent Ash'ari theologian, defines this cause and establishes its two fundamental properties: transitivity and reflection. Understanding these properties is crucial for grasping the Ash'ari position on divine attributes and their relationship to God's essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall meaning. Note the author's focus on distinguishing rational from legal cause.
2
Identify the two properties
The passage separately discusses transitivity and reflection. Find the defining phrases for each. Mark how each is described as a necessary relation.
Notice that the Arabic words 'مطردة' and 'منعكسه' are key.
3
Examine the scope of agreement
Transitivity is undisputed among all proponents of states. Reflection is undisputed for created states, but the Ash'aris extend it to eternal states. Contrast with possible opposing views.
Think about why the Mu'tazila might reject reflection for God's attributes.
4
Apply to an example
Consider the states of 'being knowing' and 'being powerful' for a human versus for God. How does reflection play out in each case?
For humans, absence of knowledge implies not being knowing. For God, Ash'aris say the same holds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العلة العقلية التي كلامنا فيها دون العلة الشرعية مطردة، يستلزم وجودها وجود حكمها، أي كلما وجدت العلة وجد الحكم على سبيل اللزوم وامتناع التخلف. وهذا أعني وجوب الاطراد مما لا خلاف فيه أصلا بين مثبتي الأحوال. ومنعكسه يستلزم عدمها عدم حكمها أي كلما انتفت العلة انتفى الحكم، ولا خلاف فيه أي في الانعكاس ووجوبه في الأحوال الحادثة فإنه مهما انتفى العلم والقدرة على واحد منا انتفى عنه العالمية والقادرية من مثبتي الأحوال. وأوجبه الأصحاب في الأحوال القديمة أيضاً، فلم يجوزوا عالمية الباري وقادريته بلا علم وقدرة.
The rational cause which is our subject, not the legal cause, is transitive: its existence necessitates the existence of its effect, meaning whenever the cause exists, the effect exists necessarily without possibility of failure. This necessity of transitivity is absolutely undisputed among the proponents of states. Its converse (reflection) necessitates that its absence necessitates the absence of its effect, meaning whenever the cause is absent, the effect is absent, and there is no dispute regarding this reflection and its necessity in the case of created states—for whenever knowledge and power are absent from one of us, his being knowing and being powerful become absent according to the proponents of states. And the Companions (Ash'aris) also required this reflection for the eternal states, so they did not permit the possibility of the Creator being knowing and powerful without knowledge and power.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage defines the rational cause (al-illa al-ʾaqliyya) and explains its two necessary properties: transitivity (al-iṭṭirād) and reflection (al-inʿikās). The author first states that the cause is transitive, meaning its existence necessarily implies the existence of its effect. This transitivity is undisputed among proponents of states (muthbitī al-aḥwāl). Then he discusses reflection: the absence of the cause necessitates the absence of the effect. For created states, this is also undisputed; for example, if a human lacks knowledge and power, he cannot be described as knowing and powerful. However, for eternal attributes, the Ash'aris require reflection as well, so they do not allow God to have the states of being knowing and being powerful without the distinct attributes of knowledge and power. This sets up the disagreement with the Mu'tazila.

Correction: The Ash'aris insist that reflection applies to eternal states as well, meaning God's states of being knowing and being powerful are necessarily dependent on the attributes of knowledge and power.

Key Takeaway
The rational cause is defined by two essential properties: transitivity (its existence guarantees its effect) and reflection (its absence guarantees absence of its effect), and the Ash'aris require both for created and eternal states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the rational cause's absence guarantees the absence of its effect, how does that challenge your reliance on worldly means—like health, wealth, or people—as if they had inherent causal power? Will you trust more in the ultimate Cause today?
?
The Ash‘arīs insisted that even God's attributes (knowledge, power) are bound by reflection: if knowledge were absent, being knowing would be absent. Does your conception of God treat His attributes as truly constitutive of His perfection, or do you sometimes imagine them as removable? How can you deepen your awe of His perfections?
?
Transitivity means that if God wills something, it must occur. When you face delays or disappointments, do you doubt the connection between His will and its effect? What would it mean for you to live with absolute certainty that nothing can oppose His decree?

Refutation of the Mu'tazila on the States of God
A dilemma refuting the Mu'tazila denial of reflection for eternal attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dilemma the Mu'tazila face when they deny that God's eternal attributes are reflected in His states (al-ahwal).

Core Idea
Denying reflection forces an impossible choice between an ungrounded cause and no cause at all.

The Mu'tazila argue that God can be knowing and powerful without having distinct attributes of knowledge and power existing as separate entities. This passage presents a devastating dilemma: either the state of being knowing is caused by something impossible, or it has no cause at all—both of which are absurd. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila position
Read the Mu'tazila claim: God has being knowing and being powerful without knowledge and power. This is the starting point of the refutation.
This is a key heterodox view in Islamic theology.
2
Identify the first horn of the dilemma
The first possibility is that the state of being knowing is caused by something other than knowledge, such as power. The passage declares this 'necessarily false' because no attribute other than knowledge makes its locus 'knowing'.
This is a direct appeal to intuitive certainty (darura).
3
Identify the second horn of the dilemma
The second possibility is that the state exists without any cause at all. This is also false because if a state can be uncaused in one case, it could be uncaused even when a cause is present, which is irrational.
This leads to a regress or absurdity.
4
Understand the concluding implication
If the Mu'tazila allow being knowing without a cause when knowledge is absent, they cannot consistently require a cause when knowledge is present. This refutes their position.
The argument shows that their view leads to a breakdown of causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنعه المعتزلة وقالوا لله تعالى عالمية وقادرية بلا علم وقدرة. ويلزمهم أحد أمرين: إما تعليل العالمية بغير العلم كالقدرة مثلا وهو ضروري البطلان؛ إذ نعلم قطعاً أن غير العلم من الصفات سواء كانت مشروطة بالحياة أو لا، لا توجب كون محلها عالماً، أو ثبوتها من غير علة، وهو أيضاً باطل؛ لأنه إذا جاز ثبوت العالمية بلا علم ولا علة مغايرة له، جاز أن تكون العالمية الثابتة مع وجود العلم غير معللة به، كما كانت ثابتة مع عدمه، وهذا خروج عن المعقول، ومخالف لما هو مسلم عند الخصم. فجاز في المقارنة في العلم أي فجاز الثبوت بلا علة في العالمية المقارنة لوجود العلم، فلا تكون معللة به.
And the Mu'tazila denied this (reflection) and said: For God there is being knowing and being powerful without knowledge and power. They are forced to one of two things: either attributing being knowing to something other than knowledge, such as power, which is necessarily false; for we know definitively that other than knowledge from among the attributes, whether conditioned by life or not, does not necessitate that its locus be knowing; or affirming it (being knowing) without a cause, which is also false; because if it is permissible for being knowing to be established without knowledge and without a cause distinct from it, then it would be permissible for being knowing that is concomitant with the existence of knowledge not to be caused by it, just as it was established in its absence. This is a departure from reason and contrary to what is conceded by the opponent. So it is permissible in the concomitant in knowledge—that is, it is permissible for being knowing that is concomitant with the existence of knowledge to be established without a cause, so it is not caused by it.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila's position that God can have the states of being knowing and being powerful without the attributes of knowledge and power existing as distinct entities. The author argues that they are forced into one of two impossible consequences. First, if they say the state of being knowing is caused by something other than knowledge, such as power, that is necessarily false because we know intuitively that no other attribute makes its locus knowing. Second, if they say the state exists without any cause at all, that is also false because if a state can exist without a cause, then the state that coexists with the cause would not necessarily be caused by it, which is irrational and contradicts what the opponent concedes. The concluding gloss emphasizes that if it is allowed for being knowing to exist without a cause when knowledge is absent, then it could also exist without being caused by knowledge when knowledge is present.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
العالمية
القادرية
العلة
ضروري البطلان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila think that God can have states like being knowing without a distinct attribute of knowledge, and that this is a tenable position.
Correction: This passage shows that such a view leads to an impossible dilemma: either the state is caused by something that cannot be a cause (like power) or it exists without any cause, both of which are absurd. Therefore, the existence of distinct attributes is necessary.

Checkpoint
What are the two impossible consequences the Mu'tazila face according to this refutation?
A) They must deny either God's knowledge or power.
B) They must either attribute being knowing to a non-knowledge cause or allow being knowing without any cause.
C) They must either affirm anthropomorphism or deny creation.
D) They must either accept Ash'arism or reject reason.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's denial of reflection for God's eternal attributes forces them into an absurd dilemma, thereby proving the necessity of distinct attributes as causes of states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Commit today to trace one belief to its logical end.

This passage shows that denying a cause for a property leads to irrationality. In your own life, when you fall into a wrong action, do you try to justify it without a cause—blaming circumstances, 'just happened'—rather than owning the cause within you? What is one sin you have been excusing as 'without cause' that you need to attribute to your own nafs and repent from?

How will you translate your belief in Allah's knowledge into a concrete action today?

Distinguishing Cause from Effect Despite Mutual Transitivity
The author refutes an objection about distinguishing cause from effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that cause and effect can both be transitive and reflected, but reason distinguishes them through necessary knowledge.

Core Idea
Not every transitive-reflected thing is a cause; reason knows the direction of causation.

This lesson tackles a key objection in causal reasoning: if cause and effect are mutually entailing, how can we tell them apart? The author clarifies that transitivity and reflection are conditions of a cause but not sufficient. Then he shows that necessary rational knowledge distinguishes cause from effect, using the example of knowledge and being knowing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the author first states a general rule, then anticipates an objection, then answers it.
Pay attention to the logical flow: general claim → objection → answer.
2
Identify the objection
The objector argues that if both cause and effect are transitive and reflected, they are mutually entailing, so we cannot tell which is which. Find where this objection begins and understand its force.
The objection is signaled by 'لا يقال'.
3
Understand the answer using necessary reason
The author answers that the cause is distinguished by 'ḍarūrat al-ʿaql' (necessity of reason). He gives the example of knowledge: we know necessarily that knowledge causes its subject to be knowing, not the reverse. Reflect on why this answer overcomes the objection.
This is a key epistemological point in Islamic kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن كل علة مطردة منعكسة وليس كل مطرد منعكس علة، كالمعلول والمتضايفين، وذلك لأن الاطراد والانعكاس شرط العلة، وليس يلزم من وجود الشرط وجود المشروط. لا يقال: إذا كان المعلول مطرداً منعكساً كالعلة كان بينهما ملازمة من الطرفين فيما إذا تمايز العلة عن غيرها، وكيف يعرف أن العلم مثلاً علة للعالمية دون العكس مع تلازمهما ثبوتاً وانتفاء؟ لأنا نقول: تمتاز العلة عن غيرها بضرورة العقل، فإنا نعلم علماً ضرورياً أن العلم يوجب كون محله عالماً إيجاباً يصدق معه وجد العلم فأوجب كون محله عالماً، ولا يصدق عكسه، وهو أن يقال: ثبت كون المحل عالماً فأوجب له العلم.
Know that every cause is transitive and reflected, but not every transitive-reflected thing is a cause, such as the effect and correlatives; because transitivity and reflection are conditions of the cause, and the existence of the condition does not necessitate the existence of the conditioned. It cannot be said: if the effect is transitive and reflected like the cause, then there is mutual entailment between them when the cause is distinguished from other things, and how can one know that knowledge, for example, is the cause of being knowing and not the reverse, given their mutual entailment in existence and absence? For we say: The cause is distinguished from other things by the necessity of reason; for we know by necessary knowledge that knowledge necessitates that its locus be knowing in a way that is true that when knowledge exists, it necessitates its locus being knowing, but its converse is not true, i.e., it cannot be said: the locus is established as knowing so it necessitates knowledge for it.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection: if both cause and effect are transitive and reflected (i.e., mutually entail each other), how can we tell which is the cause and which is the effect? The author first clarifies that not every transitive-reflected thing is a cause; the effect and correlatives are also transitive and reflected. Transitivity and reflection are conditions of the cause but not sufficient to make something a cause. The objection states that if the effect is also transitive and reflected, there would be mutual entailment, so we could not distinguish cause from effect. The answer is that we distinguish by the necessity of reason (ḍarūrat al-ʿaql): we know necessarily that knowledge causes its subject to be knowing, but not the reverse. This necessary knowledge is immediate and does not require proof.

Key Terms
المعلول
المتضايفين
شرط العلة
ضرورة العقل
علم ضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that mutual entailment (both sides are transitive and reflected) makes cause and effect logically indistinguishable.
Correction: The passage clarifies that even with mutual entailment, reason necessarily knows the direction of causation, as in the case of knowledge and being knowing.

Checkpoint
How does the author say we distinguish cause from effect when both are transitive and reflected?
A) By divine revelation
B) By consensus of scholars
C) By necessity of reason
D) By empirical observation

Key Takeaway
Reason distinguishes cause from effect, even when they are mutually entailing, through immediate necessary knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How can you train yourself to rely more on that immediate rational insight rather than on superficial appearances?

What would change if you used your God-given reason to trace those symptoms back to their real source and addressed that root instead?
