The Conditions of Causality: Coextensiveness, Reciprocity, and Rational Necessity
Author states the coextensiveness and reciprocity condition for cause and distinguishes by rational necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why every cause must be coextensive and reciprocal, and how rational necessity distinguishes a true cause from a mere concurrent factor.

Core Idea
Not every coextensive reciprocal is a cause; reason is the final arbiter.

This passage tackles a foundational rule in kalam reasoning about causality. The author explains that while a cause must always accompany its effect and be unique to it, the mere fact that two things always occur together does not prove causation. The real distinction lies in rational necessity (ḍarūrat al-ʿaql), which tells us that knowledge, for example, makes a subject knowing, not the reverse.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the full Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the technical terms for 'coextensive' (muṭṭarid) and 'reciprocal' (munʿakis).
The passage includes an objection and answer, so watch for the shift in voice.
2
Identify the main rule
The author states: 'Every cause is coextensive and reciprocal, and not every coextensive reciprocal is a cause.' Understand that coextensiveness and reciprocity are necessary but not sufficient conditions.
The examples given are the effect itself and correlatives.
3
Analyze the objection and answer
The objector asks: if both cause and effect are coextensive reciprocal, how do we tell them apart? The answer is by rational necessity—we know directly that knowledge causes worldliness, not the opposite.
The author adds that this knowledge may also come from another proof.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this distinction matters: without rational insight, mere correlation could be mistaken for causality. The author safeguards against that error.
This connects to the broader kalam project of establishing reliable causal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كان اللازم من عدم الانعكاس جواز أن يكون الحكم المقارن للعلة غير ثابت بها، قال الأصحاب: كل علة لا تكون منعكسة فهي غير مطردة أيضاً. وأما قوله: ( وسيأتي تمامه في بحث الصفات) فإشارة إلى ما ذهبوا إليه من أن الأحكام القديمة واجبة والواجب لا يعلل، سواء وجدت العلة أو لم توجد وإلى جوابه الذي فصله هناك. ( واعلم ) ( أن كل علة مطردة منعكسة وليس كل مطرد منعكس علة، كالمعلول والمتضايفين)، وذلك لأن الاطراد والانعكاس شرط العلة، وليس يلزم من وجود الشرط وجود المشروط . ( لا يقال ) : إذا كان المعلول مطرداً منعكساً، كالعلة كان بينهما ملازمة من الطرفين فيما إذا تمايز العلة عن غيرها)، وكيف يعرف أن العلم مثلاً علة للعالمية دون العكس مع تلازمهما ثبوتاً وانتفاء؟ ( ; لأنا نقول ( تمتاز العلة عن غيرها ( بضرورة العقل)، فإنا نعلم علماً ضرورياً أن العلم يوجب كون محله عالماً إيجاباً يصدق معه، وجد العلم، فأوجب كون محله عالماً، ولا يصدق عكسه، وهو أن يقال : ثبت كون المحل عالماً فأوجب له العلم، ونعلم بالضرورة أيضاً، أو بدلیل آخر يرشدنا إلى تمييز العلة عما يشاركها في الاطراد والانعكاس.
And when the necessary consequence of the lack of reciprocity is that it is possible for the judgment concurrent with the cause not to be established by it, the companions said: every cause that is not reciprocal is also not coextensive. And as for his saying: 'And its completion will come in the discussion of attributes,' it is a reference to what they held that the eternal judgments are necessary, and the necessary is not caused, whether the cause exists or not, and to its answer which he detailed there. And know that every cause is coextensive reciprocal, and not every coextensive reciprocal is a cause, like the effect and the two correlatives. That is because coextensiveness and reciprocity are conditions of the cause, and the existence of the condition does not necessitate the existence of the conditioned. It is not said: if the effect is coextensive reciprocal like the cause, then there is mutual implication between them from both sides when the cause is distinguished from others, and how is it known that knowledge, for example, is a cause for worldliness and not the opposite, given their mutual implication in existence and non-existence? (Because we say: the cause is distinguished from others by the necessity of reason). For we know by necessary knowledge that knowledge necessitates its subject being knowing with an necessitation that is true with it, if knowledge exists, so it necessitates its subject being knowing, and its converse is not true, which is to say: the subject being knowing is established so it necessitates knowledge for it. And we also know by necessity, or by another proof that guides us to distinguish the cause from what shares with it in coextensiveness and reciprocity.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the conditions of causality. The author begins by stating that lack of reciprocity implies that a concurrent judgment might not be caused, so the scholars (as-hab) assert that any non-reciprocal cause is also non-coextensive. He then references a discussion about eternal judgments being uncaused. The main point: every cause is coextensive and reciprocal, but not everything coextensive and reciprocal is a cause—examples are the effect itself and correlatives. Coextensiveness and reciprocity are only conditions, not sufficient. An objection is raised: if both cause and effect are coextensive and reciprocal, how do we distinguish them? The answer is by rational necessity (darurat al-aql). We know necessarily that knowledge causes worldliness, not the reverse. This is known either by direct rational intuition or by another proof. The lesson emphasizes that the distinction between cause and non-cause is not merely based on coextensiveness but on a rational insight into the nature of the causal relationship.

المعلول

ضرورة العقل
العالمية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That coextensiveness and reciprocity alone are sufficient to identify a cause.
Correction: The author explicitly says these are only conditions; the real distinction is made by rational necessity, which reveals the direction of dependence.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how is a true cause distinguished from things that are merely coextensive and reciprocal?
A) By coextensiveness
B) By reciprocity
C) By rational necessity
D) By consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
A cause must be coextensive and reciprocal, but these are not enough; rational necessity alone tells us the true direction of causality.

?
The passage says rational necessity can be self-evident or learned from another proof. Think of a belief you hold as certain—is it because it is obvious to your mind, or because you have been taught? Are you willing to question whether your certainty comes from reason itself or from mere repetition?
?
The author distinguishes between cause and effect using an example: knowledge causes worldliness, not the reverse. Which direction aligns with your daily effort?

Causation is not conditional; conditions pertain to existence of cause
Author asserts causation is unconditional and answers objections about life and opposites as existence conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the necessitation of a cause for its effect is unconditional, and how to distinguish between conditions for the existence of the cause and conditions for its causal efficacy.

Core Idea
Causation is unconditional: the effect follows immediately from the cause without needing a condition.

Sanusi tackles a subtle but crucial point: once a cause exists, its effect follows necessarily — no extra condition is needed. An objection based on apparent prerequisites (like life for knowledge) is answered by distinguishing between conditions for the cause's existence and conditions for its causal power. This passage sharpens our understanding of causality in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
Pay attention to the objection and answer structure.
2
Identify the main claim
Sanusi asserts that causation (إيجاب العلة لمعلولها) is not conditional. Find the phrase that states this and note the justification: it is a necessary judgment.
3
Understand the objection
The objection lists conditions like subsistence in a subject, life, and absence of opposites. Why might someone think these are necessary for causation?
The objection is about knowledge's implication for worldliness.
4
Analyse the answer
Sanusi responds by distinguishing between conditions of existence (شروط وجوده) and conditions of efficacy (شروط تأثيره). Understand the difference.
The conditions mentioned are for the existence of knowledge itself, not its causal power.
5
Reflect on the implication
Why does this distinction matter? It preserves the necessary nature of causation once the cause exists, which is essential for kalam arguments about divine attributes.
This connects to broader discussions about causality and divine essence.

المسألة الخامسة : إيجاب العلة لمعلولها لا يكون مشروطاً بشرط اتفاقاً من القائلين بثبوت الحال، وهذا حكم ضروري، فإنه لا يتصور علم بلا عالمية، يعني أنا إذا علمنا قيام العلم بمحل علمنا كونه عالماً بلا توقف على العلم بشيء آخر أصلاً، وهو المراد بقوله : سواء علمنا الشرط، أو وجوده أم لا، فلو كان إيجاب العلم للعالمية مشروطاً بشرط لم يمكن لنا الجزم بالعالمية؛ إلا بعد تصور ذلك الشرط، والتصديق بوجوده. فإن قيل : اقتضاء العلم العالمية مشروط بقيام العلم بالمحل و مشروط أيضاً بالحياة، وانتفاء أضداده أي أضداد العلم. قلنا : هذه شروط وجوده، فإن وجود العلم في نفسه مشروط بهذه الأمور، والكلام في شروط تأثيره وإيجابه للعالمية، والفرق بين شرط وجود العلة وبين شرط اقتضائها لمعلولها بعد وجودها مما لا سترة به.
The fifth question: The necessitation of the cause for its effect is not conditional upon a condition, unanimously among those who affirm the state (hal). This is a necessary judgment. For it is inconceivable that there be knowledge without worldliness. That is, if we know the subsistence of knowledge in a subject, we know that it is knowing without stopping at knowledge of anything else at all. This is the meaning of his saying: whether we know the condition or its existence or not. For if the necessitation of knowledge for worldliness were conditional upon a condition, we could not be certain of worldliness except after conceiving that condition and affirming its existence. If it is said: the implication of knowledge for worldliness is conditional upon the subsistence of knowledge in the subject, and also conditional upon life and the absence of its opposites, i.e., the opposites of knowledge. We say: these are conditions of its existence. For the existence of knowledge in itself is conditional upon these things, and the discussion is about the conditions of its efficacy and its necessitation for worldliness. The difference between the condition of existence of the cause and the condition of its implication for its effect after its existence is not concealed.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth mas'ala (question) regarding causation. The author asserts that the necessitation (ijab) of a cause for its effect is unconditional, according to those who affirm the concept of 'state' (hal). This is a necessary judgment because if we know knowledge exists in a subject, we immediately know that the subject is knowing, without needing any further condition. If causation were conditional, we could never be certain of the effect without first verifying the condition. An objection is raised: knowledge's implication requires conditions like the subject's subsistence, life, and absence of opposites. The answer distinguishes between conditions for the existence of the cause itself and conditions for its efficacy. The conditions mentioned (life, etc.) are prerequisites for the existence of knowledge, not for its causal power. Once knowledge exists, its effect (worldliness) follows necessarily without additional conditions. The author emphasizes that this distinction is obvious.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Confusing conditions for the existence of a cause with conditions for its causal efficacy.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only conditions for existence are required; causation itself is unconditional once the cause exists.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The necessitation of a cause for its effect is unconditional; any required conditions pertain only to the cause's existence, not its causal power.

One Cause Cannot Necessitate Two Separable Effects
Author argues why one cause cannot necessitate two separable effects using logical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a single cause cannot necessitate two rulings if they can exist independently, and how this argument upholds the conditions of causality.

Core Idea
If two effects can separate, one cause cannot necessitate both.

This lesson examines the sixth mas'ala in the discussion of causality: whether one cause can produce two different rulings. The author presents the disagreement and then gives the detailed position. Through a clear example and logical reasoning, he shows that if the rulings are separable, a single cause fails to meet the conditions of causality—either it makes them inseparable or it loses coextensiveness.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the source passage once to get a sense of the argument. Then read the literal translation carefully.
Pay attention to the condition 'if separation is possible'.
2
Identify the condition and example
Find where the author specifies the condition (separation possible from one or both sides) and the example (worldliness by blackness and whiteness).
The example shows two rulings that can exist without each other.
3
Follow the two-part reasoning
The author gives two possibilities: (1) if the cause necessitates both, they become inseparable; (2) if it does not, the cause is non-coextensive. Understand why each leads to contradiction.
This is the core logical argument.
4
Connect to causality conditions
Realize that coextensiveness (always present when cause is present) and reciprocity (effect only from this cause) are required. The argument shows both fail here.
Non-coextensiveness is explicitly mentioned.
5
Formulate the conclusion
State in your own words why one cause cannot necessitate two separable effects.
The conclusion is implicit but clear from the reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة السادسة : لا توجب العلة الواحدة حكمين مختلفين، وقد اختلف فيه، فجوز بعضهم هذا الإيجاب ومنعه آخرون والمختار هو التفصيل الذي أشار إليه بقوله : ( واعلم أنه إن جاز الانفكاك بين الحكمين، إما من جانب واحد، أو من الجانبين ) كالعالمية بالسواد و العالمية بالبياض، فإنهما حكمان يجوز انفكاك كل منهما عن الآخر امتنع تعليلهما بعلة واحدة، وإلا لزم عدم الانفكاك، أو عدم الاطراد، وذلك لأنه إذا وجد تلك العلة فإن وجب ثبوت كل من الحكمين كانا متلازمين والمقدر خلافه. وإن لم يجب بل جاز انتفاء أحدهما ثبوت تلك العلة كانت تلك العلة غير مطردة.
The sixth question: One cause does not necessitate two different rulings, and there is disagreement on it. Some permitted this necessitation, others forbade it, and the chosen is the detail indicated by his saying: 'And know that if separation between the two rulings is possible, either from one side or from both sides' — like worldliness by blackness and worldliness by whiteness, for they are two rulings each of which can be separated from the other — then it is impossible to make them both dependent on one cause. Otherwise, there would follow either non-separation or non-coextensiveness. That is because if that cause exists, then if the establishment of each of the two rulings is necessary, they would be mutually entailing, but the default is otherwise. And if it is not necessary but rather the negation of one of them is possible while the cause exists, then that cause would be non-coextensive.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth mas'ala: whether one cause can necessitate two different effects (ahkam). The author notes disagreement and states that the preferred position is a detailed one. If the two effects can exist separately (infikak) — for example, being knowing by blackness and being knowing by whiteness — then one cause cannot necessitate both. The reasoning: if the cause necessitates both, then they would become inseparable (contradicting the assumption that they can separate). If the cause does not necessitate both, meaning one effect can be absent while the cause is present, then the cause would not be coextensive (mutarid) with its effect. Thus, the cause fails either condition of causality (coextensiveness or reciprocity). This detail shows that the ruling depends on whether the effects are separable.

Key Terms
العلة الواحدة
الحكمان
الانفكاك
الاطراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A single cause can always produce multiple effects without issue.
Correction: The author shows that if the effects are separable, a single cause cannot necessitate both without violating either inseparability or coextensiveness.

Checkpoint
If two effects are separable, why can't one cause necessitate both, according to the author?
A) Because the cause would be non-coextensive
B) Because the effects would become inseparable
C) Both A and B
D) Because the cause would be non-reciprocal

Key Takeaway
A single cause cannot produce two effects that can exist independently, otherwise causality breaks down either into inseparability or non-coextensiveness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider a time when you held two beliefs that seemed to come from the same reasoning, but when pushed, you realized they could be separated. Did you cling to them together out of comfort? Ask yourself: Am I willing to examine whether my convictions are truly inseparable or merely convenient?
?
The author shows that separating effects requires separating causes. What are the most important 'effects' in your faith—like knowledge, sincerity, and action? Are you trying to produce them all from one habit or source? How can you ensure each has its own authentic foundation?

Causation is Unconditional: The Fifth Issue
Al-Jurjani argues that causation is unconditional and clarifies via an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the causation of a cause for its effect is unconditional, and how al-Jurjani distinguishes between conditions for the cause's existence and conditions for its causation.

Core Idea
Causation is unconditional: if the cause exists, its effect necessarily follows.

In this passage, al-Jurjani addresses a key issue in causality: is the causal link conditional? He argues it is not, and supports this with a necessary judgment. He then responds to an objection by distinguishing between conditions for the cause's existence and conditions for its effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: a claim, a proof, an objection, and a reply.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Understand the necessary judgment
Focus on the example: knowledge subsisting in a subject makes it knowing without any further condition. This is a necessary judgment (ḥukm ḍarūrī).
The author says this is agreed upon by those who affirm states (aḥwāl).
3
Analyze the objection and response
The objection says implication of knowledge for being knowing might require life and absence of opposites. Note how al-Jurjani responds: these are conditions for the existence of knowledge, not for its causation.
This distinction is the key to resolving the objection.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Contrast a condition for the cause's existence (e.g., life for knowledge) with a condition for its effect (none here). Confirm you can state the difference in your own words.
This distinction prevents confusion in causality discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة ( الخامسة : إيجاب العلة لمعلولها ( لا يكون مشروطاً بشرط اتفاقاً ) من القائلين بثبوت الحال، وهذا حكم ضروري، ( فإنه لا يتصور علم بلا عالمية )، يعني أنا إذا علمنا قيام العلم بمحل علمنا كونه عالماً بلا توقف على العلم بشيء آخر أصلاً، وهو المراد بقوله : ( سواء علمنا الشرط، أو وجوده أم لا، فلو كان إيجاب العلم للعالمية مشروطاً بشرط لم يمكن لنا الجزم بالعالمية؛ إلا بعد تصور ذلك الشرط، والتصديق بوجوده. فإن قيل : اقتضاء العلم العالمية مشروط بقيام العلم بالمحل و مشروط أيضاً ( بالحياة، وانتفاء أضداده ) أي أضداد العلم ( قلنا هذه شروط وجوده )، فإن وجود العلم في نفسه مشروط بهذه الأمور ) والكلام في شروط تأثيره). وإيجابه للعالمية، والفرق بين شرط وجود العلة وبين شرط اقتضائها لمعلولها بعد وجودها مما لا سترة به.
The fifth issue: The causation (ījāb) of the cause ('illa) for its effect (ma'lūl) is not conditional upon any condition, by agreement among those who affirm the state (ḥāl). And this is a necessary judgment (ḥukm ḍarūrī), for it is inconceivable knowledge without being knowing. That is, if we know the subsistence of knowledge in a subject, we know it to be knowing without depending on knowledge of anything else at all. This is what is meant by his statement: 'whether we know the condition or its existence or not, for if the causation of knowledge for being knowing were conditional upon a condition, we could not be certain of being knowing except after conceiving that condition and affirming its existence.' If it is said: The implication (iqtiḍā') of knowledge for being knowing is conditional upon the subsistence of knowledge in the subject and also conditional upon life and the absence of its opposites, i.e., the opposites of knowledge. We say: These are conditions for its existence, for the existence of knowledge in itself is conditional upon these matters, but the discussion is about the conditions of its effect (ta'thīr) and its causation for being knowing. And the difference between a condition for the existence of the cause and a condition for its implication of its effect after its existence is not concealed.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. It states the fifth issue: the causation (ījāb) of a cause for its effect is unconditional—this is agreed upon by those who affirm states (aḥwāl). It is a necessary judgment: for example, if we know that knowledge subsists in a subject, we necessarily know that subject is knowing, without needing to check any further condition. The author then clarifies that this remains true whether or not we know any hypothetical condition. An objection is raised: the implication of knowledge for being knowing might be conditional upon life and the absence of opposites. The answer distinguishes between conditions for the existence of the cause itself (here, life and absence of opposites are conditions for the existence of knowledge) and conditions for the cause's effect after it exists. The argument concludes that this distinction is clear and resolves the objection.

Key Terms
علة (cause)
معلول (effect)
إيجاب (causation/necessitation)
شرط (condition)
اقتضاء (implication)
حكم ضروري (necessary judgment)
حال (state)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that life and absence of opposites are conditions for knowledge to cause being knowing.
Correction: These are conditions for the existence of knowledge itself, not for its causal effect. Once knowledge exists, it necessarily makes its subject knowing without any further condition.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjānī, why is the causation of a cause unconditional?
A) Because it is agreed upon by all theologians.
B) Because it is a necessary judgment: if we know knowledge in a subject, we know it is knowing without further conditions.
C) Because conditions only apply to the existence of the cause, not to its causation.
D) Because the cause cannot have any conditions at all.

Key Takeaway
The causation of a cause for its effect is unconditional; any conditions relate to the cause's existence, not its causal impact.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

intermediate
5 min
The Rule: Single Cause Cannot Necessitate Two Different Effects
Al-Jurjānī states the rule and gives a logical argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument for why a single cause cannot necessitate two different effects, based on the possibility of separation (infikāk) and the requirement of uniformity (iṭṭirād).

Core Idea
A single cause cannot necessitate two separable effects, else cause-effect uniformity breaks down.

Before tackling theological objections, al-Jurjānī establishes a foundational causal rule: one cause cannot produce two different effects that can exist apart. He provides a precise argument grounded in logical possibilities. This lesson walks through that argument, clarifying the concepts of separation and uniformity.

Guided Path
1
Read the rule and its background
Start by reading the source passage to see the rule stated and the disagreement noted. Al-Jurjānī chooses a differentiation (tafṣīl) based on separation.
2
Understand the key condition: separation (infikāk)
The rule applies when two effects can be separated from each other, either one-sided or both-sided. Example: being knowing by blackness vs. by whiteness.
Separation means each can exist without the other.
3
Follow the argument: two possibilities
If a single cause existed, either both effects would necessarily follow (making them inseparable, contradiction) or one could be absent (making the cause non-uniform). Both lead to absurdity.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Summarize the conclusion
Therefore, a single cause cannot necessitate two different effects if separation is possible. This sets the stage for the objection about God's knowledge.

المسألة ( السادسة : لا توجب العلة الواحدة حكمين مختلفين، وقد اختلف فيه، فجوز بعضهم هذا الإيجاب ومنعه آخرون والمختار هو التفصيل الذي أشار إليه بقوله : ( واعلم أنه إن جاز (الانفكاك بين الحكمين، إما من جانب واحد، أو من الجانبين ) كالعالمية بالسواد و العالمية ( بالبياض)، فإنهما حكمان يجوز انفكاك كل منهما عن الآخر (امتنع) تعليلهما بعلة واحدة، ( وإلا لزم عدم الانفكاك، أو عدم الاطراد، وذلك لأنه إذا وجد تلك العلة فإن وجب ثبوت كل من الحكمين كانا متلازمين والمقدر خلافه. وإن لم يجب بل جاز انتفاء أحدهما ثبوت تلك العلة كانت تلك العلة غير مطردة، قيل : هاهنا إشكالان : الأول لله علم واحد، وعالميته (متعددة بحسب تعدد المعلومات؛ إذ كونه عالماً بالسواد غير كونه عالماً بالبياض، ولهذا لا يسد أحدهما مسد الآخر، فهذه العالميات التي لا تتناهى معللة بعلة واحدة هي ذلك العلم الواحد الثابت له تعالى. (قلنا: التزمه القاضي ) ، وقال : عالميته تعالى متعددة مختلفة وهي مع ذلك معللة بعلة واحدة، ده الآمدي بأن القاضي لما اعترف بأن كون الرب عالماً بسواد محل معين، مخالف لكونه عالماً ببياضه مع تعذر الاجتماع بينهما لزمه من تعليلهما بعلة واحدة إما اجتماعهما معاً، وإما عدم اطراد تلك العلة (وأثبت) أبو سهل (الصعلوكي) من الأشاعرة لله تعالى ( علوماً غير متناهية)، كل واحد منها علة العالمية واحدة. ورد بأنه مخالف لمذهب الشيخ والأئمة ولما سيأتي من البرهان على امتناع تعدد علمه تعالى . ( وأما نحن فنمنع تعدد العالمية، وإنما التعدد في تعلق العلم الواحد، (أو) تعلق ( العالمية ( الواحدة بحسب تعدد المعلومات ولا محذور في تعدد التعلقات في حقه تعالى. وأما في الشاهد، فالعلم (متعدد بتعدد المعلومات، والعالمية متعددة بتعدد العلوم
The sixth issue: A single cause does not necessitate two different effects. There is disagreement: some permitted this necessitation and others prohibited it. The chosen view is the differentiation indicated by his statement: 'Know that if separation (infikāk) between the two effects is possible, either from one side or from both sides, like being knowing by blackness and being knowing by whiteness—these are two effects each of which can be separated from the other—then their being caused by a single cause is impossible. Otherwise, either non-separation or non-uniformity would follow. For if that cause exists, then if the existence of each of the two effects is necessary, they would be inseparable, contrary to what is assumed. And if it is not necessary but the absence of one is possible despite the existence of that cause, then that cause would not be uniform.' It is said: There are two objections. The first: God has one knowledge, and His being knowing (ʿālimiyya) is multiple according to the multiplicity of objects known, for His being knowing of blackness is other than His being knowing of whiteness, and therefore one does not suffice for the other. So these infinite world-knowledges are caused by a single cause—that one knowledge established for Him, exalted. We say: al-Qāḍī (Abū Bakr al-Bāqillānī) committed to this, and said: His being knowing, exalted, is multiple and diverse, and yet it is caused by a single cause. Al-Āmidī refuted him: when al-Qāḍī admitted that the Lord's being knowing of blackness in a specific substrate is different from His being knowing of its whiteness, and that their conjunction is impossible, then from his causing them by a single cause it necessarily follows either that they exist together or that the cause is not uniform. And Abū Sahl al-Ṣaʿlūkī from the Ashʿarīs affirmed for God, exalted, infinite knowledges, each one of which is the cause of a single world-knowledge. It is refuted as being contrary to the doctrine of the Shaykh and the imams and to the proof that will come on the impossibility of His knowledge being multiple. As for us, we deny the multiplicity of being knowing (ʿālimiyya); rather the multiplicity is in the relation of the one knowledge—or the relation of the one being knowing—according to the multiplicity of objects known. And there is no harm in the multiplicity of relations with respect to Him, exalted. As for in the witnessed (shāhid), knowledge is multiple according to the multiplicity of objects known, and being knowing is multiple according to the multiplicity of knowledges.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a rule-and-objection-answer structure. First, the rule: a single cause cannot necessitate two different effects. The author gives a detailed argument based on the possibility of separation (infikāk) between effects—if two effects can be separated, they cannot be caused by one cause, otherwise either necessary inseparability or non-uniformity of the cause would result. Then the first objection from theology: God has one knowledge but multiple 'world-knowledges' (ʿālimiyyāt) corresponding to all known things; how can one cause (that knowledge) necessitate infinite different effects? Three answers are recorded: (1) al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī accepted the multiplicity of 'being knowing' as caused by one cause, but al-Āmidī refuted this by showing it leads to contradiction. (2) Abū Sahl al-Ṣaʿlūkī posited multiple knowledges in God, but this contradicts orthodox Ashʿarism. (3) The author's own position: there is no multiplicity in the attribute of 'being knowing'; multiplicity only in the relation (taʿalluq) of that one attribute to different objects. In the witnessed world, by contrast, knowledge itself is multiple and thus 'being knowing' is multiple. This resolves the objection without theological error.

Key Terms
انفكاك (separation)
اطراد (uniformity)
علة واحدة (single cause)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjānī's argument, what follows if a single cause were to necessitate two effects that can be separated?
A) The cause would be perfect.
B) The effects would be inseparable or the cause would be non-uniform.
C) The effects would become identical.
D) The cause would have to be multiple.

Key Takeaway
A single cause cannot necessitate two effects that can exist apart, because that would destroy either causal inseparability or uniformity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often expect multiple different outcomes from a single effort or prayer. According to this passage, why can't that single action be the actual cause of those varied results? How does this expose a misplaced trust in the means rather than in Allah as the direct grantor of each specific need?
?
Consider a personal situation where you assumed that one change (e.g., a new job) would bring both financial stability and personal fulfillment. How does the logical contradiction shown here—that either the effects become inseparable or the cause becomes unreliable—reveal the unsoundness of that expectation? What concrete shift in relying on Allah alone does this principle demand from you?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
The First Objection: God's One Knowledge and Multiple World-Knowledges
Al-Jurjānī presents the first objection and three responses, adopting the third.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first theological objection to the rule and how al-Jurjānī resolves it by distinguishing between the attribute of being knowing and its relations (taʿalluqāt).

Core Idea
Multiplicity in known objects does not require multiplicity in God's attribute of being knowing; it is only a multiplicity of relations.

The rule just established seems to conflict with God's one knowledge causing infinite distinct 'world-knowledges' (ʿālimiyyāt). This lesson examines the objection and three proposed solutions. The author's solution elegantly preserves both the causal rule and divine simplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection: God has one knowledge, but His being knowing (ʿālimiyya) is multiple because being knowing of blackness is different from being knowing of whiteness. These infinite world-knowledges seem to be effects of one cause (the one knowledge).
2
Examine al-Qāḍī's position and al-Āmidī's refutation
Al-Qāḍī accepted the multiplicity and the single cause, but al-Āmidī showed it leads to contradiction: either both world-knowledges exist together (impossible) or the cause is non-uniform.
This shows the rule holds even in the divine case.
3
Consider al-Ṣaʿlūkī's option and its rejection
He posited multiple knowledges in God, each causing one world-knowledge. But this contradicts orthodox Ashʿarism and the proof of God's knowledge being one.
4
Understand the author's resolution
Al-Jurjānī denies multiplicity in the attribute ʿālimiyya; instead, the multiplicity is in the relation (taʿalluq) of the one knowledge to different objects. This avoids any error.
In the witnessed world, knowledge is multiple, so ʿālimiyya is multiple accordingly.

Misconception: Thinking that the multiplicity of objects known necessitates multiplicity in God's attribute of being knowing.
Correction: Al-Jurjānī explains that multiplicity is only in the relations (taʿalluqāt) of the one attribute, not in the attribute itself.

Checkpoint
How does al-Jurjānī ultimately resolve the objection about God's one knowledge causing multiple world-knowledges?
A) By affirming that God has many knowledges.
B) By denying that God knows multiple objects.
C) By distinguishing between the attribute of being knowing and its relations to objects.
D) By accepting that a single cause can necessitate different effects in the divine case.

Key Takeaway
God's one knowledge causes multiple world-knowledges, but this multiplicity is only in its relations, not in the attribute itself, preserving both the causal rule and divine simplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the multiplicity of God's knowing is only in relation and not in His essence, how does that confront your tendency to fragment your own identity based on your varied roles and actions? What would it mean for you to see yourself as a single 'knower' before God, even when your experiences seem contradictory?
?
When you consider that God's one knowledge encompasses every detail of your life without division, does that comfort you or unsettle you? Are you willing to trust that He knows the tiniest struggle you face as intimately as the greatest joy, without either being lost in the multiplicity?
?
The author insists that no harm comes from multiple relations in God. Yet you often feel torn by the multiple demands on your life. What would change if you believed that your own multiplicity of experiences can be unified in a single relation to God, without conflict?

Life as Condition, Not Cause
Al-Jurjānī answers the objection that life necessitates two different effects by clarifying that life is a condition, not a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why life being a condition for the cause of knowledge and power does not imply that a single cause produces two different effects.

Core Idea
Being a condition is not the same as being a cause.

The second objection argues that life must be a cause because it makes both knowledge and power possible. But this conflates a condition with a cause. In this passage, al-Jurjānī clarifies that life is a condition (sharṭ) for the existence of the cause, not the cause itself. This distinction preserves the rule that a single cause cannot produce two different effects.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection: life is said to necessitate two soundnesses—the soundness of being knowing and the soundness of being powerful. This challenges the rule that one cause cannot give two effects.
Notice that the objection assumes life is the direct cause of both soundnesses.
2
Analyze the answer
Al-Jurjānī answers that life is a condition (sharṭ) for the existence of the cause, not a cause that necessitates the soundnesses. Understand the difference: a condition enables the cause, but does not itself produce the effect.
The cause here is knowledge and power; life is a prerequisite for their existence.
3
Examine the elaboration
The commentary explains why each objection is treated separately and further clarifies that the cause (ʿilla) is the muṣaḥḥiḥ (that which renders sound) and that its necessitation of soundness is a consequence of its necessitation of the judgment.
This prevents a secondary objection about the cause itself necessitating two things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإشكال (الثاني: الحياة توجب صحة العالمية، و) صحة ( القادرية)، فقد أوجبت علة واحدة حكمين مختلفين. (قلنا ) : الحياة (شرط) لوجود المصحح ، فهي شرط لوجود العلة ( لا علة موجبة للصحتين. هذا إن جاز الانفكاك بين. قوله : (الإشكال الثاني) جعل كل واحد من صورتي النقض إشكالاً برأسه، لكون جواب كل منهما مخالفا لجواب الآخر. قوله : شرط لوجود العلة أي العلم والقدرة، وإطلاق المصحح على العلة؛ لما سيجيء في بيان الفرق أن العلة مصححة اتفاقاً أي مؤثرة في صحة المعلول وموجبة لها. لا يقال : يلزم الإشكال في العلة لكونها موجبة للحكم، ولصحته؛ لأن إيجابها للصحة ليس إلا بتبع إيجابها للحكم بناء على امتناع انفكاك صحة الحكم عن ثبوته .
The second objection: Life necessitates the soundness (ṣiḥḥa) of being knowing and the soundness of being powerful—so a single cause has necessitated two different effects. We say: Life is a condition (sharṭ) for the existence of the cause (muṣaḥḥiḥ), so it is a condition for the existence of the cause, not a cause necessitating the two soundnesses. This is if separation is possible. His statement: 'The second objection' makes each of the two types of counterexample an objection on its own, because the answer for each is different from the answer for the other. His statement: 'Condition for the existence of the cause' means knowledge and power. And the application of 'muṣaḥḥiḥ' (that which renders sound) to the cause is because it will come in the explanation of the difference that the cause is, by agreement, a muṣaḥḥiḥ, i.e., effective in the soundness of the effect and necessitating it. It should not be said: The difficulty arises for the cause because it necessitates the judgment and its soundness; for its necessitation of soundness is only a consequence of its necessitation of the judgment, based on the impossibility of separating the soundness of a judgment from its existence.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The second objection argues that life (al-ḥayāh) necessitates two different effects: the soundness of being knowing and the soundness of being powerful, so a single cause (life) would produce two distinct effects. The answer clarifies that life is not a cause (ʿilla) for these effects; rather, it is a condition (sharṭ) for the existence of the cause itself (knowledge and power). The cause (ʿilla) is what actually necessitates the effects; life merely makes the cause exist. The commentator adds that this objection is treated as a separate case because its answer differs from the previous one. He also explains that the term muṣaḥḥiḥ (that which renders sound) is applied to the cause because the cause is effective in rendering the effect sound. Moreover, the necessitation of soundness by the cause is dependent on its necessitation of the judgment, since soundness cannot be separated from existence of the judgment.

Key Terms
حياة (life)
صحة (soundness)
مصحح (that which renders sound)
شرط (condition)
علة موجبة (necessitating cause)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that every attribute that is a condition for causing effects is itself a cause of those effects.
Correction: A condition (sharṭ) enables the existence of a cause, but the cause itself is what directly produces the effect. Life is a condition for knowledge and power to exist, but it is not the cause of their soundness.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjānī, why does the objection about life not refute the rule that a single cause cannot necessitate two different effects?
A) Because life is not a cause but a condition for the existence of the cause.
B) Because life necessitates only one effect.
C) Because the effects of life are not different.
D) Because the objection applies only to the witnessed world.

Key Takeaway
Life is a condition for the existence of the cause (knowledge and power), not a necessitating cause of their soundness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If life is merely a condition for your knowledge and power, not their source, then what claim do you have over your own achievements? How does this strip away any pride you feel about your intellect or abilities?
?
You often thank God for your life, but do you also thank Him for the very ability to know and act? If life is just a condition, what are you really thanking God for when you wake up each day?
?
Is there an area in your life where you treat a condition (like health, wealth, or opportunity) as if it were a cause—something that directly produces good outcomes? How would your reliance on God change if you truly saw those conditions as mere prerequisites for His enabling?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
5 min
Answering the Objection: Life as a Condition, Not a Cause
Author answers the objection that life as one cause produces two effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the distinction between a cause ('illa) and a condition (shart) resolves the objection that a single attribute (life) produces two effects.

Core Idea
Life is a condition for the cause, not the cause itself.

This lesson addresses a key objection to the principle that one cause cannot produce two different effects. The objection claims that life, being a single attribute, necessitates two distinct validities—the validity of knowledge and the validity of power. How can a single attribute produce two effects? The author's answer introduces a crucial distinction: life is not the direct cause but a condition that enables the actual causes to exist. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping the logic of divine attributes in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the objection and then the answer.
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the objection and its basis
The objection claims that life (a single attribute) leads to two different judgments (validities of knowing and being powerful). Ask yourself: Why is this a problem for the principle of causality?
The problem is that a single cause should produce a single effect.
3
Understand the answer: condition vs. cause
The author responds by saying life is a condition (shart) for the cause, not the cause itself. Life enables the true causes (knowledge and power) to exist. This preserves the principle that one cause produces one effect.
The answer is conditional on the separability of the two effects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْإِشْكَالُ الثَّانِي: الْحَيَاةُ تُوجِبُ صِحَّةَ الْعَالَمِيَّةِ، وَ) صِحَّةَ ( الْقَادِرِيَّةِ)، فَقَدْ أَوْجَبَتْ عِلَّةٌ وَاحِدَةٌ حُكْمَيْنِ مُخْتَلِفَيْنِ. (قُلْنَا ) : الْحَيَاةُ (شَرْطٌ) لِوُجُودِ الْمُصَحِّحِ ، فَهِيَ شَرْطٌ لِوُجُودِ الْعِلَّةِ ( لَا عِلَّةٌ مُوجِبَةٌ لِلصِّحَّتَيْنِ. هَذَا إِنْ جَازَ الِانْفِكَاكُ بَيْنَ الْحُكْمَيْنِ
The second objection: Life necessitates the validity of world-knowledge and the validity of capability, so a single cause has necessitated two different judgments. We said: Life is a condition for the existence of the cause, so it is a condition for the existence of the cause, not a cause necessitating the two validities. This is if separation between the two judgments is possible.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against the principle that one cause cannot produce two different effects. The objection states that life (الحياة) in God is one attribute, yet it necessitates two distinct effects: the validity of being knowing (صحة العالمية) and the validity of being powerful (صحة القادرية). Thus a single cause seems to produce two different judgments. The author's answer distinguishes between a cause and a condition. Life is not the efficient cause of those validities; rather, it is a condition (شرط) for the existence of the true cause, which is knowledge and power. In other words, life makes knowledge and power possible, but it does not directly cause the validities of being knowing or being powerful. This answer only holds when the two effects (validities) are separable from each other, i.e., where it is possible for one to exist without the other in the witnessed realm.

Key Terms
حیات
شرط
علة
صحة
عالمية
قادرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Life is the direct cause of both knowledge and power.
Correction: Life is merely a condition that enables the true causes (knowledge and power) to exist; it is not a cause itself.

Checkpoint
In the answer to the second objection, what is life considered to be?
A) A cause that directly produces two effects
B) A condition for the existence of the cause, not a cause itself
C) A separate effect of knowledge
D) An attribute that has no relation to knowledge and power

Key Takeaway
Life is a condition for the existence of the causes of knowledge and power, not a direct cause itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own understanding of God, do you treat His life as a cause that automatically produces other attributes, or as a condition that depends on His will? How does that affect your trust in His wisdom?
?
Consider a time you assumed that one good deed (like prayer) must automatically produce multiple spiritual benefits. Does the distinction between condition and cause help you see where you might be expecting God to act in a mechanical way rather than as a free agent? What change in perspective is needed?
?
Are you willing to accept that some things in your life are merely conditions, not causes, and that God may not always give the expected effects? How does this lesson challenge your sense of control?

Imam al-Haramayn on Inseparable Effects: Indeterminate Cause
Imam al-Haramayn's view on causality of inseparable effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Haramayn's position that when two effects are inseparable, we cannot determine whether they share one cause or have multiple causes without revelation.

Core Idea
Inseparable effects leave cause indeterminate without revelation.

In this passage, the author presents the case of two effects that are always inseparable, such as the knowledge of black and the knowledge of that knowledge. Imam al-Haramayn argues that in such cases, reason alone cannot decide if they have one cause or many—only revelation can settle it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the example of inseparable effects: the world-knowledge of black and the world-knowledge of knowledge of black. Note that they always go together.
The terms 'عالمية' and 'تلازم' are key.
2
Understand Imam al-Haramayn's position
He says 'يجوز الأمران'—both possibilities are permissible. No judgment can be made about the cause unless revelation indicates.
This is a cautious, revelation-dependent approach.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: why would reason be unable to decide? The commentary will explain that because the effects are inseparable, we cannot observe one without the other, so causality is ambiguous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا إِنِ امْتَنَعَ الِانْفِكَاكُ بَيْنَهُمَا، كَالْعَالَمِيَّةِ بِالسَّوَادِ، وَ) الْعَالَمِيَّةِ (بِالْعِلْمِ بِهَا أَيْ بِالْعَالَمِيَّةِ الْأُولَى، فَإِنَّهُمَا مُتَلَازِمَتَانِ، لَا يَجُوزُ الْاِنْفِكَاكُ فِي شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ، ) فَقَالَ إِمَامُ الْحَرَمَيْنِ: يَجُوزُ الْأَمْرَانِ)، فَلَا يُحْكَمُ فِيهَا أَيْ فِي الْأَحْكَامِ الْمُتَلَازِمَةِ بِاتِّحَادِ الْعِلَّةِ، وَلَا بِتَعَدُّدِهَا إِلَّا بِدَلَالَةِ السَّمْعِ عَلَى أَحَدِهِمَا . (و) قَالَ (الْآمِدِيُّ): الْحَقُّ أَنَّهُ التَّفْصِيلُ، هُوَ يَجُوزُ الْأَمْرَانِ ) فِي الشَّاهِدِ ( إِذَا كَانَتْ الْأَحْكَامُ الْمُتَلَازِمَةُ ( مِنْ جِنْسٍ وَاحِدٍ ، كَالْعَالَمِيَّاتِ ( وَيَمْتَنِعُ ) ذَلِكَ (فِي) الْأَحْكَامِ الْمُخْتَلِفَةِ) الْأَجْنَاسِ فِي بَلْ يَجِبُ تَعْلِيلُهَا بِعِلَلٍ مُتَعَدِّدَةٍ. ( وَ ) أَمَّا فِي ( الْغَائِبِ ) فَإِنْ كَانَ أَحْكَامُهُ مُخْتَلِفَةً، وَجَبَ تَعْلِيلُهَا بِعِلَلٍ ،مُتَعَدِّدَةٍ كَمَا فِي الشَّاهِدِ . وَإِنْ كَانَتْ مِنْ جِنْسٍ وَاحِدٍ، فَقَدْ سَبَقَ أَنَّ عَالَمِيَّتَهُ تَعَالَى وَاحِدَةٌ مُعَلَّلَةٌ بِعِلَّةٍ ،وَاحِدَةٍ وَإِنَّمَا التَّعَدُّدُ وَالِاخْتِلَافُ فِي التَّعَلُّقِ وَالْمُتَعَلَّقِ فَقَطْ، وَكَذَا الْحَالُ فِي الْقَادِرِيَّةِ وَنَحْوِهَا
And as for if separation between them is impossible, like the world-knowledge of blackness and the world-knowledge of knowledge of it, i.e., the first world-knowledge, then they are inseparable, no separation is allowed in either side. Then Imam al-Haramayn said: both possibilities are permissible, so no judgment is made regarding them, i.e., regarding the inseparable judgments, about the unity of the cause or its multiplicity except by the indication of revelation to one of them. And al-Amidi said: the truth is the detail: both possibilities are permissible in the witnessed realm if the inseparable judgments are of one genus, like the world-knowledges, and it is forbidden in the judgments of different genera; rather, they must be explained by multiple causes. And as for the unseen, if His judgments are different, then they must be explained by multiple causes as in the witnessed realm. And if they are of one genus, it has preceded that His world-knowledge is one, explained by one cause, and the multiplicity and difference is only in the relation and the related object, and similarly for capability and the like.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage addresses the case where two effects (judgments) are inseparable (متلازمتان), meaning they always coexist and cannot be separated. The example given is the world-knowledge of black and the world-knowledge of the knowledge of that blackness (the first world-knowledge). These two world-knowledges necessarily imply each other. Imam al-Haramayn holds that in such a case, we cannot rule definitively whether they have one cause or multiple causes; revelation may indicate one. al-Amidi distinguishes: in the witnessed realm, if the inseparable judgments are of the same genus (like different world-knowledges), then both possibilities are allowed; but if they are of different genera (e.g., volition and power), then they must have multiple causes. In the unseen (God), the same principle applies: if His attributes (judgments) are of different kinds, they need multiple causes; if of one kind (like all world-knowledges), they have one cause, and the multiplicity is only in the relation to different objects.

Key Terms
تلازم
علة
عالمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Haramayn, what is the ruling for inseparable effects regarding their cause?
A) They definitely have one cause
B) They definitely have multiple causes
C) Both possibilities are permissible; revelation decides
D) They have no cause

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Haramayn holds that inseparable effects leave the question of cause open, requiring revelation for resolution.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a conviction about something, do you ever stop to ask if reason alone can truly settle it, or do you assume your own reasoning is sufficient? What would it look like to submit that conviction to revelation today?
?
Think of a time you were certain that two things must be connected by a single cause (e.g., a repeated pattern in your life). In light of this lesson, how might you be wrong? Are you open to the possibility that revelation could show you a different truth?
?
If you believe that hard thinking alone can solve every theological question, this lesson challenges that. Will you humble yourself to let revelation have the final word even when reason says 'both are possible'? Write one specific area of your faith where you will consciously rely on revelation over your own reasoning starting this week.

Al-Amidi's Detail: Genus and Realm in Inseparable Effects
Al-Amidi's detailed ruling on cause for inseparable effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Amidi's detailed view that the permissibility of one cause for inseparable effects depends on whether the effects are of the same genus and whether the context is the witnessed realm or the unseen.

Core Idea
Cause multiplicity follows genus and realm.

Al-Amidi offers a more nuanced approach than Imam al-Haramayn. He argues that in the witnessed realm, inseparable effects of the same genus can possibly have one cause, but those of different genera must have multiple causes. In the unseen, similar reasoning applies, with special attention to divine attributes like knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Amidi's distinction by genus
Note that he says if inseparable judgments are of one genus (like multiple world-knowledges), both possibilities are allowed in the witnessed realm. But if different genera, they must have multiple causes.
Genus refers to the type of attribute or judgment.
2
Apply the same principle to the unseen
For God's attributes, if they are of different kinds (e.g., knowledge and power), they need multiple causes (though in God, cause is not like in creation). If of one kind, one cause suffices.
This aligns with the example of world-knowledge.
3
Consider the example of world-knowledge
God's knowledge is one attribute with multiple objects; the multiplicity is only in relation, not in the attribute itself.
This shows how al-Amidi applies his rule.

One Effect Cannot Have Two Causes
Statement of the seventh issue with reference to its converse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical principle that a single effect cannot be produced by two independent causes, and how it complements the first principle of causation.

Core Idea
One effect, one cause — a fundamental rule of efficient causation.

The study of causality involves several foundational principles. After establishing that one cause cannot produce two distinct effects, the converse is equally important: one effect cannot stem from two independent causes. This lesson introduces the seventh issue, which states this converse principle, and clarifies its relationship to the first issue despite the fragmentary text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the phrasing about 'converse' and the incomplete ending. Pay attention to the parentheses and fragment.
The text is fragmentary due to OCR issues; extract the core principle.
2
Identify the two principles
The passage mentions the seventh issue (one effect cannot have two causes) and refers to its converse (the first issue: one cause cannot produce two effects). Distinguish them clearly.
The explanatory phrase 'namely that two judgments are not established by one cause' seems to restate the first principle; consider why it appears here.
3
Consider the logical relationship
Understand that these two principles are converses: if you invert the cause-effect relationship, you get the other principle.
This symmetry is important in causal reasoning.
4
Reflect on the incomplete text
The passage ends with 'and the establishment from genera'. Speculate on possible completions: does it mean the two causes must be from different genera? Or is it about establishing a judgment from genera?
The full context from subsequent pages is needed for clarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْمَسْأَلَةُ ( السَّابِعَةُ : لَا يَثْبُتُ حُكْمٌ ) وَاحِدٌ (بِعِلَّتَيْنِ : عَكْسُ الْأَوَّلِ ) ، وَهُوَ أَنَّهُ لَا يَثْبُتُ حُكْمَانِ بِعِلَّةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ، وَإِثْبَاتُ · مِنْ أَجْنَاسٍ
The seventh issue: One judgment is not established by two causes: the converse of the first, namely that two judgments are not established by one cause, and the establishment ... from genera.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage states the seventh issue in the discussion of causality. It asserts that a single effect (حكم) cannot be produced by two independent causes. This is the converse (عكس) of the first principle, which was that two effects cannot come from one cause. The text includes an explanatory phrase 'namely that two judgments are not established by one cause', which actually restates the first principle rather than the seventh; this may be a scribal confusion. The continuation 'وَإِثْبَاتُ · مِنْ أَجْنَاسٍ' is fragmentary, likely indicating that the establishment of a single effect by two causes of different genera is being discussed. The overall point is that just as one cause cannot produce two distinct effects, one effect cannot have two efficient causes.

Key Terms

علة
عكس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The seventh issue is simply repeating the first issue.
Correction: The seventh issue is the converse: it states that one effect cannot have two causes, whereas the first issue states that one cause cannot produce two effects.

Checkpoint

B) Two effects cannot share one cause
C) One effect cannot have two causes
D) Two causes cannot produce one effect

Key Takeaway
The seventh issue in causality asserts that a single effect cannot have two independent causes, establishing a reciprocal relationship with the first issue on causation.

One Judgment Cannot Be Caused by Two Causes
Proves that one judgment cannot be caused by two causes, refuting collective, substitutive, and composite causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a single judgment cannot be produced by multiple causes, and the logical problems with collective, substitutive, and composite causality.

Core Idea
One effect cannot have two causes—each modality collapses into contradiction or redundancy.

The author now presents the seventh issue: the impossibility of a single judgment being caused by multiple causes. This is the reverse of the previous rule. He systematically examines three ways in which multiple causes could be thought to produce one effect—collective, substitutive, and composite—and refutes each. The argument draws on fundamental principles of causality and coherence in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins by stating the thesis and then lists the three modalities.
2
Identify the three modalities
The passage presents three ways multiple causes could produce one effect: collectively (jam'), alternately (badal), or compositely (tarkib). Find each one in the text.
3
Understand the refutation of collective causality
For collective causality, the author argues that two causes are either similar, opposite, or different. Similar causes are redundant; opposite causes cannot coexist; different causes can separate, leading to failure of constancy or converse.
Pay attention to the example from al-Amidi about knowledge and power.
4
Understand the refutation of substitutive causality
For substitutive causality, the author says it is impossible for the same attribute (e.g., being characterized as knowing) to be caused by different causes at different times, because the effect would not be uniform.
This leads to a universal necessary judgment.
5
Reflect on the composite modality
The author mentions composition but does not elaborate; it is implied invalid by the same reasoning. Consider why combining two causes into one does not avoid the problems.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة السابعة: لا يثبت حكم واحد بعلتين، وهو عكس الأول، وهو أنه لا يثبت حكمان بعلة واحدة. وإثبات الحكم الواحد بالعلل المتعددة إما على الجمع أو البدل أو التركيب، والكل باطل. أما على الجمع، فلأنه استغنى بكل عن كل كما مر في أن الواحد بالشخص لا يعلل بعلتين، ولأن العلتين إما مثلان أو ضدان فلا يجتمعان في محل واحد فلا تكونان موجبتين لحكم واحد فيه، أو مختلفتان فيجوز افتراقهما، فإذا ثبت إحدى العلتين دون الأخرى، فإن انتفى الحكم فلا اطراد للعلة الثانية، وإن ثبت فلا انعكاس للعلة المتنافية، وقد يمتنع جواز الافتراق بين المختلفتين. قال الآمدي: والمختلفان لا بد أن يختلف أحكامهما، فإنا نعلم بالضرورة أن قيام العلم بذات يوجب كونها عالمة لا قادرة، وقيام القدرة بها يوجب عكس ذلك. وأما على البدل فلضرورة أنه لا يجوز تعليل العالمية بالعلم مرة وبالقدرة أخرى. وهذا التمثيل تنبيه على حكم كلي ضروري.
The seventh issue: One judgment is not established by two causes, and it is the reverse of the first, namely that two judgments are not established by one cause. And establishing one judgment by multiple causes is either collectively, or alternately, or compositely, and all are invalid. As for collectively, because each is independent of the other, as has passed concerning that which is one in person not being caused by two causes, and because two causes are either similar or opposite, so they do not coexist in one locus, so they are not necessitating one judgment in it, or they are different and so separation between them is permissible; if one of the two causes is established without the other, then if the judgment is absent, there is no constancy for the second cause, and if it is present, there is no converse for the contradictory cause, and the permissibility of separation between different ones may be impossible. Al-Amidi said: Different ones necessarily have different judgments, for we know by necessity that the subsistence of knowledge in an essence makes it knowing, not powerful, and the subsistence of power in it makes the opposite. As for alternately, because it is necessary by necessity that it is not permissible to cause being-characterized by knowledge sometimes and by power another time. And this example is a notification of a universal necessary judgment.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a proof that one judgment cannot be caused by two causes, and it also states the reverse (two judgments cannot be caused by one cause). It examines three modalities for multiple causes: collective (jam'), where both causes act simultaneously; substitution (badal), where they act alternately; and composition (tarkib), where they combine to form one cause. All are refuted. For collective causality: either the two causes are similar or opposite—similar causes are redundant, opposite causes cannot coexist in one locus; if they are different, they can separate, leading to failure of constancy (ittirād) or converse (inkisār). Al-Amidi adds that different causes necessarily produce different effects (e.g., knowledge makes an essence knowing, not powerful). For substitution: it is impossible to cause the same attribute (e.g., being-characterized) by knowledge at one time and by power at another, because the effect would not be uniform. The example serves as a general necessary principle.

المعلول
الجمع
البدل
التركيب
الاطراد
الانعكاس
المثلان
الضدان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two different causes can produce exactly the same effect.
Correction: Different causes necessarily produce different effects; e.g., knowledge makes something knowning, power makes it powerful. They cannot produce the same attribute.

Checkpoint
Why is collective causality (al-jam') rejected?
A) Because two causes cannot be similar.
B) Because it leads to infinite regress.
C) Because two causes are either similar, opposite, or different, each leading to contradiction or redundancy.
D) Because it requires revelation to decide.

Key Takeaway
A single judgment cannot be caused by two causes, whether collectively, alternately, or compositely.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an attribute you possess—such as patience or humility. Do you unconsciously attribute its existence to multiple causes: your upbringing, personal effort, or circumstances? How does the logical certainty that one judgment has one cause challenge you to trace it back to its true, single source—your Creator's will? What would change in your self-understanding if you truly believed that only Allah's direct act establishes that quality in you?
?
The passage argues that different causes produce different judgments. Examine a time when you tried to achieve a spiritual state (like gratitude) through multiple means (prayer, charity, reading) but felt your heart remained unchanged. Could it be that you were seeking the effect from the wrong cause? How does this truth about causality call you to seek the single effective cause—sincere dependence on Allah—rather than scattered efforts?
?
Al-Amidi’s point that knowledge makes one knowing and power makes one powerful seems obvious, yet we often blur the lines between God’s attributes, expecting His mercy to act like His justice. In your worship, do you sometimes expect one attribute of Allah to produce an effect that belongs to another? How does this precision about cause and effect refine your understanding of God’s perfection and your personal response to each of His names?

Why Two Causes Cannot Combine for One Effect
Al-Jurjani refutes the combination mode of multiple causes with two main arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Jurjani argues that two causes cannot simultaneously (by combination) necessitate a single effect.

Core Idea
Two similar or opposite causes cannot coexist in one locus to produce a single ruling.

Al-Jurjani begins his refutation of multiple causes by considering the possibility of combination, where two causes act together. He argues that each cause would have to be sufficient alone, leading to contradiction. In this lesson, we examine the two key arguments against combination and the supporting insight from al-Āmidī.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the combination argument. Notice how al-Jurjani first states the three modes and then focuses on combination.
The passage includes a quote from al-Āmidī.
2
Identify the two arguments
Find the two reasons given: (a) each cause is sufficient alone, and (b) causes must be similar or opposite, leading to problems.
3
Understand the consequences
Consider the implications for iṭṭirād and inkisār. If one cause is established without the other, the second cause's constancy is broken.
4
Learn from al-Āmidī
Note the example that knowledge makes one knowing not powerful, and power makes one powerful not knowing.
5
Reflect
Why does this show combination is invalid?
Think about the impossibility of two causes sharing the same effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكم الواحد بالعلل المتعددة، إما على الجمع أو البدل أو التركيب، والكل باطل. (أما على الجمع، فلأنه استغنى بكل على عن كل كما مر، في أن الواحد بالشخص لا يعلل بعلتين) ولأن العلتين إما مثلان أو ضدان، فلا يجتمعان في محل واحد، فلا تكونان موجبتين لحكم واحد فيه أو مختلفتان فيجوز افتراقهما)، فإذا ثبت إحدى العلتين دون الأخرى، فإن انتفي الحكم فلا اطراد للعلة الثانية، وإن ثبت فلا انعكاس للعلة المتنافية، وقد يمتنع جواز الافتراق بين المختلفتين. قال الآمدي: والمختلفان لا بد أن يختلف أحكامهما، فإنا نعلم بالضرورة أن قيام العلم بذات يوجب كونها عالمة لا قادرة وقيام القدرة بها يوجب عكس ذلك. (وأما على البدل فلضرورة أنه لا يجوز تعليل العالمية بالعلم مرة وبالقدرة أخرى). وهذا التمثيل تنبيه على حكم كلي ضروري. فإن قيل: العالمية معللة على سبيل البدل بعلم الله وبعلمنا، وهي حكم واحد. قلنا: لا مخالفة بين العلمين إلا بعارض كالقدم والحدوث، والعلة هو العلم المتحد فيهما مع قطع النظر عن العوارض المختلفة. وإن سلم اختلاف العلمين في الحقيقة منع اتحاد العالميتين فيهما. (وأما على سبيل التركيب؛ فلان حقيقتهما حال الانفراد والاجتماع واحدة فإذا لم تؤثرا في الحكم منفردتين، كما هو المفروض لم تؤثرا فيه مجتمعتين؛ وذلك لأن اقتضاء العلة للحكم إنما هو لذاتها لا باعتبار أمر خارج عنها، ولا شك أن اجتماعها مع غيرها لا يخرجا عن مقتضى ذاتها. وفيه منع ظاهر؛ لأن المقتضى حينئذ هو المجموع لا كل واحدة فلا يلزم خروج شيء منهما عن مقتضاه بحسب ذاته. (ولأن الصفات المختلفة لها أحكام مختلفة ضرورة)، كما نبهنا عليه نقلاً عن الآمدي، وإذا علل حكم واحد بمجموع وصفين لم يكن هناك اختلاف في أحكامهما.
A single ruling [can] have multiple causes, either by combination (jam‘), or substitution (badal), or composition (tarkīb), and all are invalid. As for combination, because each [cause] would be sufficient for [the effect] apart from the other, as has passed, that a single individual cannot be caused by two causes. And because two causes are either similar or opposites, they cannot coexist in a single locus, so they cannot be both necessitating a single ruling in it; or they are different, so their separation is permissible. If one of the two causes is established without the other, then if the ruling is negated, there is no constant application (iṭṭirād) for the second cause, and if it is established, there is no converse (inkisār) for the contradictory cause; and the permissibility of separation between two different [causes] may be impossible. Al-Āmidī said: Two different [causes] must have different rulings; for we know necessarily that the presence of knowledge in an essence necessitates its being knowing, not powerful, and the presence of power in it necessitates the opposite. As for substitution, it is necessary that it is not permissible to explain 'worldliness' sometimes by knowledge and sometimes by power. This example is a notification of a universal necessary ruling. For if it is said: 'Worldliness is causally explained on the basis of substitution by God's knowledge and by our knowledge, and it is a single ruling.' We say: There is no opposition between the two knowledges except by an accident like pre-eternity and temporality; the cause is knowledge united in them, disregarding differing accidents. If it is granted that the two knowledges differ in reality, it prevents the unity of the two worldlinesses in them. As for composition, because their reality when alone and together is one; so if they do not produce an effect singly—as supposed—they do not produce it together. That is because the determination of the cause for the ruling resides in its essence, not in virtue of something external to it; and there is no doubt that its conjunction with something else does not remove it from its essential determination. But there is an obvious objection: the determinant then is the sum, not each one; so no part of them is removed from its essential determination. And because different attributes necessarily have different rulings, as we have pointed out on the authority of al-Āmidī; and if a single ruling is caused by a composite of two attributes, there is no difference in their rulings.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that a single effect (ḥukm) can be caused by multiple causes. The author classifies the possible modes into three: combination (jam‘) where both causes act simultaneously, substitution (badal) where they act at different times, and composition (tarkīb) where they combine to form a single cause. He declares all invalid. For combination, each cause would have to be sufficient alone, but two similar causes cannot coexist in the same locus, and two opposites cannot both necessitate the same ruling. For substitution, it is impossible to attribute the same effect (e.g., being knowing) to different causes (knowledge vs. power) because each cause entails a different ruling. An objection that God’s knowledge and our knowledge both cause ‘worldliness’ is answered: the true cause is the common essence of knowledge, disregarding accidents. For composition, if each cause alone does not produce the effect, their sum cannot either, because a cause acts by its essence; adding another does not change its essence. A further argument: different attributes have different rulings, so a composite of two different attributes cannot give a single ruling.

Key Terms
علة
جمع
اطراد
انعكاس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two causes can combine to produce a single effect.
Correction: Each cause would have to be sufficient alone, which leads to contradiction (e.g., two similar causes cannot coexist, two opposites would give conflicting rulings).

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani, two causes in combination must be either ___ or ___.
A) similar or opposite
B) essential or accidental
C) primary or secondary
D) universal or particular

Key Takeaway
Combination of two causes is impossible because each would be sufficient alone and causes cannot coexist without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life are you expecting a single outcome (e.g., tranquility, success, closeness to Allah) from contradictory causes (e.g., both reliance on worldly means and reliance on Allah)? Al-Jurjani shows such combination is impossible. Which cause will you eliminate?
?
Consider a habit you practice that you believe produces a specific result. If that result could also be produced by a different, opposite habit, how does that affect your certainty? Will you investigate which cause is truly sufficient and consistent?
?
Al-Āmidī's example shows that knowledge and power produce different attributes. Look at your own attachments: do you seek to combine causes that naturally lead to different outcomes (e.g., seeking humility from pride)? What must you change to align your causes with your desired effect?

Why Substitution of Causes for One Effect Fails
Al-Jurjani refutes the substitution mode of multiple causes and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jurjani's argument that a single effect cannot be caused by different causes at different times (substitution), and how he defends against an objection involving God's knowledge and human knowledge.

Core Idea
The same effect cannot be produced by different causes because each cause entails a different ruling.

After refuting combination, al-Jurjani turns to substitution—the idea that one effect can be caused by different causes at different times. He argues this is impossible by appealing to a universal necessary ruling. He then addresses an objection that 'worldliness' is caused both by God's knowledge and our knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the substitution argument
Find the passage beginning with 'وأما على البدل'. Understand his claim that it is necessary that you cannot explain 'worldliness' sometimes by knowledge and sometimes by power.
This is a direct claim of impossibility.
2
Grasp the universal rule
The example of knowledge vs. power shows that different attributes give different rulings, so you cannot substitute one cause for another for the same effect.
3
Examine the objection
Someone objects that 'ālamiyya is one effect caused by God's knowledge and our knowledge as alternatives.
This objector thinks substitution works in this case.
4
Understand the answer
The common essence of knowledge is the cause, disregarding accidents like pre-eternity and temporality. If the knowledges differ in reality, then the worldlinesses differ too.
So the effect is not truly one if causes differ essentially.
5
Reflect
Why does substitution fail even if causes act at different times?

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The same effect (like 'ālamiyya) can be caused by different causes (God's knowledge, human knowledge) at different times.

What example does al-Jurjani use to illustrate that substitution is impossible?
A) knowledge and power for worldliness
B) heat and cold for wetness

Key Takeaway
Substitution of causes fails because different causes entail different effects, and any apparent counterexample (like God's and human knowledge) shares a common essence that is the real cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Consider a time you assumed that two apparently similar causes (e.g., two people's 'wisdom') would produce the same outcome. Did you overlook deeper essential differences? How can this lesson train you to examine causes more carefully rather than relying on surface similarities?
?
The passage shows that accidental differences (temporal vs. eternal) do not change the essence of the cause. In your own life, do you sometimes focus on superficial differences (e.g., circumstances, appearances) when evaluating the true source of an effect? What would change if you looked past accidents to the essence?
