Why Two Ineffective Causes Cannot Combine to Form a Single Cause
Al-Jurjani refutes the composition mode and responds to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jurjani's argument against composition, where two causes that individually do not produce an effect supposedly produce it when combined, and the objection that the composite is a new entity.

Core Idea
If two things do not cause an effect alone, their combination cannot cause it either.

The final mode al-Jurjani refutes is composition—the idea that two causes, each ineffective alone, together produce a single effect. He argues that since a cause acts by its essence, adding another does not change that essence. However, an objection arises: perhaps the composite itself is the cause, not each part. Let's examine this debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the composition argument
From 'وأما على سبيل التركيب' to the end. Notice the claim: if each does not cause singly, they cannot together.
2
Understand the reasoning
Because a cause's determination (iqtiḍā') resides in its essence, and adding another does not remove that essence.
3
Consider the objection
The objector says the determinant is the composite, not each part, so the reasoning fails.
This is a strong objection.
4
Al-Jurjani's final point
Different attributes have different rulings, so a composite of two different attributes cannot yield a single ruling (citing al-Āmidī).
5
Reflect
Why does composition ultimately fail?
Think about the nature of cause and effect.

الحكم الواحد بالعلل المتعددة، إما على الجمع أو البدل أو التركيب، والكل باطل. (أما على الجمع، فلأنه استغنى بكل على عن كل كما مر، في أن الواحد بالشخص لا يعلل بعلتين) ولأن العلتين إما مثلان أو ضدان، فلا يجتمعان في محل واحد، فلا تكونان موجبتين لحكم واحد فيه أو مختلفتان فيجوز افتراقهما)، فإذا ثبت إحدى العلتين دون الأخرى، فإن انتفي الحكم فلا اطراد للعلة الثانية، وإن ثبت فلا انعكاس للعلة المتنافية، وقد يمتنع جواز الافتراق بين المختلفتين. قال الآمدي: والمختلفان لا بد أن يختلف أحكامهما، فإنا نعلم بالضرورة أن قيام العلم بذات يوجب كونها عالمة لا قادرة وقيام القدرة بها يوجب عكس ذلك. (وأما على البدل فلضرورة أنه لا يجوز تعليل العالمية بالعلم مرة وبالقدرة أخرى). وهذا التمثيل تنبيه على حكم كلي ضروري. فإن قيل: العالمية معللة على سبيل البدل بعلم الله وبعلمنا، وهي حكم واحد. قلنا: لا مخالفة بين العلمين إلا بعارض كالقدم والحدوث، والعلة هو العلم المتحد فيهما مع قطع النظر عن العوارض المختلفة. وإن سلم اختلاف العلمين في الحقيقة منع اتحاد العالميتين فيهما. (وأما على سبيل التركيب؛ فلان حقيقتهما حال الانفراد والاجتماع واحدة فإذا لم تؤثرا في الحكم منفردتين، كما هو المفروض لم تؤثرا فيه مجتمعتين؛ وذلك لأن اقتضاء العلة للحكم إنما هو لذاتها لا باعتبار أمر خارج عنها، ولا شك أن اجتماعها مع غيرها لا يخرجا عن مقتضى ذاتها. وفيه منع ظاهر؛ لأن المقتضى حينئذ هو المجموع لا كل واحدة فلا يلزم خروج شيء منهما عن مقتضاه بحسب ذاته. (ولأن الصفات المختلفة لها أحكام مختلفة ضرورة)، كما نبهنا عليه نقلاً عن الآمدي، وإذا علل حكم واحد بمجموع وصفين لم يكن هناك اختلاف في أحكامهما.
A single ruling [can] have multiple causes, either by combination (jam‘), or substitution (badal), or composition (tarkīb), and all are invalid. As for combination, because each [cause] would be sufficient for [the effect] apart from the other, as has passed, that a single individual cannot be caused by two causes. And because two causes are either similar or opposites, they cannot coexist in a single locus, so they cannot be both necessitating a single ruling in it; or they are different, so their separation is permissible. If one of the two causes is established without the other, then if the ruling is negated, there is no constant application (iṭṭirād) for the second cause, and if it is established, there is no converse (inkisār) for the contradictory cause; and the permissibility of separation between two different [causes] may be impossible. Al-Āmidī said: Two different [causes] must have different rulings; for we know necessarily that the presence of knowledge in an essence necessitates its being knowing, not powerful, and the presence of power in it necessitates the opposite. As for substitution, it is necessary that it is not permissible to explain 'worldliness' sometimes by knowledge and sometimes by power. This example is a notification of a universal necessary ruling. For if it is said: 'Worldliness is causally explained on the basis of substitution by God's knowledge and by our knowledge, and it is a single ruling.' We say: There is no opposition between the two knowledges except by an accident like pre-eternity and temporality; the cause is knowledge united in them, disregarding differing accidents. If it is granted that the two knowledges differ in reality, it prevents the unity of the two worldlinesses in them. As for composition, because their reality when alone and together is one; so if they do not produce an effect singly—as supposed—they do not produce it together. That is because the determination of the cause for the ruling resides in its essence, not in virtue of something external to it; and there is no doubt that its conjunction with something else does not remove it from its essential determination. But there is an obvious objection: the determinant then is the sum, not each one; so no part of them is removed from its essential determination. And because different attributes necessarily have different rulings, as we have pointed out on the authority of al-Āmidī; and if a single ruling is caused by a composite of two attributes, there is no difference in their rulings.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that a single effect (ḥukm) can be caused by multiple causes. The author classifies the possible modes into three: combination (jam‘) where both causes act simultaneously, substitution (badal) where they act at different times, and composition (tarkīb) where they combine to form a single cause. He declares all invalid. For combination, each cause would have to be sufficient alone, but two similar causes cannot coexist in the same locus, and two opposites cannot both necessitate the same ruling. For substitution, it is impossible to attribute the same effect (e.g., being knowing) to different causes (knowledge vs. power) because each cause entails a different ruling. An objection that God’s knowledge and our knowledge both cause ‘worldliness’ is answered: the true cause is the common essence of knowledge, disregarding accidents. For composition, if each cause alone does not produce the effect, their sum cannot either, because a cause acts by its essence; adding another does not change its essence. A further argument: different attributes have different rulings, so a composite of two different attributes cannot give a single ruling.

Key Terms
علة
تركيب
اقتضاء
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two things that individually do not cause an effect can cause it when combined.
Correction: A cause acts by its essence; combining with another does not change its essence, so if it cannot cause alone, it cannot cause in a combination. Moreover, if the two attributes are different, they necessitate different rulings, so they cannot result in a single effect.

Checkpoint
What is the objection to al-Jurjani's argument against composition?
A) The composite itself is the cause, not each part
B) The causes are not really similar
C) The effect is not actually one
D) The causes are opposites

Key Takeaway
Composition of two ineffective causes is invalid because a cause's efficacy is by its essence, and a composite of different attributes would produce different rulings, not a single one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage states that a cause's determination is by its essence, not by external association. Is there any act of worship in your life that you perform primarily because of external factors—expectation, peer pressure, habit—rather than from the essence of your own imān? How can you realign your intention to be solely for Allah?
?
Al-Jurjani refutes the idea that two ineffective causes can become effective when combined. In your spiritual life, are you relying on a combination of minor good deeds to compensate for an area of disobedience? Confess that area to yourself now and commit to addressing it directly.
?
The response in the passage invokes that different attributes have different rulings. Consider your own contradictory desires—e.g., wanting to please Allah but also seeking worldly approval. How can you ensure that your actions proceed from a single, pure attribute of sincerity rather than a composite of conflicting motives?

Cause vs Condition: First Two Distinctions
Al-Jurjani introduces the eighth issue and lists the first two of nine distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two distinctions between cause (ʿilla) and condition (sharṭ) according to the Ashʿarī theory of states.

Core Idea
Cause is constant and existential; condition may be non-constant and non-existent.

Al-Jurjani now turns to a crucial topic in uṣūl al-fiqh and kalām: the difference between a cause and a condition. The discussion is framed within the view of those who affirm states (aḥwāl). He lists nine distinctions, but here he gives the first two, which concern constancy and ontological status. Understanding these is foundational for later arguments about divine attributes and human actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly. Notice the terms ʿilla, sharṭ, muṭṭarida, wujūdiyya, ʿadamiyya. Try to see the contrast between the cause and condition in each distinction.
The passage is dense; take your time with each sentence.
2
Identify the first distinction
The first distinction is about constancy (muṭṭarida). The cause always brings the ruling, while the condition may not. Consider the example of life and knowledge.
Life is a condition for knowledge but doesn't always produce it.
3
Identify the second distinction
The second distinction is about existential status: the cause is existent (wujūdiyya), but the condition can be non-existent (ʿadamiyya), like the absence of an opposite.
Al-Qāḍī ʿAbd al-Jabbār is cited as holding this view.
4
Reflect on the importance
Ask yourself why these distinctions matter. They help theologians and jurists analyze causality in divine actions and legal rulings.
This connects to later debates about the nature of God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثامنة في الفرق بين العلة والشرط على رأي مثبتي الأحوال، وهو من وجوه تسعة: (الأول) العلة مطردة، فحيثما وجدت وجد الحكم قطعاً، والشرط قد لا يطرد، فيوجد ولا يوجد معه المشروط، كالحياة للعلم. الثاني: العلة وجودية كما مر، والشرط قد يكون عدمياً، كانتفاء الضد، وهو مختار القاضي، فإنه قال: بعلمنا.
The eighth issue: on the difference between cause and condition according to those who affirm states. It has nine aspects: First: The cause is constant; wherever it exists, the ruling certainly exists. The condition may not be constant; it may exist while the conditioned does not exist with it, like life for knowledge. Second: The cause is existent (wujūdiyya) as previously mentioned; the condition may be non-existent (ʿadamiyya), like the absence of the opposite. This is the choice of al-Qāḍī, for he said: 'By our knowledge.'
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage introduces a new topic: the distinction between cause (ʿilla) and condition (sharṭ) according to the school of those who affirm states (aḥwāl). It lists nine distinctions, but only the first two are given here. The first distinction: the cause is constant (muṭṭarida)—wherever it is found, the ruling is found. A condition, however, may not be constant; it can exist without the conditioned, like life existing without knowledge. The second distinction: the cause is existent (wujūdiyya—a positive attribute), while a condition can be non-existent, such as the absence of the opposite (intifāʾ al-ḍidd). This is the view of al-Qāḍī (ʿAbd al-Jabbār), who exemplified it with 'by our knowledge', indicating that the condition for something may be the absence of a contrary.

Key Terms
علة
شرط
مثبتو الأحوال
مطردة
وجودية
عدمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that cause and condition are interchangeable terms or share the same logical properties.
Correction: This passage demonstrates that they differ in two key respects: constancy (cause is constant, condition may not be) and existential status (cause is existent, condition can be non-existent).

Checkpoint
According to the first distinction between cause and condition, what is true of the cause?
A) It may be non-existent
B) It is constant and entails the ruling
C) It is the same as the condition
D) It may not be present when the ruling is present

Key Takeaway
The first two distinctions between cause and condition are constancy (cause always entails its effect, condition may not) and existential status (cause is existent, condition can be non-existent).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you treat conditions as though they were causes, expecting that their presence guarantees a result? How does this lesson's first distinction (constancy) challenge you to re-evaluate your expectations? What will you do this week to test your assumptions about what truly causes outcomes in your life?
?
The second distinction says a condition can be non-existent—like the absence of an opposite. Is there an 'absence' in your heart (e.g., absence of envy, arrogance, or ignorance) that you have not considered as a condition for your faith or knowledge? Will you actively work to identify one such absence and strive to maintain it?
?
Al-Jurjani attributes the second point to al-Qāḍī. Whose authority do you rely on for understanding causality? Have you ever critically examined whether your own reasoning about causes and conditions aligns with what you learn from revelation and sound theology? What step will you take today to align your understanding with the truth, even if it unsettles your previous views?

Objection and Answer on Multiple Causes for a Single Judgment
Al-Jurjani presents an objection and answer on multiple causes for a single judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani defends the possibility of multiple causes contributing to a single judgment through the concept of composition.

Core Idea
A composite cause can produce a single judgment even if individual causes are ineffective alone.

In this passage, al-Jurjani addresses a critical objection to the causal theory of judgments: if two different attributes cannot produce the same judgment, how can a judgment be caused by multiple attributes? He responds by introducing the idea of composition—a composite of causes, each ineffective alone, together becomes the cause. This argument preserves the principle that a cause's effect is due to its essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic once, then the English translation. Note the structure: objection, then answer, then a further sub-argument about composition.
2
Identify the objection and the core of the answer
The objection is that two different knowledges (or attributes) cannot produce a single judgment. The answer states that if the two are united without conflict, they can be one cause.
3
Examine the composition argument
The passage explains that if each cause individually does not effect the judgment, but together they do, the composite is the cause. Consider why this does not violate the principle that cause acts by essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعلمنا، وهي حكم واحد واحد . قلنا : لا مخالفة بين العلمين إلا بعارض كالقدم والحدوث، والعلة هو العلم المتحد فيهما مع قطع النظر عن العوارض المختلفة. وإن سلم اختلاف العلمين في الحقيقة منع اتحاد العالميتين فيهما. (وأما على سبيل التركيب؛ فلان حقيقتهما حال الانفراد والاجتماع ،واحدة فإذا لم تؤثرا في الحكم (منفردتين)، كما هو المفروض (لم تؤثرا ) فيه (مجتمعتين)؛ وذلك لأن اقتضاء العلة للحكم إنما هو لذاتها لا باعتبار أمر خارج عنها، ولا شك أن اجتماعها مع غيرها لا يخرجها عن مقتضى ذاتها. وفيه منع ظاهر؛ لأن المقتضى حينئذ هو المجموع لا كل واحدة فلا يلزم خروج شيء منهما عن مقتضاه بحسب ذاته . ( ولأن الصفات المختلفة لها أحكام مختلفة ضرورة)، كما نبهنا عليه نقلاً عن الآمدي، وإذا علل حكم واحد بمجموع وصفين لم يكن هناك اختلاف في أحكامهما.
And with our knowledge, it is a single judgment. We said: There is no conflict between the two knowledges except through an accident like pre-eternity and origination, and the cause is the knowledge united in them without consideration of the different accidents. And if it is granted that the two knowledges differ in reality, then the unity of the two knowledges in them is prevented. (As for composition: because their reality in isolation and together is one, so if they do not effect the judgment (individually), as supposed, they do not effect it (together); because the causation of the judgment by the cause is only due to its essence, not due to anything external, and there is no doubt that its conjunction with another does not remove it from the requirement of its essence. And there is an apparent objection: because the cause then is the composite, not each one, so it does not necessitate the departure of either from its requirement according to its essence. (And because different attributes necessarily have different judgments), as we noted from al-Amidi, and if a single judgment is caused by a composite of two attributes, there is no difference in their judgments.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objection is that if two different causes cannot produce the same judgment, then how can a single judgment be caused by multiple causes? The answer uses the concept of composition: if the causes are individually ineffective but together form a composite cause, then the judgment follows from the composite, not from each independently. The text also notes that different attributes have different judgments, but if they compose one cause, the judgment is one. The argument relies on the principle that the cause's effect is due to its essence, and that conjunction with another does not change that essence.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
حكم
صفات مختلفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiple causes cannot produce a single judgment.
Correction: Multiple causes can produce a single judgment if they compose a single composite cause whose effect is one.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what prevents two knowledges from conflicting in causing a single judgment?
A) They are essentially one.
B) They are different attributes.
C) The judgment is composite.
D) The cause is external.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani shows that multiple attributes can cause a single judgment either by being essentially one or by forming a composite cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the attributes of Allah, do you sometimes struggle to see how His mercy and justice can both apply to a single situation? How does al-Jurjani's argument about composite cause help you trust that they can work together without conflict?
?
Have you ever dismissed a belief because its parts seemed contradictory, without considering that the composite may have its own reality? Reflect on a time you did this and how this lesson might change your approach.
?
If you believe that multiple factors can combine to produce a single outcome in Allah's creation, why do you sometimes deny that same principle when thinking about His attributes? What does that reveal about your understanding of tawhid?

First Distinction Between Cause and Condition: Constancy
Al-Jurjani enumerates the first of nine differences between cause and condition, focusing on constancy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a cause is constant (its effect necessarily follows), while a condition may not be constant, and how this distinction is fundamental in kalam.

Core Idea
A cause always produces its effect; a condition does not.

Al-Jurjani begins his treatment of the differences between cause and condition by highlighting a fundamental logical property: constancy. This first distinction is crucial for understanding the nature of causality in kalam, especially for those who affirm states (ahwal). Let's see how the author defines this difference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that this is the first of nine aspects.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the terms for cause, condition, constant, and conditioned effect. Understand how they relate to each other.
The term 'mutarida' (constant) is central.
3
Apply the distinction to the example
Consider the example of life and knowledge. Why is life a condition and not a cause for knowledge? This will solidify your understanding.
Life can exist without knowledge, so it is not constant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثامنة في الفرق بين العلة والشرط على رأي مثبتي الأحوال، وهو من وجوه تسعة: الأول: العلة مطردة، فحيثما وجدت وجد الحكم قطعاً، والشرط قد لا يطرد، فيوجد ولا يوجد معه المشروط، كالحياة للعلم.
The eighth issue on the difference between cause and condition according to the upholders of states. It consists of nine aspects: First: The cause is constant, so wherever it exists the judgment exists necessarily, and the condition may not be constant, so it may exist without the conditioned, like life for knowledge.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage defines the first distinction between cause and condition. A cause is 'constant' (mutarida): whenever it is present, its effect necessarily follows. In contrast, a condition is not necessarily constant; it can exist without the conditioned effect. The example given is life and knowledge: life is a condition for knowledge, but life can exist without knowledge, whereas if knowledge were a cause of something, that something would always follow knowledge.

Key Terms
علة
شرط
اطراد
حكم
مشروط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People might think that a condition always necessitates its effect, like a cause.
Correction: In fact, a condition may be present without the conditioned effect, whereas a cause always brings about its effect.

Checkpoint
What does the first distinction say about the cause?
A) It is not constant.
B) It is constant.
C) It can be privative.
D) It is composite.

Key Takeaway
The first distinction between cause and condition is constancy: a cause is always followed by its effect, while a condition may exist without its conditioned.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a situation in your life where you have been treating a mere condition as if it were a cause—something you assumed would automatically bring about a result, but it didn’t. What was that condition, and how does this lesson expose your misplaced reliance? What will you change starting today?
?
In your relationship with Allah, do you ever confuse the conditions for guidance (like seeking knowledge) with the cause (Allah’s will)? How does the distinction between cause and condition humble you and redirect your trust?
?
This lesson teaches that a condition can exist without its effect. What is one spiritual condition (e.g., prayer, fasting) you have performed without seeing its intended effect? Instead of abandoning it, how will you now approach it as a condition rather than a cause, and what does that mean for your hope in Allah’s mercy?

The Second Distinction: Cause Is Existential, Condition Can Be Privative
Al-Jurjani states the second difference: cause is existential, condition may be privative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second distinction between cause and condition: the cause must be an existing attribute, but a condition can be the absence of something.

Core Idea
Conditions can be privations; causes must be positive existences.

In this concise passage, al-Jurjani presents a key distinction in the nature of causes and conditions. While a cause must always be something that exists, a condition can be the absence of a counteracting factor. This distinction, attributed to al-Qadi, has important implications for understanding how necessary conditions work in theological causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the brevity: it states a distinction and gives an example.
2
Identify the two concepts
The passage mentions 'cause' (علة) and 'condition' (شرط). Understand what each is defined as: cause is existential (وجودية), condition may be privative (عدمياً).
Privative means being a non-existence or absence.
3
Analyze the example
The example given is 'the absence of the opposite' (انتفاء الضد). Reflect on why the absence of something can be a condition for an effect to occur.
For example, for a fire to burn, the absence of water (a counteracting factor) is a condition.
4
Consider the attribution to al-Qadi
The passage attributes this view to al-Qadi and quotes him: 'with our knowledge.' Why might this distinction matter in a theological context?
Al-Qadi likely refers to the Mu'tazilite scholar al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: العلة وجودية كما مر، والشرط قد يكون عدمياً، كانتفاء الضد، وهو مختار القاضي، فإنه قال: بعلمنا.
Second: The cause is existential as has passed, and the condition may be privative, like the absence of the opposite. This is the choice of al-Qadi, for he said: 'with our knowledge.'
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage gives the second distinction: the cause must be an existent attribute (wujudiyya), whereas a condition can be a privation (adamiyya), such as the absence of a counteracting factor. The example of 'the absence of the opposite' is given, and this view is attributed to al-Qadi (likely al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar). The phrase 'with our knowledge' is a citation, possibly from al-Qadi.

Key Terms
علة
شرط
وجودية
عدم
ضد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that conditions, like causes, must always be something that exists (positive).
Correction: The passage shows that conditions can be privative, i.e., the absence of something, such as the absence of an obstacle. This is a key difference between cause and condition.

Checkpoint
According to the second distinction, what can a condition be?
A) Only existential.
B) Only privative.
C) Either existential or privative.
D) Neither.

Key Takeaway
A cause must exist, but a condition can be the absence of an obstacle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own spiritual state, do you treat the absence of sins as merely 'not doing wrong,' or do you see it as a real condition that must be intentionally maintained? How does the example of 'absence of the opposite' challenge any complacency you have about avoiding sin?
?
This lesson distinguishes cause (positive action) from condition (which can be a lack). In your relationship with Allah, are you relying on positive causes like prayer and charity while neglecting to remove the conditions of pride and distraction? Which 'absence' is most urgent for you to establish today?
?
Al-Qadi emphasized 'with our knowledge'—linking conditions to what we know. Is there a condition in your faith that you know is missing but have not acted to remove? What would change if you treated that missing condition as seriously as a cause?
